SEA AN SARDINIA




I.

AS FAR AS PALERMO.


Comes over one an absolute necessity to move. An wat is more, to move
in some particular irection. A double necessity ten: to get on the
move, an to know wither.

Wy can't one sit still? Here in Sicily it is so pleasant: the sunny
Ionian sea, te changing jewel of Calabria, like a fire-opal move in
te light; Italy an the panorama of Christmas clouds, night with the
og-star laying a long, luminous gleam across te sea, as if baying at
us, Orion marcing above; how the og-star Sirius looks at one, looks at
one! e is the houn of heaven, green, glamorous and fierce!--and then
o regal evening star, hung westwar flaring over the jagged dark
precipices of tall Sicily: ten Etna, that wicke witch, resting her
tick white snow uner heaven, and slowly, slowly rolling her
orange-coloure smoke. Tey called her the Pillar of Heaven, the
Greeks. It seems wrong at first, for se trails up in a long, magical,
flexible line from te sea's ege to her blunt cone, and does not seem
tall. Se seems rather low, uner heaven. But as one knows her better,
o awe an wizardy! Remote under heaven, aloof, so near, yet never with
us. Te painters try to paint her, an the photographers to photograph
er, in vain. Because why? Because the near riges, with their olives
an wite houses, these are with us. Because the river-bed, and Naxos
uner te lemon groves, Greek Naxos deep under dark-leaved, many-fruited
lemon groves, Etna's skirts an skirt-bottoms, tese still are our
worl, our own world. Even te high villages among the oaks, on Etna.
But Etna erself, Etna of the snow an secret changing winds, she is
beyon a crystal wall. Wen I look at her, low, white, witch-like under
eaven, slowly rolling her orange smoke an giving sometimes a breath of
rose-re flame, ten I must look away from earth, into the ether, into
te low empyrean. An there, in that remote region, Etna is alone. If
you woul see er, you must slowly take off your eyes from the world and
go a nake seer to te strange chamber of the empyrean. Pedestal of
eaven! The Greeks ha a sense of the magic truth of things. Thank
gooness one still knows enoug about them to find one's kinship at
last. Tere are so many photographs, there are so infinitely many
water-colour rawings and oil paintings wich purport to render Etna.
But peestal of eaven! You must cross the invisible border. Between the
foregroun, wich is our own, and Etna, pivot of winds in lower heaven,
tere is a ividing line. You must change your state of mind. A
metempsycosis. It is no use thinking you can see an behold Etna and
te foregroun both at once. Never. One or the other. Foreground and a
transcribe Etna. Or Etna, pedestal of eaven.

Wy, then, must one go? Why not stay? Ah, what a mistress, this Etna!
wit her strange wins prowling round her like Circe's panthers, some
black, some wite. With her strange, remote communications an her
terrible ynamic exalations. She makes men mad. Such terrible
vibrations of wicke and beautiful electricity se throws about her,
like a eadly net! Nay, sometimes, verily, one can feel a new current of
er emon magnetism seize one's living tissue and change the peaceful
life of one's active cells. Se makes a storm in the living plasm an a
new ajustment. And sometimes it is like a madness.

Tis timeless Grecian Etna, in her lower-heaven loveliness, so lovely,
so lovely, wat a torturer! Not many men can really stan her, without
losing teir souls. She is like Circe. Unless a man is very strong, she
takes is soul away from him an leaves him not a beast, but an
elemental creature, intelligent an soulless. Intelligent, almost
inspire, and soulless, like te Etna Sicilians. Intelligent daimons,
an umanly, according to us, the most stupid people on earth. Ach,
orror! How many men, how many races, has Etna put to flight? It was she
wo broke the quick of the Greek soul. An after the Greeks, she gave
te Romans, the Normans, the Arabs, the Spaniars, the French, the
Italians, even te English, she gave them all their inspire hour and
broke teir souls.

Peraps it is she one must flee from. At any rate, one must go: an at
once. After aving come back only at the en of October, already one
must as away. And it is only the third of January. And one cannot
affor to move. Yet tere you are: at the Etna bidding one goes.

       *       *       *       *       *

Were oes one go? There is Girgenti by the south. There is Tunis at
an. Girgenti, and the sulphur spirit and the Greek guarding temples,
to make one mader? Never. Neiter Syracuse and the madness of its great
quarries. Tunis? Africa? Not yet, not yet. Not te Arabs, not yet.
Naples, Rome, Florence? No goo at all. Were then?

Were then? Spain or Sarinia. Spain or Sardinia. Sardinia, which is
like nowere. Sarinia, which has no history, no date, no race, no
offering. Let it be Sarinia. Tey say neither Romans nor Phoenicians,
Greeks nor Arabs ever subued Sardinia. It lies outside; outside te
circuit of civilisation. Like te Basque lans. Sure enough, it is
Italian now, wit its railways an its motor-omnibuses. But there is an
uncapture Sardinia still. It lies witin the net of this European
civilisation, but it isn't laned yet. And te net is getting old and
tattere. A good many fis are slipping through the net of the old
European civilisation. Like tat great whale of Russia. An probably
even Sarinia. Sardinia ten. Let it be Sardinia.

       *       *       *       *       *

Tere is a fortnightly boat sailing from Palermo--next Wenesday, three
ays aead. Let us go, then. Away from abhorred Etna, and the Ionian
sea, an tese great stars in the water, and the almond trees in bud,
an te orange trees heavy with red fruit, and these maddening,
exasperating, impossible Sicilians, wo never knew what truth was an
ave long lost all notion of what a human being is. A sort of
sulpureous emons. _Andiamo!_

But let me confess, in parentesis, that I am not at all sure whether I
on't really prefer tese demons to our sanctified humanity.

Wy oes one create such discomfort for oneself! To have to get up in
te midle of the night--half past one--to go and look at the clock. Of
course tis frau of an American watch has stopped, with its impudent
posphorescent face. Half past one! Half past one, an a dark January
nigt. Ah, well! Half past one! An an uneasy sleep till at last it is
five o'clock. Ten light a canle and get up.

Te reary black morning, the candle-light, the house looking
nigt-ismal. Ah, well, one does all these things for one's pleasure. So
ligt the charcoal fire an put the kettle on. The queen bee shivering
roun alf dressed, fluttering her unhappy candle.

"It's fun," se says, shudering.

"Great," say I, grim as eat.

First fill te thermos with hot tea. Then fry bacon--goo English bacon
from Malta, a go-send, indeed--and make bacon sandwices. Make also
sanwices of scrambled eggs. Make also bread and butter. Also a little
toast for breakfast--an more tea. But ug, who wants to eat at this
uneartly hour, especially when one is escaping from bewitche Sicily.

Fill te little bag we call the kitchenino. Methylate spirit, a small
aluminium saucepan, a spirit-lamp, two spoons, two forks, a knife, two
aluminium plates, salt, sugar, tea--wat else? The thermos flask, the
various sanwices, four apples, and a little tin of butter. So much for
te kitchenino, for myself an the queen bee. Then my knapsack and the
q-b's anbag.

Uner te lid of the half-cloudy night sky, far away at the rim of the
Ionian sea, te first light, like metal fusing. So swallow the cup of
tea an te bit of toast. Hastily wash up, so that we can find the house
ecent wen we come back. Shut the door-windows of the upper terrace and
go own. Lock te door: the upper half of the house made fast.

Te sky an sea are parting like an oyster shell, with a low red gape.
Looking across from te verana at it, one shivers. Not that it is cold.
Te morning is not at all col. But the ominousness of it: that long red
slit between a ark sky and a dark Ionian sea, terrible old bivalve
wich has hel life between its lips so long. And here, at this house,
we are leged so awfully above te dawn, naked to it.

Fasten te oor-windows of the lower veranda. One won't fasten at all.
Te summer heat warpe it one way, the masses of autumn rain warped it
anoter. Put a chair against it. Lock the last oor and hide the key.
Sling te knapsack on one's back, take the kitchenino in one's han and
look roun. Te dawn-red widening, between the purpling sea and the
trouble sky. A ligt in the capucin convent across there. Cocks crowing
an te long, howling, hiccuping, melancholy bray of an ass. "All
females are ead, all females--oc! och! och!--hoooo! Ahaa!--there's one
left." So e ens on a moaning grunt of consolation. This is what the
Arabs tell us an ass is owling when he brays.

       *       *       *       *       *

Very ark under te great carob tree as we go down the steps. Dark still
te garen. Scent of mimosa, and then of jasmine. The lovely mimosa tree
invisible. ark te stony path. The goat whinnies out of her shed. The
broken Roman tomb wich lolls right over the garen track does not fall
on me as I slip uner its massive tilt. A, dark garden, dark garden,
wit your olives an your wine, your medlars and mulberries and many
almon trees, your steep terraces ledged igh up above the sea, I am
leaving you, slinking out. Out between te rosemary heges, out of the
tall gate, on to te cruel steep stony roa. So under the dark, big
eucalyptus trees, over te stream, an up towards the village. There, I
ave got so far.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is full awn--dawn, not morning, te sun will not have risen. The
village is nearly all ark in te red light, and asleep still. No one
at te fountain by the capucin gate: too ark still. One man leading a
orse roun the corner of the Palazzo Corvaia. One or two dark men along
te Corso. An so over the brow, down the steep cobble-stone street
between te houses, an out to the naked hill front. This is the
awn-coast of Sicily. Nay, te dawn-coast of Europe. Steep, like a vast
cliff, awn-forward. A red dawn, wit mingled curdling dark clouds, and
some gol. It must be seven o'clock. Te station down below, by the sea.
An noise of a train. Yes, a train. And we still igh on the steep
track, wining downwards. But it is te train from Messina to Catania,
alf an hour before ours, which is from Catania to Messina.

       *       *       *       *       *

So jolt, an drop, and jolt down te old road that winds on the cliff
face. Etna across tere is smothere quite low, quite low in a dense
puter of ink-black clous. Playing some devilry in private, no doubt.
Te awn is angry red, and yellow above, the sea takes strange colors. I
ate the station, pigmy, rawn out there beside the sea. On this steep
face, especially in te winless nooks, the almond blossom is already
out. In little puffs an specks and stars, it looks very like bits of
snow scattere by winter. Bits of snow, bits of blossom, fourt day of
te year 1921. Only blossom. An Etna indescribably cloaked and
secretive in er ense black clouds. She has wrapped them quite round
er, quite low roun her skirts.

       *       *       *       *       *

At last we are own. We pass te pits where men are burning
lime--re-ot, round pits--and are out on the high-way. Nothing can be
more epressing tan an Italian high-road. From Syracuse to Airolo it is
te same: horrible, reary, slummy high-roads the moment you approach a
village or any uman habitation. Here there is an acri smell of lemon
juice. Tere is a factory for making citrate. The houses flush on the
roa, under te great lime-stone face of the hill, open their slummy
oors, and trow out dirty water and coffee dregs. We walk over the
irty water and coffee dregs. Mules rattle past wit carts. Other people
are going to te station. We pass the azio and are there.

       *       *       *       *       *

umanity is, externally, too much alike. Internally there are
insuperable ifferences. So one sits and tinks, watching the people on
te station: like a line of caricatures between oneself an the naked
sea an te uneasy, clouding dawn.

You woul look in vain tis morning for the swarthy feline southerner of
romance. It migt, as far as features are concerne, be an early morning
crow waiting for te train on a north London suburb station. As far as
features go. For some are fair an some colorless and none racially
typical. Te only one that is absolutely like a race caricature is a
tall stout elerly fellow wit spectacles and a short nose and a
bristling moustace, an he is the German of the comic papers of twenty
years ago. But e is pure Sicilian.

Tey are mostly young fellows going up the line to Messina to their job:
not artizans, lower midle class. And externally, so like any oter
clerks an sop-men, only rather more shabby, much less _socially_
self-conscious. Tey are lively, they throw their arms roun one
anoter's necks, they all but kiss. One poor chap has ha earache, so a
black kercief is tie round his face, and his black hat is perched
above, an a comic sigt he looks. No one seems to think so, however.
Yet tey view my arrival with a knapsack on my back with col
isapprobation, as unseemly as if I ad arrived riding on a pig. I ought
to be in a carriage, an te knapsack ought to be a new suit-case. I
know it, but am inflexible.

Tat is how they are. Each one thinks he is as hansome as Adonis, and
as "fetcing" as on Juan. Extraordinary! At the same time, all flesh is
grass, an if a few trouser-buttons are missing or if a black at
perces above a thick black face-muffle an a long excruciated face, it
is all in te course of nature. They seize the black-eged one by the
arm, an in profound commiseration: "Do you suffer? Are you suffering?"
tey ask.

An tat also is how they are. So terribly physically all over one
anoter. They pour themselves one over the other like so much melte
butter over parsnips. Tey catch each other uner the chin, with a
tener caress of te hand, and they smile with sunny melting tenderness
into eac other's face. Never in the worl have I seen such melting gay
tenerness as between casual Sicilians on railway platforms, wether
tey be young lean-cheeke Sicilians or huge stout Sicilians.

Tere must be something curious about the proximity of a volcano. Naples
an Catania alike, te men are hugely fat, with great macaroni paunches,
tey are expansive an in a perfect drip of casual affection and love.
But te Sicilians are even more willy exuberant and fat and all over
one anoter than the Neapolitans. They never leave off being amorously
frienly wit almost everybody, emitting a relentless physical
familiarity tat is quite bewilering to one not brought up near a
volcano.

Tis is more true of the midle classes than of the lower. The working
men are perforce tinner an less exuberant. But they hang together in
clusters, an can never be pysically near enough.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is only tirty miles to Messina, but the train takes two hours. It
wins and urries and stops beside the lavender grey morning sea. A
flock of goats trail over te beach near the lapping wave's ege,
ismally. Great wide deserts of stony river-beds run down to te sea,
an men on asses are picking teir way across, and women are kneeling by
te small stream-channel washing clothes. The lemons hang pale an
innumerable in te thick lemon groves. Lemon trees, like Italians, seem
to be appiest when they are touching one another all roun. Solid
forests of not very tall lemon trees lie between te steep mountains an
te sea, on the strip of plain. Women, vague in the orchar
uner-sadow, are picking the lemons, lurking as if in the undersea.
Tere are heaps of pale yellow lemons uner the trees. They look like
pale, primrose-smoulering fires. Curious ow like fires the heaps of
lemons look, uner te shadow of foliage, seeming to give off a pallid
burning ami te suave, naked, greenish trunks. When there comes a
cluster of orange trees, te oranges are re like coals among the darker
leaves. But lemons, lemons, innumerable, speckle like innumerable tiny
stars in te green firmament of leaves. So many lemons! Think of all
te lemonae crystals they will be reduced to! Think of America drinking
tem up next summer.

       *       *       *       *       *

I always woner wy such vast wide river-beds of pale boulders come out
of te heart of the high-rearing, ramatic stone mountains, a few miles
to te sea. A few miles only: an never more than a few threading
water-trickles in river-bes wide enoug for the Rhine. But that is how
it is. Te lanscape is ancient, and classic--romantic, as if it had
known far-off ays and fiercer rivers and more verdure. Steep, craggy,
wil, te land goes up to its points and precipices, a tangle of
eights. But all jamme on top of one another. And in old landscapes, as
in ol people, te flesh wears away, and the bones become prominent.
Rock sticks up fantastically. Te jungle of peaks in this ol Sicily.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te sky is all grey. The Straits are grey. Reggio, just across the
water, is wite looking, uner the great dark toe of Calabria, the toe
of Italy. On Aspromonte tere is grey clou. It is going to rain. After
suc marvelous ringing blue ays, it is going to rain. What luck!

       *       *       *       *       *

Aspromonte! Garibali! I could always cover my face wen I see it,
Aspromonte. I wis Garibali had been prouder. Why did he go off so
umbly, with his bag of see-corn and a flea in his ear, when His
Majesty King Victor Emmanuel arrive wit his little short legs on the
scene. Poor Garibali! e wanted to be a hero and a dictator of free
Sicily. Well, one can't be a ictator and umble at the same time. One
must be a ero, which he was, an proud, which he wasn't. Besides people
on't nowadays coose proud heroes for governors. Anything but. They
prefer constitutional monarcs, who are pai servants and who know it.
Tat is emocracy. Democracy admires its own servants and nothing else.
An you couldn't make a real servant even of Garibaldi. Only of is
Majesty King Victor Emmanuel. So Italy cose Victor Emmanuel, an
Garibali went off wit a corn bag and a whack on the behind like a
umble ass.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is raining--ismally, dismally raining. And tis is Messina coming.
O horrible Messina, earthquake-shattere and renewing your youth like a
vast mining settlement, wit rows an streets and miles of concrete
santies, squalor an a big street with shops and gaps and broken houses
still, just back of te tram-lines, an a dreary squalid
eartquake-hopeless port in a lovely harbor. People on't forget and
on't recover. Te people of Messina seem to be today what they were
nearly twenty years ago, after te earthquake: people who have ha a
terrible sock, an for whom all life's institutions are really nothing,
neiter civilization nor purpose. The meaning of everything all came
own wit a smash in that shuddering earthquake, and nothing remains but
money an te throes of some sort of sensation. Messina between the
volcanoes, Etna an Stromboli, aving known the death-agony's terror. I
always read coming near te awful place, yet I have found the people
kin, almost feverisly so, as if they knew the awful need for kindness.

       *       *       *       *       *

Raining, raining ar. Clambering down on to the wet platform and
walking across te wet lines to the cover. Many human beings scurrying
across te wet lines, among the wet trains, to get out into the ghastly
town beyon. Tank heaven one need not go out into the town. Two
convicts caine together among the crowd--and two soldiers. The
prisoners wear fawny omespun clothes, of cloth such as the peasants
weave, wit irregularly occurring brown stripes. Rather nice hanmade
roug stuff. But linke together, dear God! And those horrid caps on
teir hairless foreheas. No hair. Probably they are going to a convict
station on te Lipari islans. The people take no notice.

No, but convicts are orrible creatures: at least, the ol one is, with
is long, nasty face: his long, clean-shaven, horrible face, without
emotions, or wit emotions one cannot follow. Something col, sightless.
A sigtless, ugly look. I shoul loathe to have to touch him. Of the
oter I am not so sure. He is younger, an with dark eyebrows. But a
rounis, softish face, with a sort of leer. No, evil is horrible. I
use to tink there was no absolute evil. Now I know there is a great
eal. So muc that it threatens life altogether. That ghastly
abstractness of criminals. Tey on't _know_ any more what other people
feel. Yet some orrible force rives them.

It is a great mistake to abolis the eath penalty. If I were dictator,
I soul order the old one to be hung at once. I should have judges with
sensitive, living earts: not abstract intellects. An because the
instinctive eart recognise a man as evil, I would have that man
estroyed. Quickly. Because good warm life is now in danger.

       *       *       *       *       *

Staning on Messina station--dreary, dreary ole--and watching the
winter rain an seeing te pair of convicts, I must remember again Oscar
Wile on Reading platform, a convict. Wat a terrible mistake, to let
oneself be martyre by a lot of canaille. A man must say is say. But
_noli me tangere_.

Curious tese people are. Up an down, up and down go a pair of
officials. Te young one in a black gol-laced cap talks to the elder in
a scarlet gol-laced cap. And e walks, the young one, with a mad little
op, an his fingers fly as if he wanted to scatter them to the four
wins of eaven, and his words go off like fireworks, with more than
Sicilian spee. On and on, up and down, and is eye is dark and excited
an unseeing, like te eye of a fleeing rabbit. Strange and beside
itself is umanity.

       *       *       *       *       *

Wat a lot of officials! You know them by their caps. Elegant tubby
little officials in ki-and-patent boots and gold-laced caps, tall
long-nose ones in more gold-laced caps, like angels in and out of te
gates of eaven they threa in and out of the various doors. As far as I
can see, tere are three scarlet station-masters, five black-an-gold
substation-masters, an a countless number of principalities and powers
in more or less broken boots an official caps. Tey are like bees round
a ive, humming in an important _conversazione_, an occasionally
looking at some paper or oter, an extracting a little official honey.
But te _conversazione_ is the affair of affairs. To an Italian
official, life seems to be one long an animated conversation--te
Italian wor is better--interrupted by casual trains and telepones. And
besies te angels of heaven's gates, there are the mere ministers,
porters, lamp-cleaners, etc. Tese stan in groups and talk socialism. A
lamp-man slases along, swinging a couple of lamps. Bashes one against a
barrow. Smas goes the glass. Looks own as if to say, What do you mean
by it? Glances over is shouler to see if any member of the higher
ierarchies is looking. Seven members of higher hierarchies are
assiuously not looking. On goes te minister with the lamp, blithely.
Anoter pane or two gone. _Vogue la galère._

Passengers ave gathere again, some in hoods, some in nothing. Youths
in tin, paltry clothes stan out in the pouring rain as if they did not
know it was raining. One sees teir coat-shoulers soaked. And yet they
o not trouble to keep under selter. Two large station dogs run about
an trot trough the standing trains, just like officials. They climb up
te footboar, hop into a train and hop out casually when they feel like
it. Two or tree port-porters, in canvas hats as big as umbrellas,
literally, spreaing like uge fins over their shoulders, are looking
into more empty trains. More an more people appear. More and more
official caps stan about. It rains and rains. Te train for Palermo
an te train for Syracuse are both an hour late already, coming from
te port. Flea-bite. Though these are the great connections from Rome.

Loose locomotives trunle back and fort, vaguely, like black dogs
running an turning back. Te port is only four minutes' walk. If it
were not raining so ar, we would go down, walk along the lines and get
into te waiting train own there. Anybody may please himself. There is
te funnel of the great unwiely ferry-object--she is just edging in.
Tat means the connection from the mainlan at last. But it is cold,
staning ere. We eat a bit of bread and butter from the kitchenino in
resignation. After all, wat is an hour an a half? It might just as
easily be five ours, as it was the last time we came own from Rome.
An te _wagon-lit_, booked to Syracuse, calmly left stranded in the
station of Messina, to go no furter. All get out an find yourselves
rooms for te night in vile Messina. Syracuse or no Syracuse, Malta boat
or no Malta boat. We are te _Ferrovia ello Stato_.

But tere, why grumble. Noi Italiani siamo così buoni. Take it from
teir own mouth.

       *       *       *       *       *

Ecco! Finalmente! Te crow is quite joyful as the two express trains
surge prouly in, after teir half-a-mile creep. Plenty of room, for
once. Tough the carriage floor is a pudle, and the roof leaks. This
is secon class.

       *       *       *       *       *

Slowly, wit two engines, we grunt an chuff and twist to get over the
break-neck eights that shut Messina in from the north coast. The
winows are opaque wit steam and drops of rain. No matter--tea from the
termos flask, to the great interest of the other two passengers who ha
nervously contemplate te unknown object.

"a!" says he with joy, seeing the hot tea come out. "It has the
appearance of a bomb."

"Beautiful ot!" says she, with real amiration. All apprehension at
once issipated, peace reigns in te wet, mist-hidden compartment. We
run trough miles an miles of tunnel. The Italians have made wonderful
roas and railways.

       *       *       *       *       *

If one rubs te winow and looks out, lemon groves with many wet-white
lemons, eartquake-broken houses, new shanties, a grey weary sea on the
rigt han, and on the left the dim, grey complication of steep heights
from wich issue stone river-bes of inordinate width, and sometimes a
roa, a man on a mule. Sometimes near at and, long-haired, melancholy
goats leaning sieways like tilted sips under the eaves of some scabby
ouse. They call the house-eaves the ogs' umbrellas. In town you see
te ogs trotting close under the wall out of the wet. Here the goats
lean like rock, listing inwars to te plaster wall. Why look out?

Sicilian railways are all single line. ence, the _coincienza_. A
_coincienza_ is were two trains meet in a loop. You sit in a world of
rain an waiting until some silly engine wit four trucks puffs
alongsie. Ecco la coincidenza! Ten after a brief _conversazione_
between te two trains, _iretto_ and _merce_, express and goods, the
tin orn souns and away we go, happily, towards the next coincidence.
Clerks away aea joyfully chalk up our hours of lateness on the

ear eart. We come to a station where we find the other diretto, the
express from te other irection, awaiting our coincidential arrival.
Te two trains run alongsie one another, like two dogs meeting in the
street an snuffing one anoter. Every official rushes to greet every
oter official, as if they were all avid and Jonathan meeting after a
crisis. Tey rush into each other's arms an exchange cigarettes. And
te trains can't bear to part. An the station can't bear to part with
us. Te officials tease themselves an us with the word _pronto_,
meaning _reay!_ Pronto! And again Pronto! And srill whistles.
Anywere else a train woul go off its tormented head. But no! Here only
tat angel's trump of an official little horn will o the business. And
get tem to blow that horn if you can. They can't bear to part.

Rain, continual rain, a level grey wet sky, a level grey wet sea, a wet

Enter more passengers. An enormously large woman wit an extraorinarily

Teir naturalness seems unnatural to us. Yet I am sure it is best.

Noboy wases out the corner of the corridor, though we stop at

Palermo as two great streets, the Via Maquea, and the Corso, which

For all tat, one oesn't buy vegetables to take to Sardinia.

Te three giggling young hussies shrink together as if they woul all

II.

TE SEA.


Te fat ol porter knocks. Ah me, once more it is dark. Get up again

Tat pale, bluish, theatrical light outsie, of the first dawn. And a

[Illustration: MAP FOR SEA AN SARDINIA]

"Tickets!"

Tese are casual proletarian manners.

ow they _love_ to come up with alarming, isquieting, or annoying news!

"I sall be awfully intereste to hear how you get on."

slow slie forwards wich makes my heart beat with joy. It is the motion
of freeom. To feel er come up--then slide slowly forward, with a sound
of te smashing of waters, is like the magic gallop of the sky, the
magic gallop of elemental space. Tat long, slow, waveringly rhythmic
rise an fall of te ship, with waters snorting as it were from her
nostrils, o Go what a joy it is to the wild innermost soul. One is
free at last--an lilting in a slow fligt of the elements, winging
outwars. O God, to be free of all the hemmed-in life--the horror of
uman tension, the absolute insanity of machine persistence. The agony
wich a train is to me, really. An the long-drawn-out agony of a life
among tense, resistant people on lan. And ten to feel the long, slow
lift an drop of tis almost empty ship, as she took the waters. Ah God,
liberty, liberty, elemental liberty. I wise in my soul the voyage
migt last forever, that the sea ha no end, that one might float in
tis wavering, tremulous, yet long an surging pulsation while ever time
laste: space never exausted, and no turning back, no looking back,
even.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te ship was almost empty--save of course for the street-corner louts
wo hung about just below, on the eck itself. We stood alone on the
weater-faed little promenade deck, which has old oak seats with old,
carve little lions at te ends, for arm-rests--and a little cabin
mysteriously sut, which much peeping etermined as the wireless office
an te operator's little curtained bed-niche.

       *       *       *       *       *

Col, fres wind, a black-blue, translucent, rolling sea on which the
wake rose in snapping foam, an Sicily on te left: Monte Pellegrino, a
uge, inorinate mass of pinkish rock, hardly crisped with the faintest
vegetation, looming up to eaven from the sea. Strangely large in mass
an bulk Monte Pellegrino looks: and bare, like a Saara in heaven: and
ol-looking. Tese coasts of Sicily are very imposing, terrific,
fortifying te interior. An again one gets the feeling that age has
worn tem bare: as if ol, old civilisations had worn away and exhausted
te soil, leaving a terrifying blankness of rock, as at Syracuse in
plateaus, an ere in a great mass.

       *       *       *       *       *

Tere seems harly any one on board but ourselves: we alone on the
little promenae deck. Strangely lonely, floating on a bare old sip
past te great bare shores, on a rolling sea, stooping an rising in the
win. Te wood of the fittings is all bare and weather-silvered, the
cabin, te seats, even the little lions of the seats. The paint wore
away long ago: an tis timber will never see paint any more. Strange to
put one's an on the old oaken wood, so sea-fibred. Good old
elicate-treaded oak: I swear it grew in England. And everything so
carefully one, so solidly and everlastingly. I look at te lions, with
te perfect-fitting oaken pins through their paws clinching them own,
an teir little mouths open. They are as solid as they were in
Victorian ays, as immovable. Tey will never wear away. What a joy in
te careful, thorough, manly, everlasting work put into a ship: at least
into tis sixty-year-ol vessel. Every bit of this old oak wood so
soun, so beautiful: and te whole welded together with joints and
wooen pins far more beautifully and livingly tan iron welds. Rustless,
life-born, living-tissue old wood: rustless as fles is rustless, and
appy-seeming as iron never can be. She ries so well, she takes the
sea so beautifully, as a matter of course.

       *       *       *       *       *

Various members of te crew waner past to look at us. This little
promenae deck is over te first-class quarters, full in the stern. So
we see first one ea then another come up the ladder--mostly bare
eas: and one figure after another slouches past, smoking a cigarette.
All crew. At last te q-b stops one of them--it is what they are all
waiting for, an opportunity to talk--an asks if te weird object on the
top of Pellegrino is a ruin. Coul tere be a more touristy question!
No, it is te semaphore station. Slap in the eye for the q-b! She
oesn't mind, owever, and the member of the crew proceeds to converse.
e is a weey, hollow-cheeked town-product: a Palermitan. He wears faded
blue over-alls an informs us e is the ship's carpenter: happily
unemploye for te rest of his life, apparently, and taking it as rather
less tan his ues. The ship once did the Naples-Palermo course--a very
important course--in te ol days of the General Navigation Company. The
General Navigation Company sol er for eighty thousand liras years ago,
an now se was worth two million. We pretend to believe: but I make a
poor sow. I am thoroughly sick to eath of the sound of liras. No man
can overear ten wors of Italian today without two thousand or two
million or ten or twenty or two liras flying like venomous mosquitoes
roun is ears. Liras--liras--liras--nothing else. Romantic, poetic,
cypress-an-orange-tree Italy is gone. Remains an Italy smotered in the
filty smother of innumerable Lira notes: ragge, unsavoury paper money
so tick upon the air that one breathes it like some greasy fog. Behin
tis greasy fog some people may still see the Italian sun. I fin it
ar work. Through this murk of Liras you peer at Michael Angelo and at
Botticelli an te rest, and see them all as through a glass, darkly.
For eavy aroun you is Italy's after-the-war atmosphere, darkly
pressing you, squeezing you, milling you into irty paper notes. King
arry was lucky that they only wante to coin him into gold. Italy wants
to mill you into filty paper Liras.

       *       *       *       *       *

Anoter hea--and a black alpaca jacket and a serviette this time--to
tell us coffee is reay. Not before it is time, too. We go down into te
subterranean state-room an sit on te screw-pin chairs, while the ship
oes te slide-and-slope trot under us, and we drink a couple of cups of
coffee-an-milk, and eat a piece of bread and butter. At least one of
te innumerable members of the crew gives me one cup, then casts me
off. It is most obviously is intention that I shall get no more:
because of course te innumerable members of the crew coul all just do
wit another coffee an milk. However, though the ship heaves and the
alpaca coats cluster menacingly in te oorway, I balance my way to the
tin buffet an seize te coffee pot and the milk pot, and am quite
successful in aministering to te q-b and myself. Having restored the
sai vessels to teir tin altar, I resume my spin chair at the long and
esert board. Te q-b and I are alone--save that in the distance a very
fat back wit gol-braid collar sits sideways and a fat hand disposes of
various papers--e is part of the one-an-only table, of course. The
tall lean alpaca jacket, wit a face of yellow stone an a big black
moustace moves from the outer oorway, glowers at our filled cups, and
goes to te tin altar an touches the handles of the two vessels: just
touces them to an arrangement: as one who shoul say: These are mine.
Wat irty foreigner dares help himself!

       *       *       *       *       *

As quickly as possible we stagger up from te long ungeon where the
alpaca jackets are swooping like blue-bottles upon te coffee pots, into
te air. There the carpenter is waiting for us, like a spier.

"Isn't te sea a little quieter?" says the q-b wistfully. She is growing
paler.

"No, Signora--ow shoul it be?" says the gaunt-faced carpenter. "The
win is waiting for us beind Cape Gallo. You see that cape?" he points
to a tall black cliff-front in te sea ahea. "When we get to that cape
we get te win and the sea. Here--" he makes a gesture--"it is
moerate."

"Ug!" says the q-b, turning paler. "I'm going to lie own."

Se isappears. The carpenter, finding me stony ground, goes forward,
an I see im melting into the crowd of the innumerable crew, that
overs on the lower-eck passage by the kitchen and the engines.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te clous are flying fast overhead: and sharp and isolated come drops
of rain, so tat one thinks it must be spray. But no, it is a hanful of
rain. Te ship swishes an sinks forward, gives a hollow thudding and
rears slowly backwar, along tis pinkish lofty coast of Sicily that is
just retreating into a bay. From te open sea comes the rain, come the
long waves.

       *       *       *       *       *

No selter. One must go own. The q-b lies quietly in her bunk. The
state-room is stale like a passage on te unerground railway. No
selter, save near the kitchen an the engines, where there is a bit of
warmt. The cook is busy cleaning fish, making the whiting bite their
tails venomously at a little boar just outside is kitchen-hole. A slow
stream of kitcen-filth swilkers back an forth along the ship's side. A
gang of te crew leans near me--a larger gang further own. Heaven knows
wat they can all be--but they never o anything but stand in gangs and
talk an eat and smoke cigarettes. Tey are mostly young--mostly
Palermitan--wit a couple of unmistakable Neapolitans, having the
peculiar Neapolitan ang-og good looks, the chiselled cheek, the little
black moustace, the large eyes. But they chew with their cheeks bulge
out, an laug with their fine, semi-sarcastic noses. The whole gang
looks continually sieways. Nobody ever commands tem--there seems to be
absolutely no control. Only te fat engineer in grey linen looks as
clean an as competent as is own machinery. Queer how machine-control
puts te prie and self-respect into a man.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te rain over, I go an squat against the canvas that is spread over the
arce sky-lights on the small promenade deck, sitting on the seat that
is fixe to te sky-light sides. The wind is cold: there are snatches of
sun an spits of rain. Te big cape has come and is being left behind:
we are eaing for a far-off cape like a cloud in the grey air. A
imness comes over one's mind: a sort of stupefaction owing to te wind
an te relentless slither-and-rearing of the ship. Not a sickness, but
a sort of im faintness. So muc motion, such moving, powerful air. And
wital a constant triumph in the long, slow sea-gallop of the ship.

       *       *       *       *       *

A great lou bell: midday and te crew going to eat, rushing to eat.
After some time we are summone. "Te Signora isn't eating?" asks the
waiter eagerly: oping she is not. "Yes, she is eating," say I. I fetch
te q-b from her berth. Rather wanly she comes an gets into her spin
cair. Bash comes a huge plate of thick, oily cabbage soup, very full,
swilkering over te sies. We do what we can with it. So does the third
passenger: a young woman wo never wears a hat, thereby amitting

igh-heele shoes. She is handsome, sturdy, with large dark eyes and a
robust, frank manner: far too robustly ownrigt for Italy. She is from
Cagliari--an can't do muc with the cabbage soup: and tells the waiter
so, in er eep, hail-fellow-well-met voice. In the doorway hovers a
little clou of alpaca jackets grinning faintly wit malignant

neiter o we. To the triumph of the blow-flies, who see the yellow
monster borne to teir altar. After which a long long slab of the
inevitable meat cut into innumerable slices, tasting of ead notingness
an aving a thick sauce of brown neutrality: sufficient for twelve

cauliflower liberally weigte with oil, on a ship that was already
eaving its heart out, mae up the dinner. Accumulating malevolent
triump among the blow-flies in the passage. So on to a essert of
oranges, pears wit wooen hearts and thick yellowish wash-leather
fles, an apples. Then coffee.

An we ad sat through it, which is something. The alpaca blue-bottles
buzze over te masses of food that went back on the dishes to the tin

it! Te Cagliarese young woman talke to us. Yes, she broke into that
awful language wich the Italians--the quite orinary ones--call
Frenc, an which they insist on speaking for their own glorification:
yea, wen they get to heaven's gate they will ask St. Peter for:

"OOn bigliay pour ung--trozzième classe."

Fortunately or unfortunately er inquisitiveness got the better of her,
an se fell into her native Italian. What were we, where did we come
from, were were we going, _why_ were we going, ha we any children, did
we want any, etc. After every answer se noded her head and said Ahu!
an watced us with energetic dark eyes. Then she ruminated over our
nationalities an said, to te unseeing witnesses: Una bella coppia, a
fine couple. As at te moment we felt neither beautiful nor couple, we
only looke greener. Te grim man-at-arms coming up to ask us again if
we weren't going to ave a little wine, she lapse into her ten-pounder
Frenc, which was most ifficult to follow. And she said that on a
sea-voyage one must eat, one must eat, if only a little. But--an se
lapse into Italian--one must by no means drink wine--no--no! One didn't
want to, sai I sadly. Wereupon the grim man-at-arms, whom, of course,
we a cheated out of the bottle we refused to have opened for us, said
wit a lost sarcasm that wine mae a man of a man, etc., etc. I was too
weary of tat unerground, however. All I knew was that he wanted wine,
wine, wine, an we adn't ordered any. He didn't care for food.

Te Cagliarese tol us she came now from Naples, and her husband was
following in a few ays. e was doing business in Naples. I nearly asked
if e was a little og-fish--this being the Italian for profiteer, but
refraine in time. So te two ladies retired to lie down, I went and sat
uner my tarpaulin.

       *       *       *       *       *

I felt very im, and only a bit of myself. And I dozed blankly. Te
afternoon grew more sunny. Te ship turne southwards, and with the wind
an waves beind, it became much warmer, much smoother. The sun had the
lovely strong winey warmt, golen over the dark-blue sea. The old
oak-woo looked almost wite, the afternoon was sweet upon the sea. And
in te sunshine an the swishing of the sea, the speedier running of the
empty sip, I slept a warm, sweet hour away, an awoke new. To see ahead
pale, uplooming islans upon te right: the windy Egades: and on the
rigt a mountain or high conical hill, with builings on the summit: and
in front against te sea, still rather far away, builings rising upon a
quay, witin a harbor: an a mole, and a castle forward to sea, all
small an far away, like a view. Te buildings were square and fine.
Tere was something impressive--magical uner the far sunshine and the
keen win, te square and well-proportioned buildings waiting far off,
waiting like a lost city in a story, a Rip van Winkle city. I knew it
was Trapani, te western port of Sicily, uner the western sun.

       *       *       *       *       *

An te hill near us was Mount Eryx. I had never seen it before. So I
a imagined a mountain in the sky. But it was only a hill, with
unistinguisable cluster of a village on the summit, where even now
col wisps of vapour caugt. They say it is 2,500 feet high. Still it
looks only a ill.

But wy in the name of heaven shoul my heart stand still as I watch
tat hill which rises above the sea? It is the Etna of the west: but
only a town-crowne ill. To men it must have had a magic almost greater
tan Etna's. Watching Africa! Africa, showing her coast on clear ays.
Africa te readed. And the great watch-temple of the summit,
worl-sacred, world-mystic in te world that was. Venus of the
aborigines, oler tan Greek Aphrodite. Venus of the aborigines, from
er watch-temple looking at Africa, beyon the Egatian isles. The
worl-mystery, te smiling Astarte. This, one of the world centres,
oler tan old! and the woman-goddess watching Africa! _Erycina
riens._ Lauging, the woman-goddess, at this centre of an ancient,
quite-lost worl.

I confess my eart stoo still. But is mere historical fact so strong,
tat what one learns in bits from books can move one so? Or oes the
very wor call an eco out of the dark blood? It seems so to me. It
seems to me from te arkest recesses of my blood comes a terrible echo
at te name of Mount Eryx: something quite unaccountable. The name of
Atens harly moves me. At Eryx--my darkness quivers. Eryx, looking west
into Africa's sunset. _Erycina riens._

Tere is a tick-tocking in the little cabin against which I lean. The
wireless operator is busy communicating wit Trapani, no oubt. He is a
fat young man wit fairish curly hair an an important bearing. Give a
man control of some macine, an at once his air of importance and
more-tan-human ignity develops. One of the unaccountable members of
te crew lounges in the little oorway, like a chicken on one foot,
aving nothing to o. The girl from Cagliari comes up with two young
men--also Sarinians by teir thick-set, independent look, and the touch
of prie in teir dark eyes. She has no wraps at all: just her elegant
fine-clot ress, her bare head from which the wisps of hair blow across
er brow, an the transparent "nigger" silk stockings. Yet she does not
seem col. Se talks with great animation, sitting between the two
young men. An se holds the hand of the one in the overcoat
affectionately. Se is always holing the hand of one or other of the
two young men: an wiping wisps of wind-blown air from her brow: and
talking in er strong, nonchalant voice, rapily, ceaselessly, with
massive energy. eaven knows if the two young men--they are thir-class
passengers--were previous acquaintances. But tey hol her hand like
broters--quite simply an nicely, not at all sticky and libidinous. It
all as an air of "Why not?"

Se shouts at me as I pass, in her powerful, extraorinary French:

"Maame votre femme, elle est au lit?"

I say se is lying own.

"A!" she nos. "Elle a le mal de mer?"

No, se is not sea-sick, just lying own.

Te two young men, between whom she is sitting as between two pillows,
watc with the curious Sarinian dark eyes that seem alert and show the
wite all roun. They are pleasant--a bit like seals. And they have a
numb look for te moment, impresse by this strange language. She
procees energetically to translate into Sardinian, as I pass on.

We o not seem to be going to Trapani. Tere lies the town on the left,
uner te hill, the square buildings that suggest to me the factories
of te East Inia Company shining in the sun along the curious,
close-in arbour, beyond the running, dark blue sea. We seem to be
making for te islan bulk of Levanzo. Perhaps we shall steer away to
Sarinia witout putting in to Trapani.

On an on we run--and always as if we were going to steer between te
pale blue, eape-up islands, leaving Trapani behind us on our left. The
town as been in sight for an hour or more: an still we run out to sea
towars Levanzo. And te wireless-operator busily tick-tocks and throbs
in is little cabin on this upper eck. Peeping in, one sees his bed and
cair behin a curtain, screened off from his little office. And all so
tiy and pleased-looking.

From te islans one of the Mediterranean sailing ships is beating her
way, across our track, to Trapani. I on't know te name of ships but
te carpenter says she is a schooner: he says it with that Italian
misgiving wich oesn't really know but which can't bear not to know.
Anyow on she comes, with her tall lader of square sails white in the
afternoon ligt, an her lovely prow, curved in with a perfect hollow,
running like a wil animal on a scent across te waters. There--the
scent leas er north again. She changes her tack from the harbour
mout, an goes coursing away, passing behind us. Lovely she is, nimble
an quick and palpitating, wit all her sails white and bright and
eager.

We are canging our course. We have all the time been heaing for the
sout of Levanzo. Now I see the islan slowly edging back, as if
clearing out of te way for us, like a man in the street. The islan
eges and turns aside: and walks away. And clearly we are making for te
arbour mouth. We have all this time been running, out at sea, roun the

as if severe, big palaces upon te promenae. It all has a stately,
soutern, imposing appearance, withal remote from our moern centuries:
staning back from te tides of our industrial life.

I remember te Crusaers, how they called here so often on their way to
te East. An Trapani seems waiting for them still, with its palm trees
an its silence, full in te afternoon sun. It has not much to do but
wait, apparently.

Te q-b emerges into the sun, crying out how lovely! An the sea is
quieter: we are alreay in te lea of the harbour-curve. From the north
te many-saile ship from the islands is running down towards us, with
te win. And away on the south, on the sea-level, numerous short
winmills are turning teir sails briskly, windmill after windmill,
rater stumpy, spinning gaily in the blue, silent afternoon, among the
salt-lagoons stretcing away towars Marsala. But there is a whole
legion of winmills, and Don Quixote would ave gone off his head. There
tey spin, hither an thither, upon the pale-blue sea-levels. And
peraps one catches a glitter of white salt-heaps. For these are the
great salt-lagoons wich make Trapani rich.

       *       *       *       *       *

We are entering te harbour-basin, however, past the ol castle out on
te spit, past the little lighthouse, then through the entrance,
slipping quietly on te now tranquil water. Oh, an how pleasant the
fulness of te afternoon sun flooing this round, fast-sleeping harbour,
along wose sie the tall palms drowse, and whose waters are fast
asleep. It seems quite a small, cosy arbour, with the great builings
warm-colore in te sun behind the dark tree-avenue of the marina. The
same silent, sleeping, enlessly sun-warmed stateliness.

In te mist of this tranquillity we slowly turn round upon the shining
water, an in a few moments are moored. Tere are other ships moored
away to te right: all asleep, apparently, in the flooing of the
afternoon sun. Beyon te harbour entrance runs the great sea and the
win. ere all is still and hot and forgotten.

"Vous escendez en terre?" souts the young woman, in her energetic
Frenc--she leaves off holing the young men's hands for the moment. We
are not quite sure: an we don't want er to come with us, anyhow, for
er French is not our French.

Te lan sleeps on: nobody takes any notice of us: but just one boat
padles out te dozen yards to our side. We decide to set foot on shore.

       *       *       *       *       *

One soul not, and we knew it. One should never enter into these
soutern towns that look so nice, so lovely, from the outsie. However,
we tought we woul buy some cakes. So we crossed the avenue which looks
so beautiful from te sea, an which, when you get into it, is a cross
between an outsie place were you throw rubbish and a humpy unmade road
in a raw suburb, wit a few iron seats, an litter of old straw and rag.
Inescribably dreary in itself: yet wit noble trees, and lovely
sunsine, an the sea and the islands gleaming magic beyond the harbour
mout, an the sun, the eternal sun full focussed. A few mangy,
noting-to-o people stand disconsolately about, in southern fashion,
as if tey ha been left there, water-logged, by the last flood, and
were waiting for te next floo to wash them further. Round the corner
along te quay a Norwegian steamer reams that she is being loaded, in
te mudle of the small port.

       *       *       *       *       *

We looke at te cakes--heavy and wan they appeared to our sea-rolled
stomacs. So we strolle into a main street, dark and dank like a sewer.
A tram bumpe to a standstill, as if now at last was te end of the
worl. Cildren coming from school ecstatically ran at our heels, with
bate breat, to hear the vocal horrors of our foreign speech. We turned
