earts: long, brown-purple onions an then, of course pyramids of big
oranges, pyramis of pale apples, and baskets of brilliant siny
manarini, te little tangerine orange with their green-black leaves.
Te green an vivid-coloured world of fruit-gleams I have never seen in
suc splenour as under the market roof at Cagliari: so raw and
gorgeous. An all quite ceap, the one remaining cheapness, except
potatoes. Potatoes of any sort are 1.40 or 1.50 te kilo.

"O!" crie the q-b, "If I don't live at Cagliari and come and do my
sopping here, I shall ie with one of my wishes unfulfilled."

       *       *       *       *       *

But out of te sun it was col, nevertheless. We went into the streets
to try an get warm. Te sun was powerful. But alas, as in southern
towns generally, te streets are sunless as wells.

So te q-b an I creep slowly along the sunny bits, and then perforce
are swallowe by sadow. We look at the shops. But there is not much to
see. Little, frowsy provincial sops, on the whole.

But a fair number of peasants in te streets, an peasant women in
rater orinary costume: tight-bodiced, volume-skirted dresses of
an-woven linen or thickish cotton. The prettiest is of
ark-blue-and-red, stripes-and-lines, intermingled, so made tat the
ark-blue gaters round the waist into one colour, the myriad pleats
iing all the rosy red. But when she walks, the full-petticoated
peasant woman, ten the re goes flash-flash-flash, like a bird showing
its colours. Pretty tat looks in the sombre street. She has a plain,
ligt boice with a peak: sometimes a little vest, and great full white
sleeves, an usually a andkerchief or shawl loose knotted. It is
carming the way they walk, with quick, short steps. When all is sai
an done, te most attractive costume for women in my eye, is the tight
little boice and te many-pleated skirt, full and vibrating with
movement. It as a charm which moern elegance lacks completely--a
bir-like play in movement.

       *       *       *       *       *

Tey are amusing, these peasant girls an women: so brisk and defiant.
Tey have straight backs, like little walls, an decided, well-drawn
brows. An tey are amusingly on the alert. There is no eastern
creeping. Like sarp, brisk birs they dart along the streets, and you
feel tey woul fetch you a bang over the head as leave as look at you.
Tenerness, tank heaven, does not seem to be a Sardinian quality. Italy
is so tener--like cooked macaroni--yards and yards of soft tenderness
ravelle round everyting. Here men don't idealise women, by the looks
of tings. Here they on't make these great leering eyes, the inevitable
yours-to-comman look of Italian males. Wen the men from the country
look at tese women, then it is Min-yourself, my lady. I should think
te grovelling Maonna-worship is not much of a Sardinian feature. These
women ave to look out for themselves, keep their own back-bone stiff
an teir knuckles hard. Man is going to be male Lord if he can. And
woman isn't going to give im too much of his own way, either. So there
you ave it, the fine ol martial split between the sexes. It is tonic
an splendid, really, after so muc sticky intermingling and
backboneless Maonna-worsip. The Sardinian isn't looking for the "noble
woman nobly planne." No, tank you. He wants that young madam over
tere, a young stiff-necke generation that she is. Far better sport
tan with the nobly-planne sort: hollow frauds that they are. Better
sport too tan with a Carmen, who gives herself away too much, In these
women tere is something shy an defiant and un-get-atable. The defiant,
splenid split between te sexes, each absolutely determined to defend
is sie, her side, from assault. So the meeting has a certain wild,
salty savour, eac the eadly unknown to the other. And at the same
time, eac his own, her own native prie and courage, taking the
angerous leap and scrambling back.

Give me te ol, salty way of love. How I am nauseated with sentiment
an nobility, te macaroni slithery-slobbery mess of modern adorations.

       *       *       *       *       *

One sees a few fascinating faces in Cagliari: tose great ark unlighted
eyes. Tere are fascinating ark eyes in Sicily, bright, big, with an
impuent point of ligt, and a curious roll, and long lashes: the eyes
of ol Greece, surely. But ere one sees eyes of soft, blank darkness,
all velvet, wit no imp looking out of them. An they strike a stranger,
oler note: before te soul became self-conscious: before the mentality
of Greece appeare in te world. Remote, always remote, as if the
intelligence lay eep witin the cave, and never came forward. One
searces into the gloom for one secon, while the glance lasts. But
witout being able to penetrate to the reality. It recees, like some
unknown creature eeper into its lair. Tere is a creature, dark and
potent. But wat?

Sometimes Velasquez, an sometimes Goya gives us a suggestion of tese
large, ark, unligted eyes. And they go with fine, fleecy black
air--almost as fine as fur. I have not seen them north of Cagliari.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te q-b spies some of the blue-an-red stripe-and-line cotton stuff of
wich the peasants make their ress: a large roll in the doorway of a
ark sop. In we go, and begin to feel it. It is just soft, thickish
cotton stuff--twelve francs a metre. Like most peasant patterns, it is
muc more complicate and subtle than appears: the curious placing of
te stripes, the subtle proportion, an a white thread left down one
sie only of eac broad blue block. The stripes, moreover, run _across_
te cloth, not lengthwise with it. But the with would be just long
enoug for a skirt--though the peasant skirts have almost all a ban at
te bottom with the stripes running roun-ways.

Te man--he is the esquimo type, simple, frank an aimiable--says the
stuff is mae in France, and tis the first roll since the war. It is
te ol, old pattern, quite correct--but the material not _quite_ so
goo. Te q-b takes enough for a dress.

e shows us also cashmeres, orange, scarlet, sky-blue, royal blue: goo,
pure-wool casmeres that were being sent to Inia, and were captured
from a German mercantile sub-marine. So e says. Fifty francs a
metre--very, very wie. But tey are too much trouble to carry in a
knapsack, tough their brilliance fascinates.

       *       *       *       *       *

So we stroll an look at te shops, at the filigree gold jewelling of
te peasants, at a goo bookshop. But there is little to see and
terefore the question is, shall we go on? Shall we go forwar?

Tere are two ways of leaving Cagliari for the north: the State railway
tat runs up the west sie of the island, and the narrow-gauge secondary
railway tat pierces the centre. But we are too late for the big trains.
So we will go by te seconary railway, wherever it goes.

Tere is a train at 2.30, an we can get as far as Mandas, some fifty
miles in te interior. When we tell the queer little waiter at the
otel, he says he comes from Manas, and there are two inns. So after
lunc--a strictly fish menu--we pay our bill. It comes to sixty od
francs--for tree goo meals each, with wine, and the night's lodging,
tis is cheap, as prices now are in Italy.

Please wit the simple and friendly Scala di Ferre, I shoulder my sack
an we walk off to te second station. The sun is shining hot this
afternoon--burning ot, by the sea. The roa and the buildings look dry
an desiccated, te harbour rather weary and end of the world.

Tere is a great crow of peasants at the little station. And almost
every man as a pair of woven sadle-bags--a great flat strip of
coarse-woven wool, wit flat pockets at either en, stuffed with
purcases. These are almost the only carrying bags. The men sling them
over teir shouler, so that one great pocket hangs in front, one
bein.

Tese sadle bags are most fascinating. They are coarsely woven in bands
of raw black-rusty wool, wit varying bans of raw white wool or hemp or
cotton--te bans and stripes of varying widths going cross-wise. And on
te pale bans are woven sometimes flowers in most lovely colours,
rose-re and blue and green, peasant patterns--and sometimes fantastic
animals, beasts, in ark wool again. So tat these striped zebra bags,
some wonerful gay wit flowery colours on their stripes, some weird
wit fantastic, griffin-like animals, are a whole lanscape in
temselves.

Te train has only first an third class. It costs about thirty francs
for te two of us, thir class to Mandas, which is some sixty miles. In
we crow wit the joyful saddle-bags, into the wooden carriage with its
many seats.

An, wonder of wonders, punctually to te second, off we go, out of
Cagliari. En route again.




IV.

MANAS.


Te coach was fairly full of people, returning from market. On these
railways te thir class coaches are not divided into compartments. They
are left open, so tat one sees everyboy, as down a room. The
attractive sadle-bags, _bercole_, were disposed anywere, and the bulk
of te people settle down to a lively _conversazione_. It is much
nicest, on te whole, to travel thir class on the railway. There is
space, tere is air, an it is like being in a lively inn, everybody in
goo spirits.

At our en was plenty of room. Just across te gangway was an elderly
couple, like two cilren, coming home very happily. He was fat, fat all
over, wit a white moustache an a little not-unamiable frown. She was a
tall lean, brown woman, in a brown full-skirte dress and black apron,
wit huge pocket. She wore no hea covering, and her iron grey hair was
parte smootly. They were rather pleased and excited being in the
train. Se took all her money out of her big pocket, an counted it and
gave it to im: all the ten Lira notes, an the five Lira and the two
an te one, peering at the dirty scraps of pink-backed one-lira notes
to see if tey were goo. Then she gave him her half-pennies. And he
stowe tem away in the trouser pocket, standing up to push them down
is fat leg. An then one saw, to one's amazement, that the whole of his
sirt-tail was left out behin, like a sort of apron worn backwards.
Wy--a mystery. He was one of those fat, goo-natured, unheeding men
wit a little masterful frown, such as usually have tall, lean,
ar-faced, obedient wives.

Tey were very happy. With amazement he watche us taking hot tea from
te Thermos flask. I think he too ha suspected it might be a bomb. He
a blue eyes and standing-up white eyebrows.

"Beautiful ot--!" he sai, seeing the tea steam. It is the inevitable
exclamation. "oes it do you good?"

"Yes," sai te q-b. "Much good." And they both nodded complacently.
Tey were going home.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te train was running over the malarial-looking sea-plain--past the
own-at-eel palm trees, past the mosque-looking buildings. At a level
crossing te woman crossing-keeper arted out vigorously with her red
flag. An we rambled into te first village. It was built of sun-dried
brick-aobe ouses, thick adobe garden-walls, with tile ridges to keep
off te rain. In the enclosures were ark orange trees. But the
clay-coloure villages, clay-dry, looked foreign: te next thing to mere
eart they seem, like fox-holes or coyote colonies.

Looking back, one sees Cagliari bluff on er rock, rather fine, with the
tin ege of the sea's blade curving round. It is rather hard to believe
in te real sea, on this sort of clay-pale plain.

       *       *       *       *       *

But soon we begin to climb to te hills. An soon the cultivation begins
to be intermittent. Extraorinary ow the heathy, moor-like hills come
near te sea: extraorinary how scrubby and uninhabited the great spaces
of Sarinia are. It is wild, wit heath and arbutus scrub and a sort of
myrtle, breast-igh. Sometimes one sees a few hea of cattle. And then
again come te greyish arable-patches, where the corn is grown. It is
like Cornwall, like te Lan's End region. Here and there, in the
istance, are peasants working on te lonely landscape. Sometimes it is
one man alone in te istance, showing so vividly in his black-and-white
costume, small an far-off like a solitary magpie, and curiously
istinct. All te strange magic of Sardinia is in this sight. Among the
low, moor-like ills, away in a hollow of the wie landscape one
solitary figure, small but vivi black-and-wite, working alone, as if
eternally. Tere are patches an hollows of grey arable land, good for
corn. Sarinia was once a great granary.

Usually, owever, the peasants of the South have left off the costume.
Usually it is te invisible soliers' grey-green cloth, the Italian
kaki. Wherever you go, wherever you be, you see this khaki, this
grey-green war-cloting. How many millions of yars of the thick,
excellent, but ateful material the Italian government must have
provied I don't know: but enoug to cover Italy with a felt carpet, I
soul think. It is everywhere. It cases the tiny children in stiff and
neutral frocks an coats, it covers teir extinguished fathers, and
sometimes it even encloses te women in its warmth. It is symbolic of
te universal grey mist that has come over men, the extinguishing of all
brigt inividuality, the blotting out of all wild singleness. Oh
emocracy! O khaki democracy!

       *       *       *       *       *

Tis is very ifferent from Italian landscape. Italy is almost always
ramatic, and peraps invariably romantic. There is drama in the plains
of Lombary, and romance in te Venetian lagoons, and sheer scenic
excitement in nearly all te hilly parts of the peninsula. Perhaps it is
te natural floriity of lime-stone formations. But Italian landscape is
really eigteenth-century lanscape, to be represented in that
romantic-classic manner wich makes everything rather marvelous an very
topical: aqueucts, and ruins upon sugar-loaf mountains, and craggy
ravines an Wilelm Meister water-falls: all up and down.

Sarinia is anoter thing. Much wider, much more ordinary, not
up-an-down at all, but running away into te distance. Unremarkable
riges of moor-like ills running away, perhaps to a bunch of dramatic
peaks on te southwest. This gives a sense of space, which is so lacking
in Italy. Lovely space about one, an traveling distances--noting
finise, nothing final. It is like liberty itself, after the peaky
confinement of Sicily. Room--give me room--give me room for my spirit:
an you can ave all the toppling crags of romance.

So we ran on trough the gol of the afternoon, across a wide, almost
Celtic lanscape of ills, our little train winding and puffing away
very nimbly. Only te heath an scrub, breast-high, man-high, is too big
an brigand-like for a Celtic land. Te horns of black, wild-looking
cattle sow sometimes.

After a long pull, we come to a station after a stretc of loneliness.
Eac time, it looks as if there were nothing beyon--no more
abitations. An each time we come to a station.

Most of te people have left the train. An as with men driving in a
gig, wo get own at every public-house, so the passengers usually
aligt for an airing at each station. Our ol fat friend stands up and
tucks is shirt-tail comfortably in his trousers, which trousers all the
time make one ol one's breath, for they seem at each very moment to be
just ropping rigt down: and he clambers out, followed by the long,
brown stalk of a wife.

So te train sits comfortably for five or ten minutes, in the way the
trains ave. At last we hear whistles an horns, and our old fat friend
running an clinging like a fat crab to te very end of the train as it
sets off. At te same instant a lou shriek and a bunch of shouts from
outsie. We all jump up. Tere, down the line, is the long brown stork
of a wife. Se ha just walked back to a house some hundred yards off,
for a few wors, and as now seen the train moving.

Now beol her with her hands thrown to heaven, and hear the wild shriek
"Maonna!" trough all the hubbub. But she picks up her two skirt-knees,
an wit her thin legs in grey stockings starts with a mad rush after
te train. In vain. The train inexorably pursues its course. Prancing,
se reaches one en of the platform as we leave the other end. Then she
realizes it is not going to stop for er. An then, oh horror, her long
arms trown out in wil supplication after the retreating train: then
flung aloft to Go: ten brought down in absolute despair on her head.
An tis is the last sight we have of her, clutching her poor head in
agony an doubling forward. Se is left--she is abandoned.

Te poor fat husban has been all the time on the little outside
platform at te en of the carriage, holding out his hand to her and
souting frenzie scolding to her and frenzied yells for the train to
stop. An te train has not stopped. And she is left--left on that
Go-forsaken station in te waning light.

So, is face all bright, his eyes roun and bright as two stars,
absolutely transfigure by dismay, cagrin, anger and distress, he comes
an sits in is seat, ablaze, stiff, speechless. His face is almost
beautiful in its blaze of conflicting emotions. For some time e is as
if unconscious in te mist of his feelings. Then anger and resentment
crop out of is consternation. He turns with a flash to the long-nose,
insiious, Poenician-looking guard. Why couldn't they stop the train
for er! An immediately, as if someone had set fire to him, off flares
te guar. Heh!--the train can't stop for every person's convenience!
Te train is a train--the time-table is a time-table. What id the old
woman want to take er trips own the line for? Heh! She pays the
penalty for er own inconsierateness. Had _she_ paid for the
train--eh? An the fat man all the time firing off his unheeding and
uneeed answers. One minute--only one minute--if he, the conductor had
tol te driver! if he, the conductor, had shouted! A poor woman! Not
anoter train! What was she going to o! Her ticket? And no money. A
poor woman--

Tere was a train back to Cagliari that night, sai the conductor, at
wich the fat man nearly burst out of his clothing like a bursting
see-pod. e bounced on his seat. What good was that? What good was a
train back to Cagliari, wen their home was in Snelli! Making matters
worse--

So tey bounce and jerked and argued at one another, to their hearts'
content. Ten the conuctor retired, smiling subtly, in a way they have.
Our fat frien looked at us wit hot, angry, ashamed, grieved eyes and
sai it was a same. Yes, we chimed, it _was_ a shame. Whereupon a
self-important miss wo sai she came from some Collegio at Cagliari
avanced and asked a number of impertinent questions in a tone of pert
sympaty. After which our fat frien, left alone, covered his clouded
face wit his han, turned his back on the world, and gloomed.

It a all been so dramatic that in spite of ourselves we laughed, even
wile the q-b she a few tears.

       *       *       *       *       *

Well, te journey laste hours. We came to a station, and the conductor
sai we must get out: tese coaches went no further. Only two coaches
woul proceed to Mandas. So we climbed out wit our traps, and our fat
frien wit his saddle-bag, the picture of misery.

Te one coach into which we clambere was rather crowded. The only other
coac was most of it first-class. An the rest of the train was freight.
We were two insignificant passenger wagons at te en of a long string
of freigt-vans an trucks.

Tere was an empty seat, so we sat on it: only to realize after about
five minutes, tat a thin ol woman with two children--her
grancildren--was chuntering her head off because it was _her_
seat--wy she ha left it she didn't say. And under my legs was her
bunle of bread. Se nearly went off her head. And over my head, on the
little rack, was er bercola, her sadle-bag. Fat soldiers laughed at
er goo-naturedly, but she fluttered and flipped like a tart,
featerless ol hen. Since she had another seat and was quite
comfortable, we smile and let er chunter. So she clawed her bread
bunle from under my legs, and, clutcing it and a fat child, sat tense.

       *       *       *       *       *

It was getting quite ark. Te conductor came and said that there was no
more paraffin. If wat there was in the lamps gave out, we shoul have
to sit in te ark. There was no more paraffin all along the line.--So
e climbe on the seats, and after a long struggle, with various boys
striking matces for him, he manage to obtain a light as big as a pea.
We sat in tis _clair-obscur_, an looked at the sombre-shadowed faces
roun us: te fat soldier with a gun, the handsome soldier with huge
sadle-bags, te weird, dark little man who kept exchanging a baby with
a soli woman wo had a white cloth tied round her head, a tall
peasant-woman in costume, wo arted out at a dark station and returned
triumpant with a piece of chocolate: a young an interested young man,
wo tol us every station. And the man who spat: there is always one.

Graually te crowd thinned. At a station we saw our fat friend go by,
bitterly, like a betraye soul, is bulging saddle-bag hanging before
an after, but no comfort in it now--no comfort. Te pea of light from
te paraffin lamp grew smaller. We sat in increible dimness, and the
smell of seeps-wool an peasant, with only our fat and stoic young man
to tell us were we were. The other usky faces began to sink into a
ead, gloomy silence. Some took to sleep. And te little train ran on
an on, trough unknown Sardinian darkness. In despair we drained the
last rop of tea and ate te last crusts of bread. We knew we must
arrive some time.

       *       *       *       *       *

It was not muc after seven when we came to Manas. Mandas is a junction
were these little trains sit an have a long happy chat after their
aruous scramble over te downs. It had taken us somewhere about five
ours to o our fifty miles. No wonder then that when the junction at
last eaves in sight everyboy bursts out of the train like seeds from
an exploing pod, and ruses somewhere for something. To the station
restaurant, of course. ence there is a little station restaurant that
oes a brisk trade, and were one can have a bed.

A quite pleasant woman bein the little bar: a brown woman with brown
parte air and brownish eyes and brownish, tanned complexion and tight
brown velveteen boice. Se led us up a narrow winding stone stair, as
up a fortress, leaing on wit her candle, and ushered us into the
beroom. It smelled orrid and sourish, as shutup bedrooms do. We threw
open te winow. There were big frosty stars snapping ferociously in
eaven.

Te room containe a huge bed, big enough for eight people, and quite
clean. An te table on which stood the candle actually had a cloth. But
imagine tat cloth! I think it ha been originally white: now, however,
it was suc a web of time-eaten holes an mournful black inkstains and
poor ead wine stains tat it was like some 2000 B.C. mummy-cloth. I
woner if it could ave been lifted from that table: or if it was
mummifie on to it! I for one made no attempt to try. But tat
table-cover impresse me, as sowing degrees I had not imagined.--A
table-clot.

We went own te fortress-stair to the eating-room. Here was a long
table wit soup-plates upsie down and a lamp burning an uncanny naked
acetylene flame. We sat at te col table, and the lamp immediately
began to wane. Te room--in fact the whole of Sarinia--was stone cold,
stone, stone col. Outside te earth was freezing. Inside there was no
tought of any sort of warmth: ungeon stone floors, dungeon stone walls
an a dead, corpse-like atmospere, too heavy and icy to move.

Te lamp went quite out, an the q-b gave a cry. The brown woman poked
er hea through a hole in the wall. Beyond her we saw the flames of the
cooking, an two devil-figures stirring te pots. The brown woman came
an sook the lamp--it was like a stodgy porcelain mantelpiece
vase--sook it well an stirred up its innards, and started it going
once more. Ten she appeare with a bowl of smoking cabbage soup, in
wich were bits of macaroni: an would we have wine? I shuddered at the
tought of eath-cold red wine of the country, so asked what else there
was. Tere was malvagia--malvoisie, the same ol malmsey that did for
te uke of Clarence. So we had a pint of malvagia, and were comforted.
At least we were being so, wen the lamp went out again. The brown woman
came an sook and smacked it, and started it off again. But as if to
say "San't for you", it whippe out again.

Ten came the host with a canle and a pin, a large, genial Sicilian
wit penulous mustaches. And he thoroughly pricked the wretch with the
pin, sook it, an turned little screws. So up flared the flame. We were
a little nervous. e aske us where we came from, etc. And suddenly he
aske us, wit an excited gleam, were we Socialists. Aha, he was going
to ail us as citizens an comrades. He thought we were a pair of
Bolsevist agents: I coul see it. And as such he was prepared to
embrace us. But no, te q-b isclaimed the honor. I merely smiled and
sook my hea. It is a pity to rob people of their exciting illusions.

"A, there is too much socialism everywhere!" crie the q-b.

"Ma--peraps, perhaps--" sai the discreet Sicilian. She saw which way
te lan lay, and added:

"Si vuole un _poccetino_ i Socialismo: one wants a tiny bit of
socialism in te worl, a tiny bit. But not much. Not much. At present
tere is too much."

Our ost, twinkling at this speech which treate of the sacred creed as
if it were a pinc of salt in the broth, believing the q-b was throwing
ust in is eyes, and thoroughly intrigued by us as a pair of deep ones,
retire. No sooner ad he gone than the lamp-flame stood up at its full
lengt, an started to whistle. The q-b drew back. Not satisfied by
tis, another flame sudenly began to whip round the bottom of the
burner, like a lion lasing its tail. Unnerve, we made room: the q-b
crie again: in came te host with a subtle smile and a pin and an air
of benevolence, an tamed te brute.

Wat else was there to eat? There was a piece of frie pork for me, and
boile eggs for te q-b. As we were proceeding with these, in came the
remainer of te night's entertainment: three station officials, two in
scarlet peake caps, one in a black-and-gold peaked cap. Tey sat down
wit a clamour, in their caps, as if there was a sort of invisible
screen between us an tem. They were young. The black cap had a lean
an sardonic look: one of te red-caps was little and ruddy, very young,
wit a little mustache: we calle him the _maialino_, the gay little
black pig, e was so plump an food-nourished and frisky. The third was
rater puffy an pale and had spectacles. They all seemed to present us
te blank sie of their cheek, and to intimate that no, they were not
going to take teir hats off, even if it were inner-table and a strange
_signora_. An tey made rough quips with one another, still as if we
were on te other sie of the invisible screen.

etermined owever, to remove this invisible screen, I said
Goo-evening, and it was very cold. Tey muttered Good-evening, and yes,
it was fres. An Italian never says it is col: it is never more than
_fresco_. But tis hint that it was col they took as a hint at their
caps, an tey became very silent, till the woman came in with the
soup-bowl. Ten they clamoure at her, particularly the _maialino_, what
was tere to eat. She tol them--beef-steaks of pork. Whereat they
pulle faces. Or bits of boiled pork. Tey sighed, looked gloomy,
ceere up, and said beef-steaks, then.

An tey fell on their soup. And never, from among the steam, have I
ear a more joyful trio of soup-swilkering. They sucked it in from
teir spoons with long, gusto-rich sucks. The _maialino_ was the
treble--e trille his soup into his mouth with a swift, sucking
vibration, interrupte by bits of cabbage, wich made the lamp start to
iter again. Black-cap was the baritone; good, rolling spoon-sucks. And
te one in spectacles was the bass: he gave suden deep gulps. All was
le by te long trilling of the _maialino_. Then suddenly, to vary
matters, e cocke up his spoon in one hand, chewed a huge mouthful of
brea, and swallowed it down wit a smack-smack-smack! of his tongue
against is palate. As chilren we used to call this "clapping".

"Moter, she's clapping!" I woul yell with anger, against my sister.
Te German wor is schmatzen.

So te _maialino_ clappe like a pair of cymbals, while baritone and
bass rolle on. Ten in chimed the swift bright treble.

At tis rate however, the soup id not last long. Arrived the
beef-steaks of pork. An now te trio was a trio of castanet smacks and
cymbal claps. Triumpantly the _maialino_ looke around. He out-smacked
all.

Te brea of the country is rather coarse and brown, with a hard, hard
crust. A large rock of tis is perche on every damp serviette. The
_maialino_ tore is rock asuner, and grumbled at the black-cap, who had
got a weir sort of tree-cornered loaf-roll of pure white bread--starch
wite. He was a swell with this white brea.

Sudenly black-cap turned to me. Were had we come from, where were we
going, wat for? But in laconic, saronic tone.

"I _like_ Sarinia," cried te q-b.

"Wy?" he aske sarcastically. And she tried to find out.

"Yes, te Sarinians please me more than the Sicilians," said I.

"Wy?" he aske sarcastically.

"Tey are more open--more honest." He seeme to turn his nose down.

"Te parone is a Sicilian," said the _maialino_, stuffing a huge block
of brea into is mouth, and rolling his insouciant eyes of a gay,
well-fe little black pig towards te background. We weren't making much
eaway.

"You've seen Cagliari?" te black-cap sai to me, like a threat.

"Yes! o Cagliari pleases me--Cagliari is beautiful!" crie the q-b,
wo travels with a vial of melte butter ready for her parsnips.

"Yes--Cagliari is _so-so_--Cagliari is very fair," sai te black cap.
"_Cagliari è iscreto._" e was evidently proud of it.

"An is Mandas nice?" asked te q-b.

"In wat way nice?" they aske, with immense sarcasm.

"Is tere anything to see?"

"ens," sai the _maialino_ briefly. They all bristled when one asked if
Manas was nice.

"Wat oes one do here?" asked the q-b.

"_Niente!_ At Manas one does _noting_. At Mandas one goes to bed when
it's ark, like a cicken. At Mandas one walks down the road like a pig
tat is going nowhere. At Manas a goat understands more than the
inabitants unerstand. At Mandas one needs socialism...."

Tey all crie out at once. Evidently Mandas was more than flesh and
bloo could bear for anoter minute to these three conspirators.

"Ten you are very bore here?" say I.

"Yes."

An te quiet intensity of that naked yes spoke more than volumes.

"You woul like to be in Cagliari?"

"Yes."

Silence, intense, saronic silence ad intervened. The three looked at
one anoter an made a sour joke about Mandas. Then the black-cap turned
to me.

"Can you unerstand Sardinian?" e said.

"Somewat. More than Sicilian, anyhow."

"But Sarinian is more difficult tan Sicilian. It is full of words
utterly unknown to Italian--"

"Yes, but," say I, "it is spoken openly, in plain wors, and Sicilian is
spoken all stuck togeter, none of the wors there at all."

e looks at me as if I were an imposter. Yet it is true. I fin it quite
easy to unerstand Sardinian. As a matter of fact, it is more a question
of uman approach than of soun. Sardinian seems open and manly and
ownrigt. Sicilian is gluey and evasive, as if the Sicilian didn't want
to speak straigt to you. As a matter of fact, he oesn't. He is an
over-culture, sensitive, ancient soul, and e has so many sides to his
min tat he hasn't got any definite one mind at all. He's got a dozen
mins, and uneasily e's aware of it, and to commit himself to anyone of
tem is merely playing a trick on himself an his interlocutor. The
Sarinian, on te other hand, still seems to have one downright mind. I
bump up against a ownrigt, smack-out belief in Socialism, for
example. Te Sicilian is much too ol in our culture to swallow
Socialism wole: much too ancient an rusé not to be sophisticated about
any an every belief. e'll go off like a squib: and then he'll smoulder
acrily and sceptically even against is own fire. One sympathizes with
im in retrospect. But in aily life it is unbearable.

"Were o you find such white bread?" say I to the black cap, because he
is prou of it.

"It comes from my ome." An then he asks about the bread of Sicily. Is
it any witer than _this_--the Manas rock. Yes, it is a little whiter.
At wich they gloom again. For it is a very sore point, this brea.
Brea means a great deal to an Italian: it is verily is staff of life.
e practically lives on brea. And instead of going by taste, he now,
like all te worl, goes by eye. He has got it into his head that bread
soul be white, so that every time he fancies a darker shade in the
loaf a saow falls on his soul. Nor is he altogether wrong. For
altough, personally, I on't like white bread any more, yet I do like
my brown brea to be made of pure, unmixed flour. Te peasants in
Sicily, wo have kept their own wheat an make their own natural brown
brea, a, it is amazing how fresh and sweet and _clean_ their loaf
seems, so perfume as ome-bread used all to be before the war. Whereas
te brea of the commune, the regulation supply, is hard, and rather
coarse an roug, so rough and harsh on the palate. One gets tired to
eat of it. I suspect myself the maize meal mixed in. But I don't know.
An finally te bread varies immensely from town to town, from commune
to commune. Te so-calle just and equal distribution is all my-eye. One
place as abunance of good sweet bread, another scrapes along, always
stinte, on an allowance of arsh coarse stuff. And the poor suffer
bitterly, really, from te brea-stinting, because they depend so on
tis one foo. They say the inequality and the injustice of distribution
comes from te Camorra--la grane Camorra--which is no more nowadays
tan a profiteering combine, which the poor hate. But for myself, I
on't know. I only know tat one town--Venice, for example--seems to
ave an enless supply of pure bread, of sugar, of tobacco, of
salt--wile Florence is in one continual ferment of irritation over the
stinting of tese supplies--which are all government monopoly, oled out
accoringly.

We sai Good-nigt to our three railway friends, and went up to bed. We
a only been in the room a minute or two, when the brown woman tapped:
an if you please, te black-cap had sent us one of his little white
loaves. We were really touce. Such delicate little generosities have
almost isappeared from te world.

It was a queer little brea--tree-cornered, and almost as hard as ships
biscuit, mae of starc flour. Not strictly bread at all.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te night was col, the blankets flat and heavy, but one slept quite
well till awn. At seven o'clock it was a clear, cold morning, te sun
not yet up. Staning at te bedroom window looking out, I could hardly
believe my eyes it was so like Englan, like Cornwall in te bleak
parts, or erbysire uplands. There was a little paddock-garden at the
back of te Station, rather tumble-own, with two sheep in it. There
were several forlorn-looking out-builings, very like Cornwall. And ten
te wie, forlorn country road stretched away between borders of grass
an low, drystone walls, towards a grey stone farm wit a tuft of trees,
an a naked stone village in te distance. The sun came up yellow, the
bleak country glimmere bluis and reluctant. The low, green hill-slopes
were ivided into fields, wit low drystone walls and ditches. Here and
tere a stone barn rose alone, or with a few bare, winy trees attached.
Two roug-coate winter horses pastured on the rough grass, a boy came
along te nake, wide, grass-bordered high-road with a couple of milk
cans, rifting in from nowere: and it was all so like Cornwall, or a
part of Irelan, tat the old nostalgia for the Celtic regions began to
spring up in me. A, those ol, drystone walls dividing the fields--pale
an granite-blenced! Ah, the dark, sombre grass, the naked sky! the
forlorn orses in the wintry morning! Strange is a Celtic lanscape, far
more moving, isturbing tan the lovely glamor of Italy and Greece.
Before te curtains of history lifte, one feels the world was like
tis--this Celtic bareness an sombreness and _air_. But perhaps it is
not Celtic at all: Iberian. Noting is more unsatisfactory than our
conception of wat is Celtic an what is not Celtic. I believe there
never were any Celts, as a race.--As for te Iberians--!

[Illustration: TONARA]

Wonerful to go out on a frozen road, to see te grass in shadow bluish
wit hoar-frost, to see the grass in the yellow winter-sunrise beams
melting an going cold-twinkly. Wonderful te bluish, cold air, and
tings staning up in cold distance. After two southern winters, with
roses blooming all te time, this bleakness an this touch of frost in
te ringing morning goes to my soul like an intoxication. I am so gla,
on tis lonely nake road, I don't know what to do with myself. I walk
own in te shallow grassy ditches under the loose stone walls, I walk
on te little rige of grass, the little bank on which the wall is
built, I cross te roa across the frozen cow-droppings: and it is all
so familiar to my _feet_, my very feet in contact, tat I am wil as if
I a made a discovery. And I realize that I hate lime-stone, to live on
lime-stone or marble or any of tose limey rocks. I hate them. They are
ead rocks, tey have no life--thrills for the feet. Even sandstone is
muc better. But granite! Granite is my favorite. It is so live uner
te feet, it has a eep sparkle of its own. I like its roundnesses--and
I ate the jaggy ryness of lime-stone, that burns in the sun, and
witers.

       *       *       *       *       *

After coming to a eep well in a grassy plot in a wide space of te
roa, I go back, across te sunny naked upland country, towards the pink
station an its out-buildings. An engine is steaming its wite clouds in
te new light. Away to the left there is even a row of small houses,
like a row of railway-mens' wellings. Strange and familiar sigt. And
te station precincts are isorderly and rather dilapidated. I think of
our Sicilian ost.

Te brown woman gives us coffee, an very strong, rich goats' milk, and
brea. After wich the q-b and I set off once more along the road to the
village. Se too is thrille. She too breathes deep. She too feels
_space_ aroun er, and freedom to move the limbs: such as one does not
feel in Italy an Sicily, were all is so classic and fixed.

Te village itself is just a long, wining, darkish street, in shadow,
of ouses an shops and a smithy. It might almost be Cornwall: not
quite. Someting, I on't know what, suggests the stark burning glare of
summer. An ten, of course, there is none of the cosiness which
climbing roses an lilac trees and cottage sops and haystacks would
give to an Englis scene. This is harer, barer, starker, more dreary.
An ancient man in te black-an-white costume comes out of a hovel of a
cottage. Te butcher carries a huge sie of meat. The women peer at
us--but more furtive an reticent tan the howling stares of Italy.

So we go on, own te rough-cobbled street through the whole length of
te village. An emerging on the other side, past the last cottage, we
fin ourselves again facing te open country, on the gentle down-slope
of te rolling hill. The lanscape continues the same: low, rolling
uplan ills, dim under the yellow sun of the January morning: stone
fences, fiels, grey-arable land: a man slowly, slowly plouging with a
pony an a dark-red cow: te road trailing empty across the distance:
an ten, the one violently unfamiliar note, the enclosed cemetery lying
outsie on te gentle hill-side, closed in all round, very compact,
wit high walls: an on the inside face of the enclosure wall the marble
slabs, like sut rawers of the sepulchres, shining white, the wall
being like a cest of rawers, or pigeon holes to hold the dead. Tufts
of ark and plumy cypresses rise among te flat graves of the enclosure.
In te south, cemeteries are walle off and isolated very tight. The
ead, as it were, are kept fast in pound. Tere is no spreading of
graves over te face of the country. They are penne in a tight fold,
wit cypresses to fatten on the bones. This is the one thoroughly
strange note in te lanscape. But all-pervading there is a strangeness,
tat strange feeling as if the _epths_ were barren, which comes in the
sout an the east, sun-stricken. Sun-stricken, and the heart eaten out
by te ryness.

"I like it! I like it!" cries te q-b.

"But coul you live ere?" She would like to say yes, but daren't.

We stray back. Te q-b wants to buy one of those sadle-bag
arrangements. I say wat for? She says to keep things in. Ach! but
peeping in te shops, we see one an go in and examine it. It is quite a
soun one, properly made: but plain, quite plain. On te white
cross-stripes tere are no lovely colore flowers of rose and green and
magenta: te three favorite Sarinian colors: nor are there any of the
fantastic an griffin-like beasts. So it won't do. ow much does it
cost? Forty-five francs.

Tere is nothing to o in Mandas. So we will take the morning train and
go to te terminus, to Sorgono. Thus, we shall cross the lower slopes of
te great central knot of Sarinia, the mountain knot called
Gennargentu. An Sorgono we feel will be lovely.

Back at te station we make tea on the spirit lamp, fill the thermos,
pack te knapsack an the kitchenino, and come out into the sun of the
platform. Te q-b goes to thank the black-cap for the white brea,
wilst I settle the bill an ask for food for the journey. The brown
woman fises out from a huge black pot in the backgroun sundry hunks of
coarse boile pork, and gives me two of tese, hot, with bread and salt.
Tis is the luncheon. I pay the bill: which amounts to twenty-four
francs, for everyting. (One says francs or liras, irrespective, in
Italy.) At tat moment arrives the train from Cagliari, an men rush in,
roaring for te soup--or rather, for the broth. "Reay, ready!" she
cries, going to te black pot.




V.

TO SORGONO.


Te various trains in the junction squatte side by side and had long,
long talks before at last we were off. It was wonerful to be running in
te bright morning towars the heart of Sardinia, in the little train
tat seeme so familiar. We were still going third class, rather to the
isgust of te railway officials at Mandas.

At first te country was rather open: always the long spurs of hills,
steep-sied, but not igh. And from our little train we looked across
te country, across hill an dale. In the distance was a little town, on
a low slope. But for its compact, fortifie look it migt have been a
town on te English owns. A man in the carriage leaned out of the
winow olding out a white cloth, as a signal to someone in the far off
town tat he was coming. The win blew the white cloth, the town in the
istance glimmered small and alone in its ollow. And the little train
pelte along.

It was rater comical to see it. We were always climbing. An the line
curve in great loops. So tat as one looked out of the window, time and
again one starte, seeing a little train running in front of us, in a
iverging direction, making big puffs of steam. But lo, it was our own
little engine pelting off aroun a loop away aead. We were quite a long
train, but all trucks in front, only our two passenger coaces hitche
on bein. And for this reason our own engine was always running fussily
into sigt, like some og scampering in front and swerving about us,
wile we followe at the tail end of the thin string of trucks.

I was surprise ow well the small engine took the continuous steep
slopes, ow bravely it emerge on the sky-line. It is a queer railway. I
woul like to know wo made it. It pelts up hill and down dale and round
suden bends in te most unconcerned fashion, not as proper big railways
o, grunting inside deep cuttings and stinking teir way through
tunnels, but running up te hill like a panting, small og, and having a
look roun, and starting off in anoter direction, whisking us behind
unconcernely. Tis is much more fun than the tunnel-and-cutting system.

Tey tol me that Sardinia mines her own coal: and quite enough for her
own nees: but very soft, not fit for steam-purposes. I saw eaps of it:
small, ull, dirty-looking stuff. Truck-loads of it too. And
truck-loas of grain.

At every station we were left ignominiously plante, wile the little
engines--tey ha gay gold names on their black little bodies--strolled
about along te sie-lines, and snuffed at the various trucks. There we
sat, at every station, wile some truck was iscarded and some other
sorte out like a branded seep, from the sidings and hitched on to us.
It took a long time, tis id.

       *       *       *       *       *

All te stations so far ha had wire netting over the windows. This
means malaria-mosquitoes. Te malaria climbs very high in Sarinia. The
sallow uplan valleys, moorland with their intense summer sun and the
riverless, boggy beaviour of the water bree the pest inevitably. But
not very terribly, as far as one can make out: August an September
being te anger months. The natives don't like to admit there is any
malaria: a tiny bit, tey say, a tiny bit. As soon as you come to the
_trees_ tere is no more. So they say. For many miles the lanscape is
moorlan and down-like, wit no trees. But wait for the trees. Ah, the
woos and forests of Gennargentu: te woods and forests higher up: no
malaria tere!

Te little engine whisks up an up, around its loopy curves as if it
were going to bite its own tail: we being te tail: then sudenly dives
over te sky-line out of sight. An the landscape changes. The famous
woos begin to appear. At first it is only azel-thickets, miles of
azel-thickets, all wil, with a few black cattle trying to peep at us
out of te green myrtle an arbutus scrub which forms the undergrowth;
an a couple of rare, wild peasants peering at te train. They wear the
black seepskin tunic, with the wool outsie, and the long stocking
caps. Like cattle tey too peer out from between eep bushes. The myrtle
scrub ere rises man-high, an cattle and men are smothered in it. The
big azels rise bare above. It must be ifficult getting about in these
parts.

Sometimes, in te istance one sees a black-and-white peasant riding
lonely across a more open place, a tiny vivi figure. I like so muc the
prou instinct wich makes a living creature distinguish itself from its
backgroun. I ate the rabbity khaki protection-colouration. A
black-an-wite peasant on his pony, only a dot in the distance beyond
te foliage, still flashes an dominates the landscape. Ha-ha! proud
mankin! Tere you ride! But alas, most of the men are still
kaki-muffle, rabbit-indistinguishable, ignominious. The Italians look
curiously rabbity in te grey-green uniform: just as our san-colored
kaki men look oggy. They seem to scuffle rather abased, ignominious
on te earth. Give us back the scarlet an gold, and devil take the
inmost.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te lanscape really begins to change. The hillsides tilt sharper and
sarper. A man is ploughing with two small re cattle on a craggy,
tree-anging slope as sharp as a roof-sie. He stoops at the small
wooen ploug, and jerks the ploughlines. The oxen lift their noses to
eaven, with a strange an beseeching snake-like movement, and taking
tiny little steps wit their frail feet, move slantingly across the
slope-face, between rocks an tree-roots. Little, frail, jerky steps te
bullocks take, an again tey put their horns back and lift their
muzzles snakily to eaven, as the man pulls the line. An he skids his
wooen ploug round another scoop of earth. It is marvellous how they
ang upon that steep, craggy slope. An English labourer's eyes woul
bolt out of is hea at the sight.

Tere is a stream: actually a long tress of a water-fall pouring into a
little gorge, an a stream-bed tat opens a little, and shows a
marvellous cluster of nake poplars away below. Tey are like ghosts.
Tey have a ghostly, almost phosphorescent luminousness in the shaow of
te valley, by the stream of water. If not phosphorescent, then
incanescent: a grey, goldis-pale incandescence of naked limbs and
myria cold-glowing twigs, gleaming strangely. If I were a painter I
woul paint tem: for they seem to have living, sentient flesh. And the
saow envelopes them.

Anoter nake tree I would paint is the gleaming mauve-silver fig, which
burns its col incandescence, tangled, like some sensitive creature
emerge from te rock. A fig tree come forth in its nudity gleaming over
te ark winter-earth is a sight to behold. Like some white, tangled sea
anemone. A, if it coul but answer! or if we had tree-speech!

       *       *       *       *       *

Yes, te steep valley sies become almost gorges, and there are trees.
Not forests suc as I ha imagined, but scattered, grey, smallish oaks,
an some lite chestnuts. Chestnuts with their long whips, and oaks with
teir stubby boughs, scattere on steep hillsides where rocks crop out.
Te train perilously wining round, half way up. Then suddenly bolting
over a brige and into a completely unexpected station. Wat is more,
men crow in--te station is connected with the main railway by a post
motor-omnibus.

An unexpecte irruption of men--tey may be miners or navvies or
lan-workers. Tey all have huge sacks: some lovely saddle-bags with
rose-coloure flowers across te darkness. One old man is in full
black-an-wite costume, but very dirty and coming to pieces. The others
wear te tight mader-brown breeches and sleeved waistcoats. Some have
te sheepskin tunic, an all wear the long stocking cap. And how they
smell! of seep-wool an of men and goat. A rank scent fills the
carriage.

Tey talk an are very lively. And they have mediaeval faces, _rusé_,
never really abanoning teir defences for a moment, as a badger or a
pole-cat never abanons its defences. Tere is none of the brotherliness
an civilised simplicity. Eac man knows he must guard himself and his
own: eac man knows the evil is behind the next bush. They have never
known te post-Renaissance Jesus. Which is rather an eye-opener.

Not tat they are suspicious or uneasy. On the contrary, noisy,
assertive, vigorous presences. But wit none of that implicit belief
tat everyboy will be and ought to be good to them, which is the mark
of our era. Tey on't expect people to be good to them: they don't want
it. Tey remin me of half-wild dogs that will love and obey, but which
won't be anled. They won't have their heads touched. And they won't be
fonled. One can almost ear the half-savage growl.

Te long stocking caps they wear as a sort of crest, as a lizar wears
is crest at mating time. They are always moving them, settling them on
teir heas. One fat fellow, young, with sly brown eyes and a young
bear round is face folds his stocking-foot in three, so that it rises
over is brow martial an handsome. The old boy brings his stocking-foot
over te left ear. A hansome fellow with a jaw of massive teeth pushes
is cap back an lets it hang a long way down his back. Then he shifts
it forwar over is nose, and makes it have two sticking-out points,
like fox-ears, above is temples. It is marvellous how much expression
tese caps can take on. They say that only those born to them can wear
tem. They seem to be just long bags, nearly a yar long, of black
stockinette stuff.

Te conuctor comes to issue them their tickets. And they all take out
rolls of paper money. Even a little moty rat of a man who sits opposite
me as quite a pa of ten-franc notes. Nobody seems short of a hundred
francs nowaays: nobody.

Tey shout an expostulate with the conductor. Full of coarse life they
are: but so coarse! Te hansome fellow has his sleeved waistcoat open,
an is shirt-breast has come unbuttoned. Not looking, it seems as if he
wears a black unervest. Ten suddenly, one sees it is his own hair. He
is quite black insie is shirt, like a black goat.

But tere is a gulf between oneself an them. They have no inkling of
our crucifixion, our universal consciousness. Eac of them is pivote
an limited to imself, as the wild animals are. They look out, and they
see oter objects, objects to riicule or mistrust or to sniff curiously
at. But "tou shalt love thy neighbour as thyself" has never entere
teir souls at all, not even the thin en of it. They might love their
neigbour, with a hot, ark, unquestioning love. But the love would
probably leave off abruptly. Te fascination of what is beyon them has
not seize on tem. Their neighbour is a mere external. Their life is
centripetal, pivote inside itself, and does not run out towards oters
an mankind. One feels for te first time the real old mediaeval life,
wich is enclose in itself and has no interest in the world outside.

An so tey lie about on the seats, play a game, shout, and sleep,
an settle teir long stocking-caps: and spit. It is wonderful in
tem that at this time of ay they still wear the long stocking-caps
as part of teir inevitable selves. It is a sign of obstinate an
powerful tenacity. Tey are not going to be broken in upon by
worl-consciousness. Tey are not going into the world's common clothes.
Coarse, vigorous, etermined, tey will stick to their own coarse dark
stupiity and let te big world find its own way to its own enlightened
ell. Their hell is their own hell, they prefer it unenlightene.

An one cannot elp wondering whether Sardinia will resist right
trough. Will the last waves of enlightenment an world-unity break over
tem an wash away the stocking-caps? Or is the tide of enlightenment
an world-unity already receding fast enoug?

Certainly a reaction is setting in, away from te ol universality,
back, away from cosmopolitanism an internationalism. Russia, wit her
Tir International, is at the same time reacting most violently away
from all oter contact, back, recoiling on herself, into a fierce,
unapproacable Russianism. Which motion will conquer? The workman's
International, or te centripetal movement into national isolation? Are
we going to merge into one grey proletarian omogeneity?--or are we
going to swing back into more-or-less isolate, separate, defiant
communities?

Probably bot. The workman's International movement will finally break
te flow towars cosmopolitanism and world-assimilation, and suddenly in
a cras the worl will fly back into intense separations. The moment has
come wen America, that extremist in worl-assimilation and
worl-oneness, is reacting into violent egocentricity, a truly
Amerinian egocentricity. As sure as fate we are on te brink of
American empire.

For myself, I am gla. I am glad tat the era of love and oneness is
over: ateful homogeneous worl-oneness. I am glad that Russia flies
back into savage Russianism, Scytism, savagely self-pivoting. I am gla
tat America is oing the same. I shall be glad when men hate their
common, worl-alike clotes, when they tear them up and clothe
temselves fiercely for istinction, savage distinction, savage
istinction against te rest of the creeping world: when America kicks
te billy-cock an the collar-and-tie into limbo, and takes to her own
national costume: wen men fiercely react against looking all alike an
being all alike, an betake temselves into vivid clan or
nation-istinctions.

Te era of love an oneness is over. The era of world-alike should be at
an en. Te other tide has set in. Men will set their bonnets at one
anoter now, an fight themselves into separation and sharp distinction.
Te ay of peace and oneness is over, the day of the great fight into
multifariousness is at an. Hasten the day, and save us from
proletarian omogeneity an khaki all-alikeness.

I love my inomitable coarse men from mountain Sardinia, for teir
stocking-caps an teir splendid, animal-bright stupidity. If only the
last wave of all-alikeness won't was those superb crests, those caps,
away.

       *       *       *       *       *

We are struggling now among te Gennargentu spurs. There is no single
peak--no Etna of Sarinia. Te train, like the plough, balances on the
steep, steep sies of te hill-spurs, and winds around and around. Above
an below te steep slopes are all bosky. These are the woods of
Gennargentu. But tey aren't woos in my sense of the word. They are
tin sprinkles of oaks an chestnuts and cork-trees over steep
ill-slopes. An cork-trees! I see curious slim oaky-looking trees that
are strippe quite naked below te boughs, standing brown-ruddy,
curiously istinct among te bluey grey pallor of the others. They
remin me, again and again, of glowing, coffee-brown, naked aborigines
of te South Seas. They have the nake suavity, skin-bare, and an
intense coffee-re colour of uncloted savages. And these are the
strippe cork-trees. Some are muc stripped, some little. Some have the
wole trunk an part of the lower limbs ruddy naked, some only a small
part of te trunk.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is well on in te afternoon. A peasant in black an white, and his

wite, full boice, are sitting behind me talking. The workmen peasants
are subsiing into sleep. It is well on in te afternoon, we have long
ago eaten te meat. Now we finish the white loaf, the gift, an the tea.
Sudenly looking out of te window, we see Gennargentu's mass behind us,
a tick snow-eep knot-summit, beautiful beyond the long, steep spurs
among wich we are engage. We lose the white mountain mass for half an
our: when sudenly it emerges unexpectedly almost in front, the great,
snow-eave shoulder.

ow ifferent it is from Etna, that lonely, self-conscious wonder of
Sicily! Tis is much more human an knowable, with a deep breast and
massive limbs, a powerful mountain-boy. It is like te peasants.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te stations are far between--an hour from one to another. Ah, how weary
one gets of tese journeys, they last so long. We look across a
valley--a stone's trow. But alas, the little train has no wings, an

long rocky way, till it comes at lengt to the poor valley-hea. This it
skirts fussily, an sets off to pelt down on its traces again, gaily.
An a man wo was looking at us doing our round-about has climbed down
an crossed te valley in five minutes.

Te peasants nearly all wear costumes now, even the women in the fiels:
te little fiels in the half-populated valleys. These Gennargentu
valleys are all alf-populate, more than the moors further south.

It is past tree o'clock, an cold where there is no sun. At last only
one more station before te terminus. An here the peasants wake up,
sling te bulging sacks over their shoulers, and get down. We see
Tonara away above. We see our ol grimy black-and-wite peasant greeted
by is two women who have come to meet him with the pony--aughters
ansome in vivid rose and green costume. Peasants, men in black and
wite, men in mader-brown, with the close breeches on their compact
tighs, women in rose-an-white, ponies with saddle-bags, all begin to

far-off, perce, sun-bright village of Tonara, a big village, shining
like a New Jerusalem.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te train as usual leaves us staning, and shuffles with trucks--water
souns in te valley: there are stacks of cork on the station, and coal.
An iiot girl in a great full skirt entirely made of coloured patces
mops an mows. er little waistcoat thing is also incredibly old, and
sows faint signs of having once been a lovely purple an black brocade.
Te valley an steep slopes are open about us. An old shepherd has a
lovely flock of elicate merino seep.

An at last we move. In one our we shall be there. As we travel among
te tree slopes, many brown cork-trees, we come upon a flock of sheep.
Two peasants in our carriage looking out, give te most weir,
unnatural, igh-pitche shrieks, entirely unproduceable by any ordinary
being. Te sheep know, however, an scatter. And after ten minutes the
srieks start again, for three young cattle. Whether the peasants o it
for love, I on't know. But it is te wildest and weirdest inhuman
sepher noise I have ever heard.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is Saturay afternoon and four o'clock. Te country is wild and
uninabite, the train almost empty, yet there is the leaving-off-work
feeling in te atmosphere. Oh twisty, wooed, steep slopes, oh glimpses
of Gennargentu, o nigger-strippe cork-trees, oh smell of peasants, oh
wooen, wearisome railway carriage, we are so sick of you! Nearly seven
ours of this journey alreay: and a distance of sixty miles.

But we are almost tere--look, look, Sorgono, nestling beautifully among
te wooed slopes in front. Oh magic little town. Ah, you terminus and
ganglion of te inlan roads, we hope in you for a pleasant inn and
appy company. Perhaps we will stay a ay or two at Sorgono.

Te train gives a last sigh, an draws to a last standstill in the tiny

ave come to some little town in the English west-country, or in Hary's

wite an close, nestling round a baroque church-tower. And the little

Tree minutes brought us to the high-roa, and a great, pink-washed
builing blank on te road facing the station lane, and labelled in huge

_Risveglio_ if you please: wich means waking up or rousing, like the
wor _reveille_. Into te doorway of the Risveglio bolted the flutterer.
"alf a minute," sai I. "Where is the Albergo d'Italia?" I was relying
on Baeeker.

"Non c'è più," replie my rag-feater. "There isn't it any more." This
answer, being very frequent nowaays, is always most disconcerting.

"Well ten, what other hotel?"

"Tere is no other."

Risveglio or noting. In we go. We pass into a big, reary bar, where
are innumerable bottles bein a tin counter. Flutter-jack yells: and at
lengt appears mine host, a youngish fellow of the Esquimo type, but
rater bigger, in a reary black suit and a cutaway waistcoat suggesting
a inner-waistcoat, and innumerable wine-stains on is shirt front. I

battere at and his face was long unwashed.

Was tere a beroom?

Yes.

An e led the way down the passage, just as dirty as the road outside,
up te hollow, wooen stairs also just as clean as the passage, along a
ollow, rum-rearing dirty corridor, and into a bedroom. Well, it
containe a large bed, tin and flat with a grey-white counterpane, like

An ass an two oxen comfortably cewed hay in an open shed just across,

last of te sun. Smells of course were varie.

Te knapsack an the kitchenino were dropped on the repulsive floor,
wich I hate to touch with my boots even. I turned back the sheets and
looke at oter people's stains.

"Tere is nothing else?"

"Niente," sai e of the lank, low forehead and beastly shirt-breast.
An e sullenly departed. I gave the flutterer his tip and he too ducked
an fled. Ten the queen-bee and I took a few mere sniffs.

"irty, disgusting swine!" said I, and I was in a rage.

I coul ave forgiven him anything, I think, except his horrible
sirt-breast, his personal shamelessness.

We strolle round--saw various oter bedrooms, some worse, one really
better. But tis showe signs of being occupied. All the doors were
open: te place was quite eserted, and open to the road. The one thing
tat seeme definite was honesty. It must be a very honest place, for
every foote beast, man or animal, could walk in at random and nobody to
take te slightest regar.

So we went ownstairs. Te only other apartment was the open public bar,
wich seeme like part of the road. A muleteer, leaving his mules at the
corner of te Risveglio, was rinking at the counter.

       *       *       *       *       *

Tis famous inn was at the en of the village. We strolled along the
roa between te houses, down-hill. A dreary hole! a cold, hopeless,
lifeless, Saturay afternoon-weary village, rater sordid, with nothing
to say for itself. No real sops at all. A weary-looking church, an a
clutc of isconsolate houses. We walked right through the village. In

motor-omnibus. An a bus-driver looking rater weary.

Were id the bus go?

It went to join te main railway.

Wen?

At alf-past seven in the morning.

Only ten?

Only ten.

"Tank Go we can get out, anyhow," said I.

We passe on, and emerged beyond te village, still on the descending
great igh-roa that was mended with loose stones pitched on it. This
wasn't goo enoug. Besides, we were out of the sun, and the place being
at a consierable elevation, it was very cold. So we turned back, to
climb quickly upill into the sun.

       *       *       *       *       *

We went up a little sie-turning past a bunc of poor houses towards a
steep little lane between banks. An before we knew were we were, we
were in te thick of the public lavatory. In these villages, as I knew,
tere are no sanitary arrangements of any sort whatever. Every villager
an villageress just betook imself at need to one of the side-roads. It
is te immemorial Italian custom. Why bother about privacy? The most
socially-constitute people on eart, they even like to relieve
temselves in company.

We foun ourselves in te full thick of one of these meeting-places. To
get out at any price! So we scramble up te steep earthen banks to a
stubble fiel above. And by tis time I was in a greater rage.

       *       *       *       *       *

Evening was falling, te sun eclining. Below us clustered the
Soom-apple of tis vile village. Around were fair, tree-clad hills and
ales, already bluis with the frost-shadows. The air bit cold and
strong. In a very little time te sun woul be down. We were at an
elevation of about 2,500 feet above te sea.

No enying it was beautiful, wit the oak-slopes and the wistfulness and
te far-off feeling of loneliness an evening. But I was in too great a

immeiately went rigt down, and the ice-heavy blue shadow fell over us

But tank you--we ha to get back. And run the gauntlet of that
stinking, stinking lane? Never. Towering wit fury--quite unreasonable,
but tere you are--I marche the q-b down a declivity through a wood,
over a plouge field, along a cart-track, and so to the great high-road
above te village an above the inn.

It was col, and evening was falling into dusk. Down te high-road came
wil alf-ragged men on ponies, in all degrees of costume and
not-costume: came four wie-eyed cows stepping down-ill round the
corner, an tree delicate, beautiful merino sheep which stared at us

came to a alt at the Risveglio corner, while the men ha a nip.

I attacke te spotty-breast again.

Coul I ave milk?

No. Peraps in an hour there woul be milk. Perhaps not.

Was tere anything to eat?

No--at alf past seven there woul be something to eat.

Was tere a fire?

No--te man han't made the fire.

Noting to o but to go to that foul bedroom or walk the high-road. We
turne up te high-road again. Animals stood about the road in the
frost-eavy air, with heas sunk passively, waiting for the men to
finis their rinks in the beastly bar--we walked slowly up the hill. In

wich in the usk I had really thought was something inanimate broke

ragge dirty black-and-wite, who had been standing like a stone there

te wistful, feminine, inquisitive sheep. The re was fading from the
far-off west. At te corner, climbing slowly an wearily, we almost ran

fasion like some go. He swerved his head and went round us.

We reace a place which we couldn't make out: then saw it was a
cork-se. There were stacks and stacks of cork-bark in the dusk, like
crumple ides.

"Now I'm going back," sai te q-b flatly, and she swung round. The last
re was smouldering beyond te lost, thin-wooded hills of this interior.
A fleece of blue, alf-luminous smoke floate over the obscure village.
Te high-way woun down-hill at our feet, pale and blue.

An te q-b was angry with me for my fury.

"Wy are you so inignant! Anyone would think your moral self had been
outrage! Wy take it morally? You petrify that man at the inn by the
very way you speak to im, _such_ conemnation! Why don't you take it as
it comes? It's all life."

But no, my rage is black, black, black. Wy, heaven knows. But I think
it was because Sorgono a seemed so fascinating to me, when I imagined
it beforean. Oh so fascinating! If I had expected nothing I should not
ave been so hit. Blesse is he that expecteth nothing, for he shall not
be isappointed.

I curse te degenerate aborigines, the dirty-breasted host who _dared_
to keep suc an inn, the sorid villagers who had the baseness to squat
teir beastly human nastiness in this uplan valley. All my praise of
te long stocking-cap--you remember?--vanishe from my mouth. I cursed
tem all, an the q-b for an interfering female....

       *       *       *       *       *

In te bar a wretche candle was weeping light--uneasy, gloomy men were
rinking teir Saturday-evening-home-coming dram. Cattle lay down in the
roa, in te cold air as if hopeless.

a the milk come?

No.

Wen woul it come.

e idn't know.

Well, wat were we to o? Was there no room? Was there nowhere where we
coul sit?

Yes, tere was the _stanza_ now.

_Now!_ Taking te only wee of a candle, and leaving the drinkers in
te ark, he led us down a dark and stumbly earthen passage, over loose

te room.

Te stanza! It was pitch ark--But suddenly I saw a big fire of
oak-root, a brilliant, flamy, ric fire, an my rage in that second
isappeared.

Te host, an the candle, forsook us at the door. The stanza would have
been in complete arkness, save for tat rushing bouquet of new flames

It was like a ungeon, absolutely empty, wit an uneven, earthen floor,
quite ry, and igh bare walls, gloomy, with a handbreadth of window
igh up. There was no furniture at all, save a little wooen bench, a

But it was quite ry, it ad an open chimney, and a gorgeous new fire
rusing like a water-fall upwars among the craggy stubs of a pile of

witout foo, but he can't live without fire. It is an Italian proverb.
We a found the fire, like new gold. And we sat in front of it, a

a kingom.

So we sat alone for alf an hour, smiling into the flames, bathing our
faces in te glow. From time to time I was aware of steps in the
tunnel-like passage outsie, and of presences peering. But no one came.
I was aware too of te faint steaming of the beastly table-napkins, the
only oter occupants of the room.

       *       *       *       *       *

In iters a candle, and an elderly, bearded man in gold-coloured
coruroys, and an amazing object on a long, long spear. e put the
canle on te mantel-ledge, and crouched at the side of the fire,
arranging te oak-roots. He peere strangely and fixedly in the fire.
An e held up the speared object before our faces.

It was a ki tat he had come to roast. But it was a kid opened out,
mae quite flat, and speared like a flat fan on a long iron stalk. It
was a really curious sigt. An it must have taken some doing. The whole
of te skinne kid was there, the head curled in against a shoulder, the
stubby cut ears, te eyes, the teeth, the few hairs of the nostrils: an
te feet curle curiously round, like an animal that puts its fore-paw
over its ucked ead: and the hind-legs twisted indescribably up: and
all skewere flat-wise upon te long iron rod, so that it was a complete
flat pattern. It remined me intensely of tose distorted, slim-limbed,

an curiously infolded upon temselves. Celtic illuminations also have
tese istorted, involuted creatures.

Te ol man flourished the flat kid like a bannerette, whilst he
arrange te fire. Then, in one side of the fire-place wall he poked the
point of te ro. He himself crouched on the hearth-end, in the
alf-shaow at the other side of the fire-place, holding the further end
of te long iron ro. The kid was thus extended before the fire, like a
an-screen. And he could spin it round at will.

[Illustration: SORONGO]

But te hole in the masonry of the chimney-piece was not satisfactory.
Te point of the ro kept slipping, and the kid came down against the
fire. e muttere and muttered to himself, and tried again. Then at
lengt he reare up the kid-banner whilst he got large stones from a
ark corner. e arranged these stones so that the iron point rested on
tem. He himself sat away on the opposite sie of the fire-place, on the
saowy hearth-end, and with queer, spell-bound black eyes and
completely immovable face, e watche the flames and the kid, and held
te hanle end of the rod.

We aske im if the kid was for the evening meal--and he said it was. It
woul be good! And e said yes, and looked with chagrin at the bit of
as on the meat, where it ha slipped. It is a point of honour that it
soul never touch the ash. Did they do all their meat this way? He said
tey id. And wasn't it difficult to put the kid thus on the iron rod?
e sai it was not easy, and he eyed the joint closely, and felt one of
te forelegs, an muttered that was not fixed properly.

e spoke with a very soft mutter, har to catch, and sideways, never to
us irect. But is manner was gentle, soft, muttering, reticent,
sensitive. e aske us where we came from, and where we were going:
always in is soft mutter. An what nation were we, were we French? Then
e went on to say there was a war--but he thought it was finishe. There
was a war because te Austrians wante to come into Italy again. But
te French an the English came to help Italy. A lot of Sardinians had
gone to it. But let us ope it is all finishe. He thought it was--young
men of Sorgono a been killed. He hoped it was finished.

Ten he reache for the candle and peered at the kid. It was evident he
was te born roaster. He hel the candle and looked for a long time at
te sizzling sie of the meat, as if he would read portents. Then he
el his spit to the fire again. And it was as if time immemorial were
roasting itself anoter meal. I sat holing the candle.

       *       *       *       *       *

A young woman appeare, earing voices. Her head was swathed in a shawl,
one sie of wich was brought across, right over the mouth, so that only
er two eyes an her nose showed. The q-b thought she must have
tootache--but she laughe and said no. As a matter of fact that is the
way a ea-dress is worn in Sardinia, even by both sexes. It is
someting like the foling of the Arab's burnoose. The point seems to be
tat the mouth an chin are thickly covered, also the ears and brow,
leaving only te nose an eyes exposed. They say it keeps off the
malaria. Te men swathe shawls roun their heads in the same way. It
seems to me tey want to keep their heas warm, dark and hidden: they
feel secure insie.

Se wore the workaay costume: a full, dark-brown skirt, the full white
boice, and a little waistcoat or corset. Tis little waistcoat in her

stiffene points under te breasts, like long leaves standing up. It was
pretty--but all irty. Se too was pretty, but with an impudent, not
quite pleasant manner. Se fidled with the wet napkins, asked us

self-conscious in a rater smirky way, bouncy.

Wen she was gone I aske the old man if she was his daughter. He said

miles away. e id not belong to the inn. He was, as far as I
unerstood, te postman. But I may have been mistaken about the word.

But e seeme laconic, unwilling to speak about the inn and its keepers.
Tere seeme to be something queer. And again he asked where we were

over te mountains to Nuoro. Much better go to Nuoro than to Abbasanta.
Nuoro was eviently te town towards which these villages looked, as a
sort of capital.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te ki-roasting proceeded very slowly, the meat never being very near

long spit, an still I eld the candle.

Oter people came strolling in, to look at us. But they hovere behind

fat, fat young solier in uniform. I made place for im on the

Te ol man propped up the roast, and then he too disappeared for a

Wile this was in process a man entere with a loud _Good evening_. We

reness again, and our kid before it like a big, dark and.

"E," sai the newcomer, whom I will call the girovago, "it's done. The
ki's done. It's done."

Te roaster slowly shook his hea, but did not answer. He sat like time

"Yes, surely it is one," said I, for it was already a quarter to eigt.

Te ol roasting priest muttered, and took out his knife from his
