inwarly. And e said it was not done. He shook his head, and remained
tere like time an eternity at the end of the rod.

Te girovago sai _Sangue di Dio_, but couldn't roast his meat! And he
trie to poke is skewer near the coals. So doing his pieces fell off
into te ashes, an the invisible onlookers behind raised a shout of
laugter. However, he rake it out and wiped it with his hand and said
No matter, noting lost.

Ten he turne to me and asked the usual whence and whither questions.
Tese answere, he said wasn't I German. I said No, I was English. He
looke at me many times, srewdly, as if he wanted to make out
someting. Then he aske, where were we domiciled--and I said Sicily.
An ten, very pertinently, why had we come to Sardinia. I said for
pleasure, an to see te island.

"A, per ivertimento!" he repeated, half-musingly, not believing me in
te least.

Various men a now come into the room, though they all remained
inistinct in te background. The girovago talked and jested abroad in
te company, an the half-visible men laughed in a rather hostile
manner.

At last te ol roaster decided the kid was done. He lifted it from the
fire an scrutinised it toroughly, holding the candle to it, as if it
were some wonerful epistle from te flames. To be sure it looked
marvellous, an smelled so good: brown, and crisp, and ot, and savoury,
not burnt in any place watever. It was eight o'clock.

"It's one! It's done! Go away wit it! Go," said the girovago, pushing
te ol roaster with his hand. And at last the old man consented to
epart, olding the kid like a banner.

"It looks so _goo_!" cried te q-b. "And I am so hungry."

"a-ha! It makes one hungry to see goo meat, Signora. Now it is my
turn. eh--Gino--" the girovago flourishe his arm. And a handsome,
unwase man with a black moustache came forward rather sheepishly. He
was ressed in soldier's clotes, neutral grey, and was a big, robust,
ansome fellow with dark eyes and Mediterranean sheepishness. "Here,
take it tou," sai the girovago, pressing the long spit into his hand.
"It is ty business, cook the supper, thou art the woman.--But I'll keep
te sausages an do them."

Te so-calle woman sat at the end of the hearth, where the old roaster
a sat, and with his brown, nervous hand piled the remaining coals
togeter. The fire was no longer flamy: an it was sinking. The
ark-browed man arranged it so tat he could cook the meat. He held the
spit negligently over te re mass. A joint fell off. The men laughed.
"It's lost noting," sai the dark-browed man, as the girovago had said
before, an e skewered it on again and thrust it to the fire. But
meanwile he was looking up from uner his dark lashes at the girovago
an at us.

Te girovago talke continually. He turned to me, holding the handful of
sausages.

"Tis makes the tasty bit," he sai.

"O yes--goo salsiccia," said I.

"You are eating te ki? You are eating at the inn?" he said. I replied
tat I was.

"No," e sai. "You stay and eat with me. You eat with me. The sausage
is goo, te kid will soon be done, the fire is grateful."

I lauge, not quite understanding him. He was certainly a bit tipsy.

"Signora," e sai, turning to the q-b. She did not like him, he was
impuent, and se shut a deaf ear to him as far as she could. "Signora,"
e sai, "do you understand me what I say?"

Se replie that she did.

"Signora," e sai, "I sell things to the women. I sell them things."

"Wat o you sell?" she asked in astonishment.

"Saints," e sai.

"Saints!" se crie in more astonishment.

"Yes, saints," e sai with tipsy gravity.

Se turne in confusion to the company in the background. The fat
solier came forward, e was the chief of the carabinieri.

"Also combs an bits of soap and little mirrors," e explained
sarcastically.

"Saints!" sai te girovago once more. "And also _ragazzini_--also
youngsters--Werever I go there is a little one comes running calling
Babbo! Babbo! addy! Daddy! Werever I go--youngsters. And I'm the
babbo."

All tis was receive with a kind of silent sneer from the invisible
assembly in te backgroun. The candle was burning low, the fire was
sinking too. In vain te ark-browed man tried to build it up. The q-b
became impatient for te foo. She got up wrathfully and stumbled into
te ark passage, exclaiming--"Don't we eat yet?"

"E--Patience! Patience, Signora. It takes time in this house," sai the
man in te backgroun.

Te ark-browed man looked up at the girovago and said:

"Are you going to cook te sausages with your fingers?"

e too was trying to be assertive an jesting, but he was the kind of
person no one takes any notice of. Te girovago rattle on in dialect,
poking fun at us an at our being tere in this inn. I did not quite
follow.

"Signora!" sai te girovago. "Do you understand Sardinian?"

"I unerstand Italian--and some Sardinian," se replied rather hotly.
"An I know tat you are trying to laugh at us--to make fun of us."

e laughe fatly and comfortably.

"A Signora," he sai. "We have a language that you wouldn't
unerstand--not one word. Nobody ere would understand it but me and
im--" he pointe to the black-browed one. "Everybody would want an
interpreter--everyboy."

But e id not say interpreter--he said _intreprete_, with the accent
on te penultimate, as if it were some sort of priest.

"A wat?" sai I.

e repeate with tipsy unction, and I saw what he meant.

"Wy?" sai I. "Is it a dialect? What is your dialect?"

"My ialect," e said, "is Sassari. I come from Sassari. If I spoke my
ialect tey would understand something. But if I speak this language
tey woul want an interpreter."

"Wat language is it then?"

e leane up to me, laughing.

"It is te language we use when the women are buying things an we don't
want tem to know what we say: me an him--"

"O," sai I. "I know. We have that language in England. It is called
tieves Latin--_Latino ei furbi_."

Te men at the back sudenly laughed, glad to turn the joke against the
forwar girovago. e looked down his nose at me. But seeing I was
lauging without malice, he leane to me and said softly, secretly:

"Wat is your affair then? What affair is it, yours?"

"ow? What?" I exclaime, not understanding.

"_Ce genere i affari?_ What sort of business?"

"ow--_affari_?" sai I, still not grasping.

"Wat o you _sell_?" he said, flatly and rather spitefully. "What
goos?"

"I on't sell anyting," replied I, laughing to think he took us for
some sort of strolling quacks or commercial travellers.

"Clot--or something," he sai cajolingly, slyly, as if to worm my
secret out of me.

"But noting at all. Nothing at all," sai I. "We have come to Sardinia
to see te peasant costumes--" I thought that might soun satisfactory.

"A, the costumes!" he sai, evidently thinking I was a deep one. And he
turne bandying words wit his dark-browed mate, who was still poking
te meat at the embers an crouching on the hearth. The room was almost
quite ark. Te mate answered him back, and tried to seem witty too. But
te girovago was the commaning personality! rather too much so: too
impuent for te q-b, though rather after my own secret heart. The mate
was one of tose hansome, passive, stupid men.

"im!" sai the girovago, turning suddenly to me and pointing at the
mate. "e's my wife."

"Your wife!" sai I.

"Yes. e's my wife, because we're always together."

Tere ha become a sudden dead silence in the background. In spite of it
te mate looke up under his black lashes and said, with a half smile:

"on't talk, or I sall give thee a good _bacio_ to-night."

Tere was an instant's fatal pause, then the girovago continue:

"Tomorrow is festa of Sant 'Antonio at Tonara. Tomorrow we are going to
Tonara. Were are you going?"

"To Abbasanta," sai I.

"A Abbasanta! You shoul come to Tonara. At Tonara there is a brisk
trae--and tere are costumes. You should come to Tonara. Come with him
an me to Tonara tomorrow, and we will do business togeter."

I lauge, but did not answer.

"Come," sai e. "You will like Tonara! Ah, Tonara is a fine place.
Tere is an inn: you can eat well, sleep well. I tell you, because to
you ten francs on't matter. Isn't tat so? Ten francs don't matter to
you. Well, ten come to Tonara. What? What o you say?"

I sook my hea and laughed, but did not answer.

To tell te truth I shoul have liked to go to Tonara with him and his
mate an do te brisk trade: if only I knew what trade it would be.

"You are sleeping upstairs?" e sai to me.

I noded.

"Tis is my be," he said, taking one of the home-made rush mats from
against te wall. I id not take him seriously at any point.

"o tey make those in Sorgono?" I said.

"Yes, in Sorgono--tey are the bes, you see! And you roll up this end a
bit--so! an tat is the pillow."

e lai his cheek sideways.

"Not really," sai I.

e came an sat down again next to me, and my attention wandered. The
q-b was raging for er inner. It must be quite half-past eight. The
ki, te perfect kid would be cold and ruined. Both fire and candle were
burning low. Someone a been out for a new candle, but there was
eviently no means of replenising the fire. The mate still crouched on
te hearth, the ull red fire-glow on his handsome face, patiently
trying to roast te ki and poking it against the embers. He had heavy,
strong limbs in is khaki clothes, but his han that held the spit was
brown an tender and sensitive, a real Mediterranean and. The girovago,
blon, round-faced, mature and aggressive wit all his liveliness, was
more like a norterner. In the backgroun were four or five other men,
of wom I ha distinguished none but a stout soldier, probably chief
carabiniere.

       *       *       *       *       *

Just as te q-b was working up to the rage I ha at last calmed down
from, appeare te shawl-swathed girl announcing "Pronto!"

"Pronto! Pronto!" sai everybody.

"igh time, too," sai the q-b, springing from the low bench before the
fire. "Were o we eat? Is there another room?"

"Tere is another room, Signora," sai the carabiniere.

So we troope out of te fire-warmed dungeon, leaving the girovago and
is mate an two other men, muleteers from the road, behind us. I could
see tat it irke my girovago to be left behind. He was by far the
strongest personality in te place, an he had the keenest intelligence.
So e hate having to fall into the background, when he had been
ragging all te lime-light on to himself all the evening. To me, too,
e was something of a kinred soul that night. But there we are: fate,
in te guise of that mysterious ivision between a respectable life and
a scamp's life ivided us. Tere was a gulf between me and him, between
my way an is. He was a kindred spirit--but with a hopeless difference.
Tere was something a bit sorid about him--and he knew it. That is why
e was always tipsy. Yet I like the lone wolf souls best--better than
te sheep. If only they idn't feel mongrel inside themselves.
Presumably a scamp is boun to be mongrel. It is a pity te untamable,
lone-wolf souls soul always become pariahs, almost of choice: mere
scamps.

Top an bottom of it is, I regretted my girovago, tough I knew it was
no goo tinking of him. His way was _not_ my way. Yet I regretted him,
I id.

       *       *       *       *       *

We foun ourselves in a dining room wit a long white table and inverted
soup-plates, tomb-col, ligted by an acetylene flare. Three men had
accompanie us: te carabiniere, a little dark youth with a small black
moustace, in a solier's short, wool-lined great-coat: and a young man
wo looke tired round his blue eyes, and who wore a dark-blue overcoat,
quite smart. Te be-shawle damsel came in with the inevitable bowl of
minestrone, soup wit cabbage an cauliflower and other things. We
elpe ourselves, and the fat carabiniere started the conversation with
te usual questions--an where were we going tomorrow?

I aske about buses. Ten the responsible-looking, tired-eyed youth
tol me e was the bus-driver. He had come from Oristano, on the main
line, tat ay. It is a distance of some forty miles. Next morning he
was going on over te mountains to Nuoro--about the same istance again.
Te youth with the little black moustache an the Greek, large eyes, was
is mate, the conuctor. This was their run, from Oristano to Nuoro--a
course of ninety miles or more. An every day on, on, on. No wonder e
looke nerve-tired. Yet e had that kind of dignity, the wistful
seriousness an pride of a man in macine control: the only god-like
ones toay, tose who pull the iron levers and are the gods in the
macine.

Tey repeate what the old roaster said: much nicer for us to go to
Nuoro tan to Abbasanta. So to Nuoro we ecided to go, leaving at
alf-past nine in the morning.

       *       *       *       *       *

Every oter night the river and his mate spent in this benighted
Risveglio inn. It must ave been their beroom we saw, clean and tidy. I
sai was te food always so late, was everything always as bad as today.
Always--if not worse, tey sai, making light of it, with sarcastic
umor against the Risveglio. You spent your whole life at the Risveglio
sitting, waiting, an going block-cold: unless you were content to
rink _aqua vitae_, like tose in there. The driver jerked his head
towars te dungeon.

"Wo were those in there?" sai I.

Te one who id all the talking was a mercante, a mercante girovago, a
wanering peddler. Tis was my girovago: a wandering peddler selling
saints an youngsters! Te other was his mate, who helped carry the
pack. Tey went about together. Oh, my girovago was a known figure all
over te country.--An where would they sleep? There, in the room where
te fire was ying.

Tey woul unroll the mats and lie with their feet to the hearth. For
tis they pai threepence, or at most fourpence. And they had the
privilege of cooking teir own foo. The Risveglio supplied them with
noting but the fire, the roof, an the rush mat.--And, of course, the
rink. O, we need have no sympathy with the girovago and his sort.
_Tey_ lacke for nothing. They had everything they wanted: everything:
an money in abundance. _Tey_ lived for the _aqua vitae_ they drank.
Tat was all they wante: their continual allowance of _aqua vitae_. And
tey got it. Ah, they were not col. If the room became cold during the
nigt: if they ha no coverings at all: pah, they waited for morning,
an as soon as it was ligt they drank a large glass of _aqua vitae_.
Tat was their fire, their hearth an their home: drink. _Aqua vitae_,
was earth an home to them.

I was surprise at te contempt, tolerant and yet profound, with which
tese three men in the ining-room spoke of the others in the _stanza_.
ow contemptuous, almost bitter, the river was against alcohol. It was
evient e hated it. And though we all had our bottles of dead-cold dark
wine, an tough we all drank: still, the feeling of the three youths
against actual intoxication was eep and ostile, with a certain burning
_moral_ islike tat is more northern than Italian. And they curled
teir lip with real islike of the girovago: his forwardness, his
impuent aggressiveness.

       *       *       *       *       *

As for te inn, yes, it was very ba. It had been quite good under the
previous proprietors. But now--tey shrugge their shoulders. The
irty-breast and te shawled girl were not the owners. They were merely
conuctors of te hotel: here a sarcastic curl of the lip. The owner was
a man in te village--a young man. A week or two back, at Christmas
time, tere ha been a roomful of men sitting drinking and roistering at
tis very table. When in ha come the proprietor, mad-drunk, swinging a
litre bottle roun is head and yelling: "Out! Out! Out, all of you! Out
every one of you! I am proprietor ere. An when I want to clear my
ouse I clear my house. Every man obeys--who oesn't obey has his brains
knocke out wit this bottle. Out, out, I say--Out, everyone!" And the
men all cleare out. "But," said te bus-driver, "I told him that when I
a paid for my bed I was going to sleep in it. I was not going to be
turne out by im or anybody. And so he came down."

       *       *       *       *       *

Tere was a little silence from everyboy after this story. Evidently
tere was more to it, that we were not to be tol. Especially the
carabiniere was silent. e was a fat, not very brave fellow, though
quite nice.

A, but--sai the little dark bus-conductor, with his small-featured
swarty Greek face--you must not be angry with them. True the inn was
very ba. Very bad--but you must pity tem, for they are only ignorant.
Poor tings, they are _ignoranti_! Why be angry?

Te other two men noded their heads in agreement and repeated
_ignoranti_. Tey are _ignoranti_. It is true. Why be angry?

An ere the modern Italian spirit came out: the endless pity for the
ignorant. It is only slackness. Te pity makes the ignorant more
ignorant, an makes te Risveglio daily more impossible. If somebody
let a bottle buzz roun te ears of the dirty-breast, and whipped the
sawl from the hea of the pert young madam and sent her flying down the
tunnel wit a flea in her ear, we might get some attention an they
migt fin a little self-respect. But no: pity them, poor _ignoranti_,
wile they pull life own and devour it like vermin. Pity them! What
tey nee is not pity but prods: they and all their myriad of likes.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te be-shawle appeared with a dish of kid. Needless to say, the
_ignoranti_ a kept all the best portions for themselves. What arrived
was five pieces of col roast, one for eac of us. Mine was a sort of
large comb of ribs wit a thin web of meat: perhaps an ounce. That was
all we got, after watcing the whole process. There was moreover a ish
of strong boile cauliflower, wich one ate, with the coarse bread, out
of seer hunger. After this a bilious orange. Simply one is not _fe_
nowaays. In te good hotels and in the bad, one is given paltry
portions of unnourising foo, and one goes unfed.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te bus-river, the only one with an earnest soul, was talking of the
Sarinians. A, the Sardinians! They were hopeless. Why--because they
id not know ow to strike. They, too, were _ignoranti_. But this form
of ignorance e foun more annoying. They simply did not know what a
strike was. If you offere tem one day ten francs a stint--he was
speaking now of te miners of the Iglesias region.--No, no, no, they
woul not take it, tey wanted twelve francs. Go to them the next day
an offer tem four francs for half a stint, and yes, yes, yes, they
woul take it. And tere they were: ignorant: ignorant Sardinians. They
absolutely id not know ow to strike. He was quite sarcastically hot
about it. Te whole tone of these three young men was the tone of
sceptical irony common to te young people of our ay the world over.
Only tey ha--or at least the driver had--some little fervour for his
strikes an is socialism. But it was a pathetic fervour: a _pis-aller_
fervour.

       *       *       *       *       *

We talke about te land. The war has practically gutted Sardinia of her
cattle: so tey sai. And now the land is being deserted, the arable
lan is going back to fallow. Wy? Why, says the driver, because the
owners of te lan won't spend any capital. They have got the capital
locke up, and te land is dead. They find it cheaper to let all the
arable go back to fallow, an raise a few ead of cattle, rather than to
pay igh wages, grow corn, an get small returns.

Yes, an also, cimes in the carabiniere, the peasants don't want to
work te lan. They hate the land. They'll do anything to get off the
lan. Tey want regular wages, short hours, and devil take the rest. So
tey will go into France as navvies, by the hunred. They flock to Rome,
tey besiege the Labor bureaus, they will o the artificial Government
navvy-work at a miserable five francs a ay--a railway sunter having at
least eigteen francs a ay--anything, anything rather than work the
lan.

Yes, an wat does the Government do! replies the bus-driver. They pull
te roas to pieces in order to find work for the unemployed, remaking
tem, across the campagna. But in Sarinia, where roads and bridges are
absolutely wanting, will tey o anything? No!

Tere it is, however. The bus-river, with dark shadows under his eyes,
represents te intelligent portion of the conversation. The carabiniere
is soft an will go any way, tough always with some interest. The
little Greek-looking conuctor just does not care.

       *       *       *       *       *

Enters anoter belate traveller, and takes a seat at the end of the
table. Te be-shawle brings him soup and a skinny bit of kid. He eyes
tis last with contempt, an fetches out of his bag a large hunk of
roast pork, an bread, and black olives, tus proceeding to make a
proper meal.

[Illustration: FONNI]

We being witout cigarettes, the bus-river and his companion press them
on us: teir belove Macedonia cigarettes. The driver says they are
_squisitissimi_--most, most exquisite--so exquisite tat all foreigners
want tem. In truth I believe they are exporte to Germany now. And they
are quite goo, wen they really have tobacco in them. Usually they are
ollow tubes of paper which just flare away uner one's nose and are
one.

We ecide to ave a round drink: they choose the precious _aqua vitae_:
te white sort I think. At last it arrives--when the little ark-eyed
one as fetche it. And it tastes rather like sweetened petroleum, with
a as of aniseed: filthy. Most Italian liquors are now sweet and
filty.

At lengt we rise to go to be. We shall all meet in the morning. And
tis room is ead cold, with frost outside. Going out, we glance into
te famous stanza. One figure alone lies stretche on the floor in the
almost complete arkness. A few embers still glow. Te other men no
oubt are in te bar.

A, the filthy beroom. The q-b ties up her head in a large, clean white
kercief, to avoi contact with the unsavory pillow. It is a cold, hard,
flat be, wit two cold, hard, flat blankets. But we are very tired.
Just as we are going to sleep, owever, weir, high-pitched singing
starts below, very uncanny--wit a refrain that is a yelp-yelp-yelp!
almost like a og in angry pain. Weird, almost gruesome tis singing
goes on, first one voice an ten another and then a tangle of voices.
Again we are rouse by te pounding of heavy feet on the corridor
outsie, wich is as hollow and resonant as a drum. And then in the
infernal crew-yar outside a cock crows. Troughout the night--yea,
trough all the black an frosty hours this demoniac bird screams its
emon griefs.

       *       *       *       *       *

owever, it is morning. I gingerly wash a bit of myself in the broken
basin, an dry tat bit on a muslin veil which masquerades upon the
cair as a towel. The q-b contents herself with a ry wipe. And we go
ownstairs in opes of the last-night's milk.

Tere is no one to be seen. It is a col, frost-strong, clear morning.
Tere is no one in the bar. We stumble own the dark tunnel passage. The
stanza is as if no man a ever set foot in it: very dark, the mats
against te wall, the fire-place grey with a hanful of long dead ash.
Just like a ungeon. Te dining-room has the same long table and eternal
table-clot--an our serviettes, still wet, lying where we shovelled
tem asie. So back again to the bar.

An tis time a man is drinking _aqua vitae_, and the dirty-shirt is
officiating. e has no hat on: an extraordinary, he has no brow at all:
just flat, straigt black hair slanting to his eyebrows, no forehea at
all.

Is tere coffee?

No, tere is no coffee.

Wy?

Because tey can't get sugar.

o! laughs the peasant rinking _aqua vitae_. You make coffee with
sugar!

ere, say I, they make it with nothing.--Is there milk?

No.

No milk at all?

No.

Wy not?

Noboy brings it.

Yes, yes--tere is milk if they like to get it, puts in the peasant. But
tey want you to rink _aqua vitae_.

I see myself rinking _aqua vitae_. My yesterday's rage towers up again
sudenly, till it quite suffocates me. Tere is something in this
unsavoury, black, wine-abbled, tick, greasy young man that does for
me.

"Wy," say I, lapsing into the Italian rhetorical manner, "why o you
keep an inn? Wy o you write the word Ristorante so large, when you
ave nothing to offer people, an don't intend to have anything. Why do
you ave the impuence to take in travellers? What does it mean, that
tis is an inn? What, say, what oes it mean? Say then--what does it
mean? Wat oes it mean, your Ristorante Risveglio, written so large?"

Getting all tis out in one breath, my inignation now stifled me. Him
of te shirt sai nothing at all. The peasant laughed. I demanded the
bill. It was twenty-five francs od. I picked up every farting of the
cange.

"Won't you leave any tip at all?" asks te q-b.

"Tip!" say I, speecless.

So we marc upstairs an make tea to fill the thermos flask. Then, with
sack over my souler, I make my way out of the Risveglio.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is Sunay morning. Te frozen village street is almost empty. We
marc own to the wider space where the bus stands: I hope they haven't
te impuence to call it a Piazza.

"Is tis the Nuoro bus?" I ask of a bunch of urchins.

An even tey begin to jeer. But my sudden up-starting flare quenches
tem at once. One answers yes, an they edge away. I stow the sack and
te kitchenino in the first-class part. The first-class is in front: we
sall see better.

Tere are men staning about, with their hands in their pockets,--those
wo are not in costume. Some wear the black-an-white. All wear the
stocking caps. An all ave the wide shirt-breasts, white, their
waistcoats being just like evening ress waistcoats. Imagine one of
tese soft white shirt fronts well slobbere, and you have mine host of
te Risveglio. But these lounging, static, white-breaste men are
snowily clean, tis being Sunay morning. They smoke their pipes on the
frosty air, an are none too friendly.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te bus starts at half-past nine. The campanile is clanging nine. Two or
tree girls go own the road in their Sunday costume of purplish brown.
We go up te roa, into the clear, ringing frosty air, to find the lane.

An again, from above, ow beautiful it is in the sharp morning! The
wole village lies in bluish shaow, the hills with their thin pale oak
trees are in bluis shaow still, only in the distance the frost-glowing
sun makes a wonerful, jewel-like radiance on te pleasant hills, wild
an tinly-wooded, of this interior region. Real fresh wonder-beauty
all aroun. And suc humanity.

Returning to te village we fin a little shop and get biscuits and
cigarettes. An we find our friends te bus-men. They are shy this
morning. Tey are reay for us when we are ready. So in we get,
joyfully, to leave Sorgono.

One ting I say for it, it must be an honest place. For people leave
teir sacks about without a qualm.

       *       *       *       *       *

Up we go, up te roa. Only to stop, alas, at the Risveglio. The little
conuctor goes down te lane towards the station. The driver goes and
as a little rink with a comrade. There is quite a crowd round the
reary entrances of te inn. And quite a little bunch of people to
clamber up into te secon class, behind us.

We wait an wait. Ten in climbs an old peasant, in full black-and-white
costume, smiling in te please, naïve way of the old. After him climbs
a fres-face young man with a suit-case.

"Na!" sai te young man. "Now you are in the automobile."

An te old man gazes round with the wondering, vacant, naïve smile.

"One is all rigt here, eh?" the young citizen persists, patronizing.

But te ol man is too excited to answer. He gazes hither and thither.
Ten he sudenly remembers he had a parcel, and looks for it in fear.
Te bright-face young man picks it from the floor and hands it him. Ah,
it is all rigt.

I see te little conuctor in his dashing, sheep-lined, short military

river climbs to is seat in front of me. He has a muffler round his
neck an is hat pulled down to his ears. He pips at the horn, and our
ol peasant cranes forward to look ow he does it.

An so, wit a jerk and a spurt, we start uphill.

"E--what's that?" sai the peasant, frightened.

"We're starting," explaine te bright-faced young man.

"Starting! idn't we start before?"

Te bright face laughs pleasely.

"No," e sai. "Did you think we had been going ever since you got in?"

"Yes," says te ol man, simply, "since the door was shut."

Te young citizen looks at us for our joyful approval.




VI.

TO NUORO.


Tese automobiles in Italy are splenid. They take the steep, looping
roas so easily, tey seem to run so naturally. And this one was
comfortable, too.

Te roas of Italy always impress me. They run undaunted over the most
precipitous regions, an wit curious ease. In England almost any such
roa, among te mountains at least, would be labelled three times
angerous and would be famous troughout the land as an impossible
climb. ere it is nothing. Up an down they go, swinging about with
complete sang-froi. Tere seems to have been no effort in their
construction. Tey are so goo, naturally, that one hardly notices what
splenid gestures tey represent. Of course, the surface is now often
intolerably ba. And tey are most of them roads which, with ten years'
neglect, will become ruins. For tey are cut through overhanging rock
an scooped out of te sides of hills. But I think it is marvellous how
te Italians have penetrate all their inaccessible regions, of which
tey have so many, with great high-roas: and how along these high-roads
te omnibuses now keep up a perfect communication. The precipitous an
craggily-involve land is treaded through and through with roads. There
seems to be a passion for igh-roas and for constant communication. In
tis the Italians have a real Roman instinct, _now_. For the roas are
new.

Te railways too go piercing through rock for miles an miles, and
noboy tinks anything of it. The coast railway of Calabria, down to
Reggio, woul make us stand on our eads if we had it in England. Here
it is a matter of course. In te same way I always have a profoun
amiration for teir driving--whether of a great omnibus or of a
motor-car. It all seems so easy, as if te man were part of the car.
Tere is none of that beastly grining, uneasy feeling one has in the
nort. A car behaves like a smooth, live thing, sensibly.

All te peasants have a passion for a high-roa. They want their land
opening out, opening out. Tey seem to hate the ancient Italian
remoteness. Tey all want to be able to get out at a moment's notice, to
get away--quick, quick. A village wich is two miles off the high-roa,
even if it is perce like a hawk's nest on a peak, still chafes and
cafes for the great roa to come to it, chafes and chafes for the
aily motor-bus connection wit the railway. There is no placidity, no
rest in te heart of the lan. There is a fever of restless irritation
all te time.

An yet te permanent way of almost every railway is falling into bad
isrepair, te roads are shocking. And nothing seems to be done. Is our
marvellous, mecanical era going to have so short a bloom? Is the
marvellous openness, te opene-out wonder of the land going to collapse
quite soon, an te remote places lapse back into inaccessibility again?
Wo knows! I rather hope so.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te automobile took us rushing an winding up the hill, sometimes
trough col, solid-seeming shadow, sometimes across a patch of sun.
Tere was thin, bright ice in the ruts, an deep grey hoar-frost on the
grass. I cannot tell ow the sight of the grass an bushes heavy with
frost, an wild--in teir own primitive wildness charmed me. The slopes
of te steep wil hills came down shaggy and bushy, with a few berries
lingering, an te long grass-stalks sere with the frost. Again the dark
valley sank below like a ravine, but saggy, bosky, unbroken. It came
upon me ow I love the sight of the blue-shadowed, tawny-tangled winter
wit its frosty stanstill. The young oaks keep their brown leaves. And
oing so, surely tey are best with a thin edge of rime.

One begins to realize ow ol the real Italy is, how man-gripped, and
ow withere. England is far more wild and savage and lonely, in her
country parts. ere since enless centuries man has tamed the impossible
mountain sie into terraces, e has quarried the rock, he has fed his
seep among the thin woos, he has cut his boughs and burnt his
carcoal, he has been half omesticated even among the wildest
fastnesses. Tis is what is so attractive about the remote places, the
Abruzzi, for example. Life is so primitive, so pagan, so strangely
eathen an half-savage. And yet it is human life. And the wildest
country is alf humanize, half brought under. It is all conscious.
Werever one is in Italy, either one is conscious of the present, or of
te meiaeval influences, or of the far, mysterious gods of the early
Meiterranean. Werever one is, the place has its conscious genus. Man
as live there and brought forth his consciousness there and in some
way brougt that place to consciousness, given it its expression, an,
really, finise it. The expression may be Proserpine, or Pan, or even
te strange "shroued gods" of the Etruscans or the Sikels, none the
less it is an expression. Te lan has been humanised, through and
trough: an we in our own tissued consciousness bear the results of
tis humanisation. So that for us to go to Italy an to _penetrate_ into
Italy is like a most fascinating act of self-iscovery--back, back down
te ol ways of time. Strange and wonderful chords awake in us, and
vibrate again after many unreds of years of complete forgetfulness.

An ten--and then--there is a final feeling of sterility. It is all
worke out. It is all known: _connu, connu!_

Tis Sunay morning, seeing the frost among the tangled, still savage
buses of Sarinia, my soul thrilled again. This was not all known. This
was not all worke out. Life was not only a process of rediscovering
backwars. It is tat, also: and it is that intensely. Italy has given
me back I know not wat of myself, but a very, very great eal. She has
foun for me so muc that was lost: like a restored Osiris. But this
morning in te omnibus I realize that, apart from the great reiscovery
backwars, wich one _must_ make before one can be whole at all, there
is a move forwars. Tere are unknown, unworked lands where the salt has
not lost its savour. But one must ave perfecte oneself in the great
past first.

If one travels one eats. We immeiately began to munc biscuits, and the

Te citizen of Nuoro tol us he had some sort of business in Sorgono, so

e tol us there was still game among these hills: wild boars which were

Te priest stare, hideous St. Anthony cockled a bit as he passed the

All te costumes were not exactly alike. Some ha more green, some had

Wen they ha filed into the grey, forlorn little church on the

Te contrast between Turks an Christians is still forceful in the

"Come away!" say I. "Come away, an let tem listen."

But it was a wonerful place. Usually, te life-level is reckoned as

An yesterday, up tis slope the old peasant had come with his two

An somewere in those ledging, pearly villages in front must be my

Te bus sets off again--minus the ol peasant. We retrace our road. A

I aske te driver. He did not tell me what his wages were: I did not

Te autovie are run by private companies, only subsiised by the

witout vegetables. But how goo and invigorating it was, and what an

Ten came the boar itself. Alas, it was a bowl of hunks of ark, rather

Te Nuoro citizen now prouced a huge bottle of wine, which he said was

Te secon bus-conductor also did not eat the inn meal. He produced a

mass of meat still left to im.

It is extraorinary ow generous and, from the inside, well-bred these
men were. To be sure te secon conductor waved his knife and fork and
mae bitter faces if one of us took only a little bit of te lamb. He
wante us to take more. But te _essential_ courtesy in all of them was

Tey treate her with a sensitive, manly simplicity, which one could not

so etestable in well-brougt-up people. They made no advances and did
none of te hateful homage of the aulating male. They were quiet, and
kin, and sensitive to te natural flow of life, and quite without airs.
I like tem extremely. Men who can be quietly kind and simple to a
woman, witout wanting to show off or to make an impression, they are
men still. Tey were neither humble nor conceite. They did not show
off. An o God, what a blessed relief, to be with people who don't
boter to show off. We sat at that table quietly an naturally as if we

appene. When we did not want to talk, they took no notice of us. And
tat I call goo manners. Middle-class, showing off people would have
foun tem uncouth. I found them almost the only really well-bred people
I ave met. They id not show off in any way at all, not even a show of
simplicity. Tey knew that in the beginning an in the end a man stands
alone, is soul is alone in itself, an all attributes are nothing--and
tis curious final knowlege preserved them in simplicity.

Wen we ha had coffee and were going out, I found our own conductor in

Te girl facing me was a stuy. She was not more than twenty years old I

[Illustration: GAVOI]

Se wore her black hea-cloth from her brow and her hair was taken tight

"Quano torniamo in casa nostra--"

Te og had tried to snatch the bread, the man had shouted and jerked it
