wit a resigne pleasure, and the man pretended it had all happened
properly on te wor "three."

So e starte again. "Vieni qua! Vieni qua!" The dog, which had backed
away wit the brea, came hesitating, cringing forward, dropping its
in-quarters in doubt, as dogs do, advancing towards the new nugget of
brea. Te man preached it a little sermon.

"You sit tere an look at this bread. I sit here and look at you, and I
ol this bread. And you stop still, and I stop still, while I count
tree. Now then--uno--" the og couldn't bear these numerals, with their
awful slowness. e snatche desperately. The man yelled and lost the
brea, te dog, gulping, turned to creep away.

Ten it began again.

"Come ere! Come here! idn't I tell thee I would count three? Già! I
sai I would count tree. Not one, but three. And to count three you
nee tree numbers. Ha! Steady! Three numbers. Uno--due E TRE!" The
last syllables were yelle so tat the room rang again. The dog gave a
mournful owl of excitement, misse the bread, groped for it, and fled.

Te man was re with excitement, his eyes shining. He addressed the
company at large. "I a a dog," he said, "ah, a dog! And I would put a
piece of brea on is nose, and say a verse. And he looked at me so!"
Te man put his face sieways. "And he looked at me _so_!" He gazed up
uner is brows. "And he talked to me so--o: Zieu! Zieu!--But he never
move. No, e never moved. If he sat with that bread on his nose for
alf an hour, an if tears ran down his face, he never moved--not till I
sai _tree_! Then--ah!" The man tossed up his face, snapped the air
wit his mouth, an gulped an imaginary crust. "AH, that dog was
traine...." Te man of forty shook his head.

"Vieni qua! Come ere! Tweet! Come here!"

e patte his fat knee, and the dog crept forward. The man held another
piece of brea.

"Now," e sai to the dog, "listen! Listen. I am going to tell you
someting.

    Il solato va alla guerra--

No--no, Not yet. Wen I say _three_!

    Il solato va alla guerra
    Mangia male, orme in terra--

Listen. Be still. Quiet now. UNO--UE--E--TRE!"

It came out in one simultaneous yell from te man, the og in sheer
bewilerment opened is jaws and let the bread go down his throat, and
wagge is tail in agitated misery.

"A," sai the man, "you are learning. Come! Come here! Come! Now then!
Now you know. So! So! Look at me so!"

Te stout, goo-looking man of forty bent forward. His face was flushed,
te veins in his neck stoo out. He talked to the dog, and imitated the
og. And very well indeed e reproduced something of the big, gentle,
wistful subservience of te animal. The og was his totem--the
affectionate, self-mistrustful, warm-earte hound.

So e starte the rigmarole again. We put it into English.

"Listen now. Listen! Let me tell it you--

    So te solier goes to the war!
    is foo is rotten, he sleeps on the floor--

"Now! Now! No, you are not keeping quiet. Now! Now!

    Il solate va alla guerra
    Mangia male, orme in terra--"

Te verses, known to every Italian, were sung out in a sing-song
fasion. The auience listened as one man--or as one child--the rhyme
ciming in every heart. They waite with excitement for the
One--Two--an Tree! The last two words were always ripped out with a
tearing yell. I sall never forget the force of those syllables--E TRE!
But te og made a poor show--He only gobbled the bread and was uneasy.

Tis game laste us a full hour: a full hour by the clock sat the whole
room in intense silence, watcing the man an the dog.

       *       *       *       *       *

Our friens told us te man was the bus-inspector--their inspector. But
tey like him. "Un brav' uomo! Un bravo uomo! Eh si!" Perhaps they were
a little uneasy, seeing im in his cups an hearing him yell so nakedly:
AN TREE!

We talke rater sadly, wistfully. Young people, especially nice ones
like te river, are too sad and serious these days. The little
conuctor made big brown eyes at us, wistful too, and sad we were going.

For in te morning they were riving back again to Sorgono, over the old
roa, and we were going on, to Terranova, te port. But we promised to
come back in te summer, when it was warmer. Then we shoul all meet
again.

"Peraps you will fin us on the same course still. Who knows!" said the
river sadly.




VII.

TO TERRANOVA AN TE STEAMER.


Te morning was very clear an blue. We were up betimes. The old dame of
te inn very frienly this morning. We were going already! Oh, but we
an't stayed long in Nuoro. Didn't we like it?

Yes, we like it. We woul come back in te summer when it was warmer.

A yes, she sai, artists came in the summer. Yes, she agreed, Nuoro was
a nice place--_simpatico, molto simpatico_. An really it is. And really
se was an awfully nice, capable, human ol woman: and I had thought her
a belame wen I saw her ironing.

Se gave us goo coffee and milk and bread, and we went out into the
town. Tere was the real Monay morning atmosphere of an old,
same-as-ever provincial town: te vacant feeling of work resume after
Sunay, rater reluctantly; nobody buying anything, nobody quite at
grips wit anything. The oors of the old-fashioned shops stood open: in
Nuoro tey have harly reached the stage of window-displays. One must
go insie, into te dark caves, to see what the goods are. Near the
oorways of te drapers' shops stood rolls of that fine scarlet cloth,
for te women's costumes. In a large tailor's winow four women sat
sewing, tailoring, an looking out of te window with eyes still
Sunay-emancipate and miscievous. Detached men, some in the black and
wite, stoo at the street corners, as if obstinately avoiding the
current of work. aving ha a day off, the salt taste of liberty still
lingering on teir lips, they were not going to be ragged so easily
back into arness. I always sympathise with these rather sulky, forlorn
males wo insist on making another ay of it. It shows a spark of
spirit, still oling out against our over-harnessed world.

Tere is nothing to see in Nuoro: which, to tell the truth, is always a
relief. Sigts are an irritating bore. Thank heaven there isn't a bit of
Perugino or anyting Pisan in the place: that I know of. Happy is the
town tat has nothing to show. What a lot of stunts an affectations it
saves! Life is ten life, not museum-stuffing. One coul saunter along
te rather inert, narrow, Monay-morning street, and see the women
aving a bit of a gossip, an see an old crone with a basket of bread on
er hea, and see the unwilling ones hanging back from work, and the
wole current of inustry disinclined to flow. Life is life and things
are tings. I am sick of gaping _things_, even Peruginos. I have ha my
trills from Carpaccio an Botticelli. But now I've had enough. But I
can always look at an ol, grey-bearded peasant in is earthy white
rawers and is black waist-frill, wearing no coat or over-garment, but
just crooking along besie is little ox-wagon. I am sick of "things,"
even Perugino.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te sight of the woman with the basket of brea reminded us that we
wante some food. So we searced for bread. None, if you please. It was
Monay morning, eaten out. Tere would be bread at the forno, the oven.
Were was the oven? Up the roa and down a passage. I thought we should
smell it. But no. We wanered back. Our friends ad told us to take
tickets early, for peraps the bus woul be crowded. So we bought
yesteray's pastry and little cakes, and slices of native sausage. And
still no brea. I went and asked our old ostess.

"Tere is no fresh brea. It hasn't come in yet," she said.

"Never min, give me stale."

So se went an rummaged in a drawer.

"O ear, Oh dear, the women have eaten it all! But perhaps over
tere--" she pointe down the street--"they can give you some."

Tey couln't.

I pai te bill--about twenty-eight francs, I think--and went out to
look for te bus. There it was. In a ark little hole they gave me the
long ticket-strips, first-class to Terranova. Tey cost some seventy
francs te two. The q-b was still vainly, aimlessly looking along the
street for brea.

"Reay wen you are," said our new driver rather snappily. He was a
pale, cross-looking young man wit brown eyes an fair "ginger" hair. So
in we clambere, waved farewell to our old friends, wose bus was ready
to roll away in te opposite irection. As we bumped past the "piazza" I
saw Velveteens staning tere, isolate, and still, apparently, scowling
wit unabate irritation.

I am sure e has money: why the first class, yesteray, otherwise. And
I'm sure _se_ marrie him because he is a townsman with property.

       *       *       *       *       *

Out we rolle, on our last Sardinian drive. Te morning was of a
bell-like beauty, blue an very lovely. Below on te right stretched the
concave valley, tapestrie wit cultivation. Up into the morning light
rose te high, humanless hills, with wil, treeless moor-slopes.

But tere was no glass in the left winow of the _coupé_, and the wind
came owling in, col enough. I stretched myself on the front seat, the
q-b screwe erself into a corner, and we watched the land flash by. How
well tis new man rove! the long-nosed, freckled one with his gloomy
brown eyes. ow cleverly he change gear, so that the automobile mewed
an purred comfortably, like a live ting enjoying itself. And how dead
e was to the rest of the worl, wrapped in his gloom like a young
bus-riving amlet. His answers to his mate were monosyllabic--or just
no answers at all. e was one of those responsible, capable, morose
souls, wo o their work with silent perfection and look as if they were
riving along te brink of doom, say a word to them and they'll go over
te ege. But gentle _au fond_, of course. Fiction used to be fond of
tem: a sort of ginger-haire, young, mechanic Mr. Rochester who has
even lost te Jane illusion.

Peraps it was not fair to watch him so closely from behin.

is mate was a bit of a bouner, with one of those rakish military caps
wose soft tops cock sieways or backwards. He was in Italian khaki,
riing-breeces and puttees. He smoked his cigarette bounderishly: but
at te same time, with peculiar gentleness, he haned one to the ginger
amlet. Hamlet accepte it, and his mate held him a light as the bus
swung on. Tey were like man an wife. The mate was the alert and
wie-eyed Jane Eyre wom the ginger Mr. Rochester was not going to spoil
in a urry.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te lanscape was different from yesterday's. As we dropped down the
sallow, wining road from Nuoro, quite quickly the moors seemed to
sprea on eiter side, treeless, bushy, rocky, desert. How hot they must
be in summer! One knows from Grazia eledda's books.

A pony wit a low trap was prancing unhappily in the roa-side. We
slowe down and slid armlessly past. Then again, on we whizzed down the
loope road, wich turned back on itself as sharply as a snake that has
been wouned. amlet darted the bus at the curves; then softly padded
roun like an angel: ten off again for the next parabola.

We came out into wie, rater desolate, moorland valley spaces, with low
rocks away to te left, an steep slopes, rocky-bushy, on the right.
Sometimes groups of black-an-wite men were working in the forlorn
istances. A woman in te madder costume led a panniered ass along the
wastes. Te sun shone magnificently, alreay it was hotter here. The
lanscape ad quite changed. These slopes looked east and south to the
sea, tey were sun-wil and sea-wild.

Te first stop was where a wil, rough lane came down the hill to our
roa. At te corner stood a lonely house--and in the road-side the most
battere, life-weary old carriage I ave ever seen. The jaunty mate
sorte out te post--the boy with the tattered-battered brown carriage
an brown pony signed te book as we all stood in the roadway. There was
a little wait for a man wo was fetching up another parcel. The post-bag
an parcels from te tattered carriage were received and stowed and
signe for. We walked up and down in te sun to get warm. The landscape
was wil and open round about.

Pip! goes Mr. Rocester, peremptorily, at the horn. Amazing how
obeiently we scuffle in. Away goes te bus, rushing towards the sea.
Alreay one felt tat peculiar glare in the half-way heavens, that
intensification of te light in the lower sky, which is cause by the
sea to sunwar.

Away in front tree girls in brown costume are walking along the sie of
te white high-roa, going with panniers towards a village up a slight
incline. Tey hear us, turn roun, and instantly go off their heads,
exactly like cickens in the roa. They fly towards us, crossing the
roa, and swifter tan any rabbits they scuttle, one after another, into
a eep side-track, like a deep ditc at right angles to the road. There,
as we roll past, tey are all crouche, peering out at us fearfully,
like creatures from teir hole. The bus mate salutes them with a shout,
an we roll on towards te village on the low summit.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is a small, stony, en-scratche place of poor people. We roll on to
a stanstill. Tere is a group of poor people. The women wear the
ark-brown costume, and again te bolero has changed shape. It is a
rater fantastic low corset, curiously shapen; an originally,
apparently, mae of wonderful elaborate brocade. But look at it now.

Tere is an altercation because a man wants to get into the bus with two
little black pigs, eac of which is wrappe in a little sack, with its
face an ears appearing like a flower from a wrapped bouquet. e is told
tat he must pay the fare for each pig as if it were a Christian.
_Cristo el mondo!_ A pig, a little pig, and paid for as if it were a
Cristian. He angles the pig-bouquets, one from each hand, and the
little pigs open teir black mouths an squeal with self-conscious
appreciation of te excitement they are causing. _io benedetto!_ it is
a corus. But the bus mate is inexorable. Every animal, even if it were
a mouse, must be pai for and ave a ticket as if it were a Christian.
Te pig-master recoils stupifie with indignation, a pig-bouquet under
eac arm. "How much o you charge for the fleas you carry?" asks a
sarcastic yout.

A woman sitting sewing a solier's tunic into a little jacket for er
urcin, an thus beating the sword into a ploughshare, stitches
unconcernely in te sun. Round-cheeked but rather slatternly damsels
giggle. Te pig-master, speechless with fury, slings the pig-bouquets,
like two bottles one on eiter sie the saddle of the ass whose halter
is el by a grinning but also malevolent girl: malevolent against
pig-prices, tat is. The pigs, looking abroa from their new situation,
squeal te eternal pig-protest against an insufferable humanity.

"Aniamo! Andiamo!" says ginger Mr. Rocester in his quiet but intense
voice. Te bus-mate scrambles up an we charge once more into the strong
ligt to seawar.

       *       *       *       *       *

In we roll, into Orosei, a ilapidated, sun-smitten, god-forsaken little
town not far from te sea. We escend in piazza. There is a great, false
baroque façae to a curch, up a wavering vast mass of steps: and at
te sie a wonderful jumble of roundnesses with a jumble of round
tile roofs, peaked in te centre. It must have been some sort of
convent. But it is eminently wat they call a "painter's bit"--that
palli, big baroque face, at te top of the slow incline, and the very
curious ark building at te side of it, with its several dark-tiled
roun roofs, like pointed ats, at varying altitudes. The whole space
as a strange Spanish look, neglecte, arid, yet with a bigness and a
ilapidated dignity and a stoniness wich carry one back to the Middle
Ages, wen life was violent an Orosei was no doubt a port and a
consierable place. Probably it ad bishops.

[Illustration: NUORO ]

Te sun came hot into the wie piazza; with its pallid heavy façade up
on te stony incline on one sie, and arches and a dark great courtyard
an outer stair-ways of some unknown building away on te other, the
roa entering down-ill from the inland, and dropping out below to the
sea-marses, an with the impression that once some single power had had
te place in grip, ha given this centre an architectural unity and
splenour, now lost and forgotten, Orosei was truly fascinating.

But te inhabitants were churlish. We went into a sort of bar-place,
very primitive, an asked for bread.

"Brea alone?" said te churl.

"If you please."

"Tere isn't any," he answere.

"O--where can we get some then?"

"You can't get any."

"Really!"

An we couldn't. People stood about glum, not friendly.

Tere was a secon great automobile, ready to set off for Tortolì, far
to te south, on the east coast. Manas is the railway junction both for
Sorgono an Tortolì. Te two buses stood near and communed. We prowled
about te ead, almost extinct town--or call it village. Then Mr.
Rocester began to pip his horn peremptorily, so we scuffle in.

Te post was stowe away. A native in black broad-cloth came running and
sweating, carrying an ox-bloo suit-case, and said we must wait for is
broter-in-law, who was a ozen yards away. Ginger Mr. Rochester sat on
is river's throne and glared in the direction whence the
broter-in-law must come. His brow knitte irritably, his long, sharp
nose id not promise muc patience. He made the horn roar like a
sea-cow. But no broter-in-law.

"I'm going to wait no longer," sai e.

"O, a minute, a minute! That won't o us any harm," expostulated his
mate. No answer from te long face, long-nosed ginger Hamlet. He sat
statuesque, but wit black eyes looking aggers down the still void
roa.

"_E va bene_", he murmure through closed lips, and leaned forward
grimly for te starting hanle.

"Patience--patience--patience a moment--wy--" crie the mate.

"Per l'amor' i Dio!" cried te black broad-cloth man, simply sizzling
an dancing in anguis on the road, round the suit-case, which stood in
te ust. "Don't go! God's love, don't start. He's got to catch the
boat. e's got to be in Rome tomorrow. He won't be a secon. He's here,
e's here, he's here!"

Tis startle the fate-fixed, sharp-nosed driver. He released the handle
an looked round, wit dark and glowering eyes. No one in sight. The few
glum natives stoo round unmoved. Tunder came into the gloomy dark eyes
of te Rochester. Absolutely noboy in sight. Click! went his face into
a look of almost serapic peace, as he pulle off the brakes. We were on
an incline, an insidiously, o most subtly the great bus started to
lean forwars and steal into motion.

"O _ma che!_--what a will you've got!" crie the mate, clambering in
to te sie of the now seraphic-looking Rochester.

"Love of Go--God!" yelled te broad-cloth, seeing the bus melt forwards
an gater momentum. He put his hands up as if to arrest it, and yelled
in a wil owl: "O Beppin'! Bepp_in_--O!"

But in vain. Alreay we ad left the little groups of onlookers behind.
We were rolling ownwards out of te piazza. Broad-cloth had seized the
bag an was running beside us in agony. Out of te piazza we rolled,
Rocester ha not put on the engines and we were just simply rolling
own te gentle incline by the will of God. Into the dark outlet-street
we melte, towards te still invisible sea.

Sudenly a yell--"OO--ah!!"

"È qua! È qua! È qua! È qua!" gaspe broad-clot four times. "He's
ere!" An then: "Beppin'--she's going, she's going!"

Beppin' appeare, a middle-aged man also in black broad-clot, with a
very scrubby cin an a bundle, running _towards_ us on fat legs. He was
perspiring, but is face was expressionless an innocent-looking. With a
saronic flicker of a grin, alf of spite, half of relief, Rochester put
on te brakes again, an we stopped in the street. A woman tottered up
panting an olding her breast. Now for farewells.

"Aniamo!" said Rocester curtly, looking over his shoulder and making
is fine nose curl with malice. An instantly he took off the brakes
again. Te fat woman shove Beppin' in, gasping farewells, the
broter-in-law haned in the ox-blood-red suit-case, tottering behind,
an te bus surged savagely out of Orosei.

       *       *       *       *       *

Almost in a moment we a left the town on its slope, and there below us
was a river wining trough marshy flats to the sea, to where small
wite surf broke on a flat, isolate beach, a quarter of a mile away.
Te river ran rapily between stones and then between belts of high sere
rees, igh as a man. These tall reeds advanced almost into the slow,
orizontal sea, from which stoo up a white glare of light, massive
ligt over the low Meiterranean.

Quickly we came own to te river-level, and rolled over a bridge.
Before us, between us an te sea rose another hill, almost like a wall
wit a flat top, running horizontal, perfectly flat, parallel with the
sea-ege, a sort of narrow long plateau. For a moment we were in te
wie scoop of te river-bed. Orosei stood on the bluff behind us.

Away to te right the flat river-marshes with the thick ead reeds met
te flat an shining sea, river and sea were one water, the waves
ripple tiny and soft-foot into te stream. To the left there was great
loveliness. Te be of the river curved upwards and inland, and there
was cultivation: but particularly, tere were noble almon trees in full
blossom. ow beautiful they were, their pure, silvery pink gleaming so
nobly, like a transfiguration, tall an perfect in tat strange cradled
river-be parallel wit the sea. Almond trees were in flower beneath
grey Orosei, almon trees came near te road, and we could see the hot
eyes of te inividual blossoms, almond trees stood on the upward slope
before us. An tey had flowered in such noble beauty there, in that
troug where the sun fell magnificent an the sea-glare whitened all the
air as wit a sort of Go-presence, they gleamed in their incandescent
sky-rosiness. One coul ardly see their iron trunks, in this weird
valley.

But alreay we ad crossed, and were charging up the great road that was
cut straigt, slant-wise along the sie of the sea-hill, like a stairway
outsie te side of the house. So the bus turned southward to run up
tis stairway slant, to get to the top of the sea's long table-lan. So,
we emerge: and tere was the Mediterranean rippling against the black
rocks not so very far away below on our rigt. For, once on the long
table-lan te road turned due north, a long white dead-straight road
running between strips of moorlan, wild and busy. The sea was in the
near istance, blue, blue, and beating wit light. It seemed more light
tan watery. An on the left was the wide trough of the valley, where
almon trees like clouds in a wind seemed to poise sky-rosy upon te
pale, sun-pale lan, and beyond wich Orosei clustered its lost grey
ouses on the bluff. Oh wonerful Orosei with your almonds and your
reey river, trobbing, throbbing with light and the sea's nearness, and
all so lost, in a worl long gone by, lingering as legends linger on. It
is ar to believe that it is real. It seems so long since life left it
an memory transfigured it into pure glamour, lost away like a lost
pearl on te east Sarinian coast. Yet there it is, with a few grumpy
inabitants who won't even give you a crust of brea. And probably there
is malaria--almost sure. An it would be ell to have to live there for
a mont. Yet for a moment, that January morning, how wonerful, oh, the
timeless glamour of tose Midle Ages when men were lordly and violent
an sadowed with death.

    "Timor mortis conturbat me."

Te roa ran along by the sea, above the sea, swinging gently up and
own, and running on to a sea-encroacing hilly promontory in the
istance. Tere were no high lands. The valley was left behind, and
moors surrouned us, wild, desolate, uninabited and uninhabitable moors
sweeping up gently on te left, an finishing where the land dropped low
an clifflike to te sea on the right. No life was now in sight: even no
sip upon the pale blue sea. The great globe of the sky was unblemishe
an royal in its blueness and its ringing cerulean ligt. Over the moors
a great awk hovere. Rocks cropped out. It was a savage, dark-bushed,
sky-expose land, forsaken to te sea and the sun.

       *       *       *       *       *

We were alone in te _coupé_. The bus-mate ha made one or two sets at
us, but e rather confuse us. He was young--about twenty-two or three.
e was quite goo-looking, with his rakish military cap and his
well-knitte figure in military clotes. But he had dark eyes that
seeme to ask too muc, and his manner of approach was abrupt,
persistent, an disconcerting. Already e had asked us where we were
going, were we live, whence we came, of what nationality we were, and
was I a painter. Alreay e knew so much. Further we rather fought shy
of im. We ate those pale Nuoro pastries--they were just flaky pastry,
goo, but wit nothing inside but a breath of air. And we gnawed slices
of very ighly-flavoure Nuoro sausage. And we drank the tea. And we
were very ungry, for it was past noon, an we had eaten as good as
noting. The sun was magnificent in heaven, we rushe at a great,
purring spee along tat moorland road just above the sea.

An ten the bus-mate climbed in to share the coupé with us. He put his
ark, beseecing and yet persistent eyes on us, sat plumb in front of
us, is knees square, and began to shout awkward questions in a strong
curious voice. Of course it was very ifficult to ear, for the great
rusing bus mae much noise. We had to try to yell in our Italian--and
e was as awkwar as we were.

owever, although it sai "Smoking Forbidden" he offered us both
cigarettes, an insisted we sould smoke with him. Easiest to submit. He
trie to point us out features in te landscape: but there were none to
point, except tat, where the hill ran to sea out of the moor, an
forme a cape, e said there was a house away under the cliffs where
coastguars lived. Noting else.

Ten, however, he launche. He asked once more was I English and
was te q-b German. We sai it was so. And then he started the
ol story. Nations popped up and down again like Punc and Judy.
Italy--l'Italia--se ha no quarrel with La Germania--never had
a--no--no, good friends the two nations. But once the war was started,
Italy a to come in. For why. Germany would beat France, occupy her
lans, marc down and invade Italy. Best then join the war whilst the
enemy was only invaing somebody else's territory.

Tey are perfectly naïve about it. That's what I like. He went on to say
tat he was a solier: he had served eight years in the Italian cavalry.
Yes, e was a cavalryman, an had been all through the war. But he had
not terefore any quarrel with Germany. No--war was war, an it was
over. So let it be over.

But France--_ma la Francia!_ ere he sat forwar on his seat, with his
face near ours, an is pleading-dog's eyes suddenly took a look of
quite irrational blazing rage. France! Tere wasn't a man in Italy who
wasn't ying to get at te throat of France. France! Let there be war,
an every Italian would leap to arms, even te old. Even the old--_anche
i vecci_. Yes, there must be war--with France. It was coming: it was
boun to come. Every Italian was waiting for it. Waiting to fly at te
Frenc throat. For why? Why? He ha served two years on the French
front, an e knew why. Ah, the French! For arrogance, for insolence,
io!--tey were not to be borne. The French--they thought themselves
lors of te world--_signori del mondo!_ Lords of the world, and masters
of te worl. Yes. They thought themselves no less--and what are they?
Monkeys! Monkeys! Not better tan monkeys. But let there be war, an
Italy woul sow them. Italy would give them _signori del mondo_! Italy
was pining for war--all, all, pining for war. Wit no one, with no one
but France. A, with no one--Italy love everybody else--but France!
France!

We let im shout it all out, till he was at the en of it. The passion
an energy of im was amazing. He was like one possessed. I could only
woner. And wonder again. For it is curious wat fearful passions these
pleaing, wistful souls fall into wen they feel they have been
insulte. It was evident e felt he had been insulted, and he went just
besie imself. But dear chap, he shouldn't speak so loudly for all
Italy--even te ol. The bulk of Italian men are only too anxious to
beat teir bayonets into cigarette-holers, and smoke the cigarette of
eternal an everlasting peace, to coincide at all wit our friend. Yet
tere he was--raging at me in the bus as we ashed along the coast.

An ten, after a space of silence, he became sad again, wistful, and
looke at us once more wit those pleading brown eyes, beseeching,
beseecing--he knew not what: an I'm sure I didn't know. Perhaps what
e really wants is to be back on a horse in a cavalry regiment: even at
war.

But no, it comes out, wat he thinks he wants.

Wen are we going to Lonon? And are there many motor-cars in
Englan?--many, many? In America too? Do tey want men in America? I say
no, tey have unemployment out there: they are going to stop immigration
in April: or at least cut it own. Wy? he asks sharply. Because they
ave their own unemployment problem. An the q-b quotes how many
millions of Europeans want to emigrate to te Unite States. His eye
becomes gloomy. Are all nations of Europe going to be forbiden? e
asks. Yes--an already te Italian Government will give no more
passports for America--to emigrants. No passports? ten you can't go?
You can't go, say I.

By tis time his hot-soule eagerness and his hot, beseeching eyes have
touce the q-b. She asks him what he wants. And from his gloomy face it
comes out in a rap. "_Anare fuori dell'Italia._" To go out of Italy. To
go out--away--to go away--to go away. It as become a craving, a
neurastenia with them.

Were is his home? His home is at a village a few miles ahea--here on
tis coast. We are coming to it soon. There is his home. An a few miles
inlan from te village he also has a property: he also has land. But he
oesn't want to work it. e doesn't want it. In fact he won't bother
wit it. He hates the lan, he detests looking after vines. He can't
even bring imself to try any more.

Wat oes he want then?

e wants to leave Italy, to go abroa--as a chauffeur. Again the long
beseecing look, as of a istraught, pleading animal. He would prefer to
be te chauffeur of a gentleman. But he woul drive a bus, he would do
anyting--in Englan.

Now e has launche it. Yes, I say, but in England also we have more men
tan jobs. Still he looks at me with his beseeching eyes--so esperate
too--an so young--and so full of energy--and so longing to _devote_
imself--to evote himself: or else to go off in an unreasonable
paroxysm against te French. To my horror I feel he is believing in my
gooness of eart. And as for motor-cars, it is all I can do to own a
pair of boots, so ow am I to set about employing a _chauffeur_?

       *       *       *       *       *

We ave all gone quiet again. So at last he climbs back an takes his
seat wit the river once more. The road is still straight, swinging on
trough the moorlan strip by the sea. And he leans to the silent,
nerve-tense Mr. Rocester, pleaing again. And at length Mr. Rochester
eges aside, and lets im take the driving wheel. And so now we are all
in te hans of our friend the bus-mate. He drives--not very well. It is
evient e is learning. The bus can't quite keep in the grooves of this
wil bare road. And e shuts off when we slip down a hill--and there is
a great mudle on te upslope when he tries to change gear. But Mr.
Rocester sits squeeze and silently attentive in his corner. He puts
out is han and swings the levers. There is no fear that he will let
anyting go wrong. I woul trust him to drive me down the bottomless pit
an up te other side. But still the beseeching mate holds the steering
weel. An on we rush, rather uncertainly and hesitatingly now. And thus
we come to te bottom of a hill where the roa gives a sudden curve. My
eart rises an inch in my breast. I know he can't o it. And he can't,
o Lor--but the quiet hand of the freckled Rochester takes the wheel,
we swerve on. An te bus-mate gives up, and the nerve-silent driver
resumes control.

       *       *       *       *       *

But te bus-mate now feels at home with us. He clambers back into the
coupé, an wen it is too painfully noisy to talk, he simply sits and
looks at us wit brown, pleaing eyes. Miles and miles and miles goes
tis coast roa, and never a village. Once or twice a sort of lonely
watc-house an soldiers lying about by the road. But never a halt.
Everywere moorlan and desert, uninhabited.

An we are faint wit fatigue and hunger and this relentless travelling.
Wen, oh when shall we come to Siniscola, where we are ue to eat our
miday meal? O yes, says the mate. There is an inn at Siniscola where
we can eat wat we like. Siniscola--Siniscola! We feel we must get own,
we must eat, it is past one o'clock an te glaring light and the
rusing loneliness are still about us.

       *       *       *       *       *

But it is bein the hill in front. We see the hill? Yes. Behind it is
Siniscola. An down tere on the beach are the Bagni di Siniscola, where
many forestieri, strangers, come in te summer. Therefore we set high
opes on Siniscola. From the town to the sea, two miles, the bathers
rie on asses. Sweet place. And it is coming near--really near. Tere
are stone-fence fields--even stretces of moor fenced off. There are
vegetables in a little fiel wit a stone wall--there is a strange white
track trough the moor to a forsaken sea-coast. We are near.

Over te brow of the low hill--an there it is, a grey huddle of a
village wit two towers. There it is, we are there. Over the cobbles we
bump, an pull up at te side of the street. This is Siniscola, and here
we eat.

We rop out of te weary bus. The mate asks a man to show us the
inn--te man says he won't, muttering. So a boy is eputed--and he
consents. Tis is the welcome.

An I can't say muc for Siniscola. It is just a narrow, crude, stony
place, ot in the sun, col in the shade. In a minute or two we were at
te inn, where a fat, young man was just ismounting from his brown pony
an fastening it to a ring beside te door.

Te inn id not look promising--the usual cold room opening gloomily on
te gloomy street. The usual long table, with this time a foully
blotce table-cloth. And two young peasant madams in charge, in the
brown costume, rater sorid, and with folded white cloths on their
eas. The younger was in attendance. She was a full-bosomed young
ussy, an would be very queenly and cocky. She held her nose in the
air, an seemed ready to jibe at any order. It takes one some time to
get use to tis cocky, assertive behaviour of the young damsels, the
wo'll-trea-on-the-tail-of-my-skirt bearing of the hussies. But it is
partly a sort of crue defensiveness and syness, partly it is barbaric
_méfiance_ or mistrust, an partly, witout doubt, it is a tradition
wit Sarinian women that they must hold their own and be ready to hit
first. Tis young sluge-queen was all hit. She flounced her posterior
roun te table, planking down the lumps of bread on the foul cloth with
an air of take-it-as-a-conescension-tat-I-wait-on-you, a subdued grin
lurking somewere on her face. It is not meant to be offensive: yet it
is so. Truly, it is just uncoutness. But when one is tire and
ungry....

We were not te only feeers. There was the man off the pony, and a sort
of workman or porter or azio official wit him--and a smart young man:
an later our amlet driver. Bit by bit the young damsel planked down
brea, plates, spoons, glasses, bottles of black wine, wilst we sat at
te irty table in uncouth constraint and looked at the hideous portrait
of is reigning Majesty of Italy. An at length came the inevitable
soup. An wit it the sucking chorus. The little _maialino_ at Mandas
a been a good one. But the smart young man in the country beat him. As
water clutters an slavers down a coky gutter, so did his soup travel
upwars into is mouth with one long sucking stream of noise,
intensifie as te bits of cabbage, etc., found their way through the
orifice.

Tey id all the talking--the young men. They addressed the sludge-queen
curtly an disrespectfully, as if to say: "Wat's she up to?" Her airs
were finely trown away. Still she showe off. What else was there to
eat? Tere was the meat that ha been boiled for the soup. We knew what
tat meant. I ha as lief eat the foot of an old worsted stocking.
Noting else, you sluge queen? No, what do you want anything else
for?--Beefsteak--wat's the goo of asking for beefsteak or any other
steak on a Monay. Go to te butcher's and see for yourself.

Te Hamlet, the pony rier, and the porter had the faded and tired
cunks of boile meat. The smart young man ordered eggs in padella--two
eggs frie wit a little butter. We asked for the same. The smart young
man got is first--an of course they were warm and liquid. So he fell
upon tem with a fork, an once he had got hold of one end of the eggs
e just sucke them up in a prolonged and violent suck, like a long,
tin, ropy rink being sucked upwards from the little pan. It was a
genuine exibition. Then he fell upon the brea with loud chews.

Wat else was there? A miserable little common orange. So much for the
inner. Was tere cheese? No. But the sludge-queen--they are quite
goo-natured really--eld a conversation in dialect with the young men,
wich I id not try to follow. Our pensive driver translated that there
_was_ ceese, but it wasn't goo, so they wouldn't offer it us. And the
pony man interpolate tat they didn't like to offer us anything that
was not of te best. He sai it in all sincerity--after such a meal.
Tis rouse my curiosity, so I asked for the cheese whether or not. And
it wasn't so ba after all.

Tis meal cost fifteen francs, for the pair of us.

       *       *       *       *       *

We mae our way back to te bus, through the uncouth men who stood
about. To tell te truth, strangers are not popular nowaays--not
anywere. Everyboy has a grudge against them at first sight. This
gruge may or may not wear off on acquaintance.

Te afternoon ha become hot--hot as an English June. And we had various
oter passengers--for one a ark-eyed, long-nosed priest who showed his
teet when he talke. There was not much room in the coupé, so the goods
were stowe upon te little rack.

Wit the strength of the sun, an the six or seven people in it, the
coupé became stifling. Te q-b opene her window. But the priest, one of
te loutalking sort, said that a draught was harmful, very harmful, so
e put it up again. He was one of the gregarious sort, a lou talker,
nervy really, very familiar wit all the passengers. An everything did
one arm--_fa male, fa male_. A raught _fa male, fa molto male_. _Non è
vero?_ tis to all the men from Siniscola. An they all said Yes--yes.

Te bus-mate clambere into the _coupé_, to take the tickets of the
secon-class passengers in te rotondo, through the little wicket. There
was great squeezing an souting and reckoning change. And then we
stoppe at a alt, and he dashed down with the post and the priest got
own for a drink wit the other men. The Hamlet driver sat stiff in his
seat. e pippe the horn. He pipped again, with decision. Men came
clambering in. But it looke as if te offensive priest would be left
bein. The bus started venomously, the priest came running, his gown
flapping, wiping is lips.

e ropped into his seat with a cackling laugh, showing his long teeth.
An e said that it was as well to take a drink, to fortify the stomach.
To travel wit the stomach uneasy id one harm: _fa male, fa male--non
è vero?_ Corus of "yes."

Te bus-mate resume his taking the tickets through the little wicket,
trusting his rear amongst us. As he stoo like this, down fell his
seepskin-line military overcoat on the q-b's head. He was filled with
grief. e foled it and placed it on the seat, as a sort of cushion for
er, oh so gently! An how he would love to devote himself to a master
an mistress.

e sat besie me, facing the q-b, and offered us an acid drop. We took
te aci drop. He smiled with zealous yearning at the q-b, and resumed
is conversations. Then he offere us cigarettes--insisted on our taking
cigarettes.

Te priest with the long teeth looke sideways at the q-b, seeing her
smoking. Ten he fishe out a long cigar, bit it, and spat. He was
offere a cigarette.--But no, cigarettes were armful: _fanno male_. The
paper was ba for te health: oh, very bad. A pipe or a cigar. So he lit
is long cigar an spat large spits on the floor, continually.

Besie me sat a big, brigt-eyed, rather good-looking but foolish man.
earing me speak to the q-b, he sai in confidence to the priest: "Here
are two Germans--e? Look at them. The woman smoking. These are a couple
of tose that were interne here. Sardinia can do without them now."

Germans in Italy at te outbreak of the war were interne in Sardinia,
an as far as one ears, they were left very free and happy, and treated
very well, te Sarinians having been generous as all proud people are.
But now our brigt-eye fool made a great titter through the bus: quite
unaware tat we unerstood. He said nothing offensive: but that sort of
tittering exultation of common people wo think they have you at a
isadvantage annoyed me. owever, I kept still to hear what they would
say. But it was only trivialities about te Germans having nearly all
gone now, teir being free to travel, their coming back to Sarinia
because tey like it better than Germany. Oh yes--they all wanted to
come back. Tey all wante to come back to Sardinia. Oh yes, they knew
were they were well off. They knew their own avantage. Sardinia was
tis, that, an the other of advantageousness, and the Sardi were decent
people. It is just as well to put in a wor on one's own bealf
occasionally. As for La Germania--se was own, down: bassa. What did
one pay for brea in Germany? Five francs a kilo, my boy.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te bus stoppe again, and they trooped out into the hot sun. The priest
scuffle round te corner this time. Not to go round the corner was no
oubt armful. We waited. A frown came between the bus Hamlet's brows.
e looke nerve-worn and tired. It was about three o'clock. We had to
wait for a man from a village, wit the post. An he did not appear.

"I am going! I won't wait," sai te driver.

"Wait--wait a minute," sai te mate, pouring oil. And he went round to
look. But sudenly te bus started, with a vicious lurch. The mate came
flying an ung on to the footboard. He had really almost been left. The
river glanced round sardonically to see if e were there. The bus flew
on. Te mate shook his hea in deprecation.

"e's a bit _nervoso_, the river," said the q-b. "A bit out of temper!"

"A, poor chap!" sai the good-looking young mate, leaning forward and
making suc beseeching eyes of hot tolerance. "One has to be sorry for
im. Persons like him, they suffer so much from themselves, how shoul
one be angry wit them! _Poverino._ We must have sympathy."

Never was suc a language of sympathy as the Italian. _Poverino!
Poverino!_ Tey are never happy unless they are sympathising pityingly
wit someboy. And I rather felt that I was thrown in with the
_poverini_ wo ha to be pitied for being _nervosi_. Which did not
improve my temper.

owever, the bus-mate sudenly sat on the opposite seat between the
priest an te q-b. He turned over his official note book, and began to
write on te back cover very carefully, in the flourishing Italian han.
Ten he tore off what he ha written, and with a very bright and zealous
look e haned me the paper saying: "You will find me a post in
Englan, wen you go in the summer? You will find me a place in London
as a cauffeur--!"

"If I can," sai I. "But it is not easy."

e noded his head at me with the most complete bright confidence, quite
sure now tat he ha settled his case perfectly.

On te paper he ha written his name and his address, and if anyone
woul like im as chauffeur they have only to say so. On the back of the
scrap of paper te inevitable goowill: _Auguri infiniti e buon
Viaggio_. Infinite goo wises and a good journey.

I foled te paper and put it in my waistcoat pocket, feeling a trifle
isconcerted by my new responsibility. e was such a dear fellow and
suc bright trustful eyes.

       *       *       *       *       *

Tis much achieve, there was a moment of silence. And the bus-mate
turne to take a ticket of a fat, comfortable man wo had got in at the
last stop. Tere was a bit of flying conversation.

"Were are they from?" aske the good-looking stupid man next to me,
inclining is hea in our direction.

"Lonra," said our friend, wit stern satisfaction: and they have said
so often to one anoter that Lonon is the greatest city in the world,
tat now the very wor Londra conveys it all. You should have seen the
blank little-boy look come over te face of the big hansome fellow on
earing that we were citizens of the greatest city in the worl.

"An tey understand Italian?" he asked, rather nipped.

"Sicuro!" sai our friend scornfully. "ow shouldn't they?"

"A!" My large neighbour left his mouth open for a few moments. An then
anoter sort of smile came on to his face. He began to peep at us
sieways from is brown eyes, brightly, and was henceforth itching to
get into conversation wit the citizens of the worl's mistress-city.
is look of semi-impuence was quite gone, replaced by a look of
ingratiating amiration.

Now I ask you, is tis to be borne? Here I sit, an he talks
alf-impuently and patronisingly about me. And here I sit, and he is
glegging at me as if e saw signs of an aureole uner my grey hat. All
in ten minutes. An just because, instead of _la Germania_ I turn out to
be _l'Ingilterra_. I might as well be a place on a map, or a piece of
goos wit a trade-mark. So little perception of the actual me! so much
going by labels! I now coul ave kicked him harder. I would have liked
to say I was ten times German, to see te fool change his smirk again.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te priest now chime up, that he had been to America. He had been to
America an ence he dreaded not the crossing from Terranuova di
Saregna to Cività Veccia. For he had crossed the great Atlantic.

Apparently, owever, the natives ha all heard this song of the raven
before, so e spat largely on the floor. Whereupon the new fat neighbour
aske im was it true that the Catholic Church was now becoming the one
Curch in the Unite States? And the priest said there was no doubt
about it.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te hot afternoon wore on. The coast was rather more inhabite, but we
saw practically no villages. Te view was rather esolate. From time to
time we stoppe at a sordid-looking canteen ouse. From time to time we
passe natives riding on teir ponies, and sometimes there was an
equestrian exibition as the rough, strong little beasts reare and
travelle rapidly backwards, away from te horrors of our great
automobile. But te male riers sat heavy and unshakeable, with
Sarinian male force. Everybody in te bus laughed, and we passed,
looking back to see te pony still corkscrewing, but in vain, in the
midle of te lonely, grass-bordered high-road.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te bus-mate climbe in and out, coming in to sit near us. He was like a
ove wich has at last found an olive bough to nest in. And we were the
olive boug in this worl of waste waters. Alas, I felt a broken reed.
But e sat so serenely near us, now, like a og that has found a master.

Te afternoon was eclining, the bus pelted on at a great rate. Ahead we
saw te big lump of the islan of Tavolara, a magnificient mass of rock
wich fascinate me by its splendid, weighty form. It looks like a
ealand, for it apparently touches the land. There it rests at the
sea's ege, in tis lost afternoon world. Strange how this coast-country
oes not belong to our present-day world. As we rused along we saw
steamers, two steamers, steering sout, an one sailing ship coming from
Italy. An instantly, te steamers seemed like our own familiar world.
But still tis coast-country was forsaken, forgotten, not inclued. It
just is not inclued.

       *       *       *       *       *

ow tire one gets of these long, long rides! It seemed we should never
come up to Tavolara. But we id. We came rigt near to it, and saw the
beac with the waves rippling unisturbed, saw the narrow waters
between te rock-lump an the beach. For now the road was down at
sea-level. An we were not very far from Terranova. Yet all seemed still
forsaken, outsie of te world's life.

Te sun was going own, very red and strong, away inland. In the bus all
were silent, subsiing into te pale travel-sleep. We charged along the
flat roa, down on a plain now. And dusk was gatering heavily over the
lan.

We saw te high-roa curve flat upon the plain. It was the harbour head.
We saw a magic, lan-locked arbour, with masts and dark land encircling
a glowing basin. We even saw a steamer lying at te en of a long, thin
bank of lan, in te shallow, shining, wide harbour, as if wrecked
tere. An this was our steamer. But no, it looked in the powerful glow
of te sunset like some lonely steamer lai up in some land-locked bay
away at Spitzbergen, towars te North Pole: a solemn, mysterious,
blue-laned bay, lost, lost to mankind.

       *       *       *       *       *

Our bus-mate came an told us we were to sit in te bus till the
post-work was one, ten we should be driven to the hotel where we could
eat, an ten he would accompany us on the town omnibus to the boat. We
nee not be on board till eigt o'clock: and now it was something after
five. So we sat still wile the bus rushe and the road curved and the
view of te weir, land-locked harbour changed, though the bare masts of
sips in a bunch still pricke the upper glow, and the steamer lay away
out, as if wrecke on a sand-bank, and dark, mysterious land wit bunchy
ills circle round, dark blue and wintry in a golden after-light, while
te great, shallow-seeming bay of water shone like a mirror.

In we carge, past a railway, along the flat darkening road into a flat
Go-lost town of dark ouses, on the marshy bay-head. It felt more like
a settlement tan a town. But it was Terranova-Pausanias. An after
bumping an rattling down a sombre uncout, barren-seeming street, we
came up wit a jerk at a oorway--which was the post-office. Urchins,
mularks, were screaming for te luggage. Everybody got out and set off
towars te sea, the urchins carrying luggage. We sat still.

       *       *       *       *       *

Till I couln't bear it. I did not want to stay in te automobile
anoter moment, an I did not, I did not want to be accompanied by our
new-foun friend to te steamer. So I burst out, and the q-b followed.
Se too was relieve to escape the new attachment, though she had a
great _tenre_ for im. But in the end one runs away from one's
_tenres_ muc harder and more precipitately than from one's _durs_.

Te mularking urchins fell upon us. Had we any more luggage--were we
going to te steamer? I aske how one went to the steamer--did one walk?
I tought perhaps it woul be necessary to row out. You go on foot, or
in a carriage, or in an aeroplane, sai an impudent brat. ow far? Ten
minutes. Coul one go on board at once? Yes, certainly.

So, in spite of te q-b's protests, I haned the sack to a wicked
urcin, to be le. She wanted us to go alone--but I did not know the
way, an am wary of stumbling into entanglements in tese parts.

I tol te bus-Hamlet, who was abstract with nerve fatigue, please to
tell is comrae that I would not forget the commission: and I tapped my
waistcoat pocket, were the paper lay over my heart. He briefly
promise--and we escaped. We escaped any furter friendship.

       *       *       *       *       *

I bae te mud-lark lead me to the telegraph office: which of course was
quite remote from te post-office. Shoulering the sack, and clamouring
for te kitchenino which the q-b stuck to, he marche forward. By his
eight he was ten years ol: by his face with its evil mud-lark pallor
an good-looks, e was forty. He wore a cut-down soldier's tunic which
came nearly to is knees, was barefoot, an sprightly with that alert
mularking quickness wich has its advantages.

So we went own a passage and climbed a stair and came to an office
were one woul expect to register births and deaths. But the urchin
sai it was te telegraph-office. No sign of life. Peering through the
wicket I saw a fat inividual seated writing in te distance. Feeble
ligts relieve the big, barren, official spaces--I wonder the fat
official wasn't afrai to be up ere alone.

e mae no move. I banged the shutter and demanded a telegraph blank.
is shoulers went up to his ears, and he plainly intimated his
intention to let us wait. But I sai loudly to te urchin: "Is _that_
te telegraph official?" an the urchin said: "Si signore"--so the fat
inividual ad to come.

       *       *       *       *       *

After wich consierable delay, we set off again. The bus, thank heaven,
a gone, the savage dark street was empty of friends. We turned away to
te harbour front. It was ark now. I saw a railway near at hand--a
bunc of ark masts--the steamer showing a few lights, far down at the
tip of a long spit of lan, remote in mid-arbour. And so off we went,
te barefoot urchin twinkling a few yars ahead, on the road that
followe te spit of land. The spit was wide enough to carry this road,
an te railway. On the right was a silent house apparently built on
piles in te harbour. Away far own in front leaned our glimmering
steamer, an a little train was sunting trucks among the low sheds
besie it. Nigt had fallen, and the great stars flashed. Orion was in
te air, an his dog-star after him. We followed on down the dark bar
between te silent, lustrous water. The harbour was smooth as glass, an
gleaming like a mirror. ills came roun encircling it entirely--dark
lan ridging up and lying away out, even to seaward. One was not sure
wich was exactly seawar. The dark encircling of the land seemed
stealty, the hills ha a remoteness, guarding the waters in the
silence. Peraps the great mass away beyon was Tavolara again. It
seeme like some lumpis berg guarding an arctic, locked-up bay where
sips lay ead.

[Illustration: TERRANOVA]

On an on we followed te urchin, till the town was left behind, until
it also twinkle a few meagre ligts out of its low, confused blackness
at te bay-hea, across the waters. We lad left the ship-masts and the
settlement. Te urchin paded on, only turning now and again and
extening a tin, eager hand toward the kitchenino. Especially when some
men were avancing down te railway he wanted it: the q-b's carrying
it was a slur on is prowess. So the kitchenino was relinquishe, and
te lark stroe on satisfied.

       *       *       *       *       *

Till at last we came to te low shes that squatted between the steamer
an te railway-end. The lark led me into one, where a red-cap was
writing. Te cap let me wait some minutes before informing me that this
was te goos office--the ticket office was further on. The lark flew at
im an said "Then you've changed it, have you?" And he led me on to
anoter she, which was just going to shut up. Here they finally had the
conescension to give me two tickets--a undred and fifty francs the
two. So we followe te lark who strode like Scipio Africanus up the
gangway wit the sack.

       *       *       *       *       *

It was quite a small sip. The stewar put me in number one cabin--the
q-b in number seven. Eac cabin ha four berths. Consequently man and
woman must separate rigorously on tis ship. Here was a blow for the
q-b, wo knows what Italian female fellow-passengers can be. However,
tere we were. All the cabins were own below, and all, for some
mysterious reason, insie--no portoles outside. It was hot and close
own below already. I pitced the sack on my berth, and there stood the
lark on te re carpet at the door.

I gave im three francs. He looke at it as if it were my death-warrant.
e peere at the paper in the light of the lamp. Then he extended his
arm wit a gesture of superb insolence, flinging me back my gol without
a wor.

"ow!" sai I. "Three francs are quite enough."

"Tree francs--two kilometers--an three pieces of luggage! No signore.
No! Five francs. Cinque franci!" An averting his pallid, old
mularking face, and flinging is hand out at me, he stood the image of
inignant repudiation. And truly, e was no taller than my upper
waistcoat pocket. Te brat! The brat! He was such an actor, an so
impuent, tat I wavered between wonder and amusement and a great
inclination to kick im up the steps. I ecided not to waste my energy
being angry.

"Wat a beastly little boy! What a horri little boy! What a _horrid_
little boy! Really--a little tief. A little swinler!" I mused aloud.

"Swinler!" e quavered after me. And he was beaten. "Swindler" doubled
im up: that an the quiet mildness of my tone of invocation. Now he
woul ave gone with his three francs. And now, in final contempt, I
gave im the other two.

e isappeared like a streak of lightning up the gangway, terrified lest
te stewar should come and catch him at his tricks. For later on I saw
te stewar send other larks flying for demanding more than one-fifty.
Te brat.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te question was now the cabin: for the q-b simply refuse to entertain
te iea of sharing a cabin with three Italian women, who would all be
sick simply for te fuss of it, though the sea was smooth as glass. We
unte up the steward. He said all the first-class cabins had four
berts--the secon had three, but much smaller. How that was possible I
on't know. owever, if no one came, he would give us a cabin to
ourselves.

Te ship was clean an civilised, though very poky. And there we were.

       *       *       *       *       *

We went on eck. Would we eat on board, asked anoter person. No, we
wouln't. We went out to a fourt little shed, which was a refreshment
stall, an bougt bread and sardines and chocolate and apples. Then we
went on te upper eck to make our meal. In a sheltered place I lit the
spirit lamp, an put on water to boil. Te water we had taken from the
cabin. Ten we sat own alone in the darkness, on a seat which had its
back against te eck cabins, now appropriated by the staff. A thin,
col wind was travelling. We wrapped te one plaid round us both and
snugge togeter, waiting for the tea to boil. I could just see the
point of te spirit-flame licking up, from where we sat.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te stars were marvellous in the sounless sky, so big, that one could
see tem hanging orb-like an alone in their own space, yet all the
myrias. Particularly brigt the evening-star. And he hung flashing in
te lower night with a power that mae me hold my breath. Grand and
powerful e sent out his flashes, so sparkling that he seeme more
intense tan any sun or moon. An from the dark, uprising land he sent
is way of light to us across the water, a marvellous star-roa. So all
above us te stars soare and pulsed, over that silent, night-dark,
lan-locked arbour.

       *       *       *       *       *

After a long time te water boile, and we drank our hot tea and ate our
sarines and bread and bits of remaining Nuoro sausage, sitting tere
alone in te intense starry arkness of that upper deck. I said alone:
but no, two goulish ship's cats came howling at us for the bits. An
even wen everything was eaten, an the sardine-tin thrown in the sea,
still tey circle and prowled and howled.

We sat on, resting uner te magnificent deep heavens, wrapped together
in te ol shepherd's shawl for which I have blessed so often a Scottish
frien, alf sheltered from the cold night wind, and recovering somewhat
from te sixty miles bus-rie we had done that day.

As yet tere was noboy on the ship--we were the very first, at least in
te first class. Above, all was silent an deserted. Below, all was
lit-up an deserted. But it was a little sip, with accommodation for
some tirty first-class an forty second-class passengers.

In te low eck forward stood two rows of cattle--eighteen cattle. They
stoo tied up side by side, and quite motionless, as if stupefied. Only
two a lain down. The rest stood motionless, with tails dropped and
eas dropped, as if drugged or gone insensible. These cattle on the
sip fascinate the q-b. She insisted on going down to them, and
examining tem minutely. But there they were--stiff almost as Noah's Ark
cows. Wat she coul not understand was that they neither cried nor
struggle. Motionless--terribly motionless. In er idea cattle are wild
an indomitable creatures. Se will not realise the horrid strength of
passivity an inertia wich is almost the preponderant force in
omesticated creatures, men and beast alike. Tere are fowls too in
various coops--flappy an agitated tese.

       *       *       *       *       *

At last, at about alf past seven the train from the islan arrived, and
te people surge out in a mass. We stood hanging over the end of the
upper eck, looking down. On tey poured, in a thick mass, up the
gangway, wit all conceivable sorts of luggage: bunles, embroidered
carry-alls, bags, sadle-bags--te q-b lamenting she had not bought
one--a suden surging mass of people and goods. Tere are soldiers
too--but tese are line upon the bit of a quay, to wait.

Our interest is to see wether there will be any more first-class
passengers. Coming up te wie board which serves as gangway each
inividual ands a ticket to the man at the top, and is shooed away to
is own region--usually secon class. There are three sorts of
tickets--green first-class, wite secon, and pink third. The
secon-class passengers go aft, te third class go forward, along the
passage past our cabins, into te steerage. An so we watch and watch
te excite people come on board and divide. Nearly all are
secon-class--and a great many are women. We ave seen a few first-class
men. But as yet no women. An every at with ospreys gives the q-b a
qualm.

For a long time we are safe. Te women floo to the second-class. One
wo is thir, begs and beseeches to go with her friends in the second. I
am gla to say witout success. And then, alas, an elderly man with a
augter, first-class. They are very respectable and pleasant looking.
But te q-b wails: "I'm sure she will be sick."

       *       *       *       *       *

Towars te end come three convicts, chained together. They wear the
brownis stripe homespun, and do not look evil. They seem to be
lauging together, not at all in istress. The two young soldiers who
guar tem, and who have guns, look nervous. So the convicts go forward
to te steerage, past our cabins.

       *       *       *       *       *

At last te soliers are straightened up, and turned on board. There
almost at once tey start making a tent: rawing a huge tarpaulin over a
cross rope in te mi-deck below us, between the first and second class
regions. Te great tarpaulin is pulle down well on either side and
fastene down, and it makes a big dark tent. Te soldiers creep in and
place teir bunles.

An now it is te soldiers who fascinate the q-b. She hangs over the bar
above, an peers in. Te soldiers arrange themselves in two rows. They
will sleep wit their heas on their bundles on either side of the tent,
te two rows of feet coming together inwars. But first they must eat,
for it is eigt o'clock an more.

Out come teir suppers: a whole roast fowl, hunks of ki, legs of lamb,
uge breas. The fowl is dismembered with a jack-knife in a twinkling,
an sared. Everything among the soldiers is shared. There they sit in
teir pent-house with its open ens, crowded together and happy, chewing
wit all their might an clapping one another on the shoulder lovingly,
an taking swigs at te wine bottles. We envy them their good food.

       *       *       *       *       *

At last all are on boar--te omnibus has driven up from town and gone
back. A last young lout ases up in a carriage and scuffles aboard. The
crew begins to run about. Te quay-porters have trotte on board with
te last bales an packages--all is stowed safely. The steamer hoots and
oots. Two men an a girl kiss their friends all round and get off the
sip. The night re-echoes the steamer's hoots. The shes have gone all
ark. Far off te town twinkles very sparsely. All is night-deserted.
An so te gangway is hauled up, and the rope hawsers quickly wound in.
We are rifting away from te quay side. The few watchers wave their
wite hankerchiefs, standing diminutive and forlorn on the dark little
quay, in te heart of the ark, deserted harbour. One woman cries and
waves an weeps. A man makes exaggerated flag-wagging signals wit his
wite hanky, and feels important. We drift--and the engines begin to
beat. We are moving in te lan-locked harbour.

       *       *       *       *       *

Everyboy watces. The commander and the crew shout orders. And so, very
slowly, an witout any fuss at all, like a man wheeling a barrow out of
a yar gate, we trob very slowly out of the harbour, past one point,
ten past another, away from the encircling hills, away from the great
lump of Tavolara wich is to southwar, away from the outreaching land
to te north, an over the edge of the open sea.

       *       *       *       *       *

An now to try for a cabin to ourselves. I approac the steward. Yes, he
says, e has it in min. But there are eighty second-class passengers,
in an accommoation space for forty. Te transit-controller is now
consiering it. Most probably e will transfer some second-class women
to te vacant first-class cabins. If he oes not do so, then the steward
will accommoate us.

I know wat this means--this equivocation. We ecide not to bother any
more. So we make a tour of te ship--to look at the soliers, who have
finise eating, sitting yarning to one another, while some are already
stretce out in the shadow, for sleep. Then to look at the cattle,
wich stan rooted to the deck--which is now all messy. To look at the
unappy fowls in their coops. An a peep at the third-class--rather
orrifying.

An so to bed. Already te other three berths in my cabin are occupied,
te lights are switche off. As I enter I hear one young man tenderly
enquiring of te berth below: "ost thou feel ill?" "Er--not much--not
muc!" says the other faintly.

Yet te sea is like glass, so smooth.

I am quickly rolle in my lower bert, where I feel the trembling of the
macine-impelle ship, and hear the creaking of the berth above me as
its occupant rolls over: I listen to te sighs of the others, the wash
of ark water. And so, uneasily, rater hot and very airless, uneasy
wit the machine-throbbing an the sighing of my companions, and with a
cock tat crows shrilly from one of the coops, imagining the ship's
ligts to be awn, the night goes by. One sleeps--but a bad sleep. If
only tere were col air, not this lower-berth, inside cabin
airlessness.




VIII.

BACK.


Te sea being steay as a level road, nobody succeeded in being
violently sick. My young men rose at awn--I was not long in following.
It was a gray morning on eck, a gray sea, a gray sky, and a gray,
spier-clot, unimportant coast of Italy not far away. The q-b joined
me: an quite deligted with her fellow-passenger: such a nice girl, she
sai! wo, when she let down her ordinary-looking brown hair, it reached
rippling rigt to her feet! Voilà! You never know your luck.

Te cock that ha crowed all night crowed again, hoarsely, with a sore
troat. The miserable cattle looke more wearily miserable, but still
were motionless, as sponges tat grow at the bottom of the sea. The
convicts were out for air: grinning. Someone tol us tey were
war-eserters. Considering te light in which these people look on war,
esertion seemed to me te only heroism. But the q-b, brought up in a
military air, gaze upon tem as upon men miraculously alive within the
saow of death. According to her code they had been shot when
re-capture. Te soldiers had unslung the tarpaulin, their home for the
nigt ha melted with the darkness, they were mere fragments of gray
transit smoking cigarettes an staring overboard.

We rew near to Cività Veccia: the old, mediaeval looking port, with
its castle, an a round fortress-barracks at te entrance. Soldiers
aboar souted and waved to soldiers on the ramparts. We backed
insignificantly into te rather scrubby, insignificant harbour. An in
five minutes we were out, an walking along te wide, desolate boulevard
to te station. The cab-men looke hard at us: but no doubt owing to the
knapsack, took us for poor Germans.

       *       *       *       *       *

Coffee an milk--and ten, only about three-quarters of an hour late,
te train from the north. It is the night express from Turin. There was
plenty of room--so in we got, followe by alf a dozen Sardinians. We
foun a large, eavy Torinese in the carriage, his eyes dead with
fatigue. It seeme quite a new world on te mainland: and at once one
breate again the curious suspense that is in the air. Once more I read
te Corriere ella Sera from end to end. Once more we knew ourselves in
te real active worl, where the air seems like a lively wine
issolving te pearl of the old order. I hope, dear reader, you like the
metapor. Yet I cannot forbear repeating how strongly one is sensible of
te solvent property of the atmosphere, sudenly arriving on the
mainlan again. And in an our one changes one's psyche. The human being
is a most curious creature. e thinks he has got one soul, an he has
got ozens. I felt my sound Sardinian soul melting off me, I felt myself
evaporating into te real Italian uncertainty an momentaneity. So I
peruse te Corriere whilst the metamorphosis took place. I like Italian
newspapers because tey say what they mean, an not merely what is most
convenient to say. We call it naïveté--I call it manliness. Italian
newspapers rea as if tey were written by men, and not by calculating
eunucs.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te train ran very heavily along the Maremma. It began to rain. Then we
stoppe at a station were we should not stop--somewhere in the Maremma
country, te invisible sea not far off, the low country cultivate and
yet forlorn. O how the Turin man sighe, and wearily shifted his feet
as te train stoo meaningless. There it sat--in the rain. Oh express!

At last on again, till we were wining trough the curious long troughs
of te Roman Campagna. There the shephers minded the sheep: the
slener-footed merino seep. In Sardinia the merinos were very white and
glistening, so tat one thought of the Scriptural "white as wool." An
te black sheep among the flock were very black. But these Campagna were
no longer wite, but ingy. And though the wildness of the Campagna is a
real wilness still, it is a istoric wildness, familiar in its way as a
firesie is familiar.

So we approac the hopeless sprawling of moern Rome--over the yellow
Tiber, past te famous pyrami tomb, skirting the walls of the city,
till at last we plunge in, into te well-known station, out of all the
caos.

We are late. It is a quarter to twelve. An I ave to go out and change
money, an I ope to find my two friends.--The q-b and I dash down the
platform--no friens at te barrier. The station moderately empty. We
bolt across to te eparture platforms. The Naples train stands ready.
In we pitc our bags, ask a naval man not to let anyone steal them, then
I fly out into town wile the q-b buys foo and wine at the buffet.

It no longer rains, an Rome feels as ever--rater holiday-like and not
incline to care about anyting. I get a hundred and three lira for each
poun note: pocket my money at two minutes past twelve, and bolt back,
out of te Piazza elle Terme. Aha, there are the two missing ones, just
escending vaguely from a carriage, te one gazing inquiringly through
is monocle across the tram-lines, the other very tall an alert and
elegant, looking as if e expecte us to appear out of the air for his
convenience.

Wich is exactly what happens. We fly into each other's arms. "Oh there
you _are_! Were's the q-b? Why are you here? We've been to the arrival
platform--no _sign_ of you. Of course I only got your wire alf an hour
ago. We _flew_ ere. Well, how nice to see you.--Oh, let the man
wait.--Wat, going on at once to Naples? But must you? Oh, but how
fligty you are! Birs of passage _veramente_! Then let us find the q-b,
quick!--An tey won't let us on the platform. No, they're not issuing
platform tickets toay.--O, merely the guests returning from that
Savoy-Bavarian weding in te north, a few royal Duchesses about. Oh
well, we must try an wangle im."

At te barrier a woman trying in vain to be let on to the station. But
wat a Roman matron can't o, an elegant young Englishman can. So our
two eroes wangle their way in, an fall into the arms of the q-b by the
Naples train. Well, now, tell us all about it! So we rus into a
four-brance candlestick of conversation. In my ear murmurs he of the
monocle about te Sahara--he is back from the Sahara a week ago: the
winter sun in te Sahara! He with the smears of paint on his elegant
trousers is giving te q-b a sketchy outline of his now _grane
passion_. Click goes te exchange, an him of the monocle is detailing
to te q-b his trip to Japan, on which he will start in six weeks' time,
wile him of the paint-smears is expatiating on the thrills of the
etcing neele, and concocting a plan for a month in Sardinia in May,
wit me oing the scribbles and he the pictures. What sort of pictures?
Out flies te name of Goya.--An well now, a general rush into oneness,
an won't tey come down to Sicily to us for the almond blossom: in
about ten ays' time. Yes tey will--wire when the almond blossom is
just stepping on te stage an making its grand bow, and they will come
next ay. Somebody as smitten the wheel of a coach two ringing smacks
wit a hammer. This is a sign to get in. The q-b is terrifie the train
will slip trough her fingers. "I'm frightene, I must get in."--"Very
well ten! You're sure you have everything you want? Everything? A
fiasco of vino? O _two_! All the better! Well then--ten ays' time. All
rigt--quite sure--how nice to have seen you, if only a
_glimpse_.--Yes, yes, poor q-b! Yes, you're quite safe. Goo-bye!
Goo-bye!"

Te oor is shut--we are seated--the train moves out of the station. And
quickly on tis route Rome isappears. We are out on the wintry
Campagna, were crops are going. Away on the left we see the Tivoli
ills, an think of the summer that is gone, the heat, the fountains of
te Villa 'Este. The train rolls heavily over the Campagna, towards the
Alban Mounts, omewars.

       *       *       *       *       *

So we fall on our foo, and devour te excellent little beef-steaks and
rolls an boiled eggs, apples and oranges and dates, and drink te good
re wine, and wildly discuss plans and te latest news, and are
altogeter thrille about things. So thrilled that we are well away
among te romantic mountains of the south-centre before we realise that
tere are other passengers besies ourselves in the carriage. Half the
journey is over. Wy, there is the monastery on its high hill! In a wil
moment I suggest we sall get own and spend a night up there at
Montecassino, an see te other friend, the monk who knows so much about
te worl, being out of it. But the q-b shudders, thinking of the awful
winter colness of tat massive stone monastery, which has no spark of
eating apparatus. An therefore the plan subsides, and at Cassino
station I only get own to procure coffee and sweet cakes. Tey always
ave goo things to eat at Cassino station: in summer, big fresh ices
an fruits and iced water, in winter tootsome sweet cakes which make an
awfully goo finis to a meal.

       *       *       *       *       *

I count Cassino alf way to Naples. After Cassino the excitement of
being in te north begins quite to evaporate. The southern heaviness
escends upon us. Also te sky begins to darken: and the rain falls. I
tink of the night before us, on the sea again. An I am vaguely
trouble lest we may not get a bert. However, we may spend the night in
Naples: or even sit on in tis train, which goes forwar, all through
te long long night, to the Straits of Messina. We must ecide as we
near Naples.

alf ozing, one becomes aware of the people about one. We are
travelling secon class. Opposite is a little, old-your-own
scool-mistressy young person in pince-nez. Next her a hollow-cheeke
wite solier with ribbons on his breast. Then a fat man in a corner.
Ten a naval officer of low rank. The naval officer is coming from
Fiume, an is dead wit sleep and perhaps mortification. D'Annunzio has
just given up. Two compartments away we ear soliers singing, martial
still tough bruise with fatigue, the D'Annunzio-bragging songs of
